# Constitutionalism

History of Constitutionalism traced by **Patterson** “_The Evolution of Constitutionalism_”, which places the doctrine of checks and balances squarely with the Ancient Greeks. Perhaps the most well-known example of a written Constitution stems from the US Constitution, the doctrine of limited government, the idea that a higher law controls governmental action. Most constitutional republics tends to derive its ultimate political mandate from the polity – the electorate. The will of the people is the source of authority and the basis of legitimate government (**Henkin**, “_A New Birth of Constitutionalism_”). In it, he sets out the principal characteristics of constitutionalism, amongst them: supreme law, separation of powers and checks and balances, and preservation and protection of individual rights.

### Liberal vs non-liberal constitutionalism

*   **Walker**, “_The Idea of Nonliberal Constitutionalism_”: term “constitutionalism” has become synonymous with the rights-oriented model of liberal constitutionalism which prioritises individual autonomy and a “neutral” state. But author suggests that it is possible to conceptualise a constitutionalism not defined by liberal values while retaining the appealing qualities of constitutionalist government
    *   Non-liberal values are typically more local than universalistic in character, except insofar as they are rooted in religions with universal pretensions, and views the individual not as the source of value but as a participant in a given system of meaning to which allegiance is naturally owed

### Singapore styled constitutionalism?

*   **Beer**, “_Constitutionalism in Asia_”: All Asian legal systems are of relatively recent origin, given the legal impact of the Western world on Asia. But Western idealism like freedom of expression, property rights, separation of religion and state are less attractive to the Asian states
*   **Ginsburg**, “_Confucian Constitutionalism_”: views power as indivisible, as the Emperor possesses the mandate of heaven, and no human force can check his power
    *   Limits to Emperor’s power is found in the concept of _li_
    *   Law is not a device to constrain the government but primarily an instrument of state power
*   Deference to the State in the idea of “leadership by gentlemen” (**Shared Values White Paper (Cmd 1 of 1991)**) – notes also 5 values that forms the basis of developing shared values
    *   Nation before community and society before self
    *   Family as the basic unity of society
    *   Regard and community support for the individual
    *   Consensus instead of contention
    *   Racial and religious harmony
*   **Lee Hsien Loong**, _Speech at 6th Asian-European Editors’ Forum_ (6 Oct 2006)
    *   Good governance is an essential prerequisite for Asia’s economic success, as the government must have legitimacy, enjoy the people’s trust, and engage the energies and talents of the people to build the nation
    *   Western model cannot be simply transplanted into Asian states as challenges faced by each nation is dynamic, and may not match its national ethos
*   **Silverstein**, “_Singapore: A Model, but of what sort_?”: suggests that Singapore’s model might be exceptional, a _sui generis_ model. Singapore is not simply an authoritarian state
*   **Kevin Tan**, “_Is Singapore an Authoritarian Constitutional Regime_?”: criticises Professor Tushnet’s typology of constitutionalism that juxtaposes two criteria – varying degrees of “liberal freedoms” and “levels of force and fraud in elections”
    *   Criticises Tushnet’s idea that liberal-democratic constitutions sit atop the normative constitutional order
    *   Argues that merely calling Singapore an authoritarian constitutionalist state is just a label, a way to say it is not a liberal democracy. It does not help to explain Singapore’s practices

Classic definition of constitutionalism found in **Montesquieu**, _The Spirit of the Laws_

*   Power separated in any government, so that one man need not be afraid of another
    *   If legislative and executive powers are united in the same person, or same body, there can be no liberty because of apprehension of tyranny
*   A similar idea can be found in **Madison**, _The Federalist No 51_
    *   Government departments should have as little dependence as possible
    *   Every act of a delegated authority, contrary to the tenor of the commission under which it is exercised, is void. No legislative act therefore contrary to the constitution can be valid. To deny this would be to affirm that the deputy is greater than his principal; that the servant is above his master; that the representatives of the people are superior to the people themselves; that men acting by virtue of powers may do only what their powers do not authorise, but what they forbid
*   In **_Vellama_**, the court affirmed the principle of separation of powers, and that it was a basic proposition of the rule of law that all discretionary power is subject to legal limits; yet court cautioned that judicial intervention in such fact-sensitive discretion was warranted only in exceptional cases

## Constitutional Supremacy

| Supremacy of Constitution4.  This Constitution is the supreme law of the Republic of Singapore and any law enacted by the Legislature after the commencement of this Constitution which is inconsistent with this Constitution shall, to the extent of the inconsistency, be void. | Supremacy of Constitution | 4.  This Constitution is the supreme law of the Republic of Singapore and any law enacted by the Legislature after the commencement of this Constitution which is inconsistent with this Constitution shall, to the extent of the inconsistency, be void. |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Supremacy of Constitution |
| 4.  This Constitution is the supreme law of the Republic of Singapore and any law enacted by the Legislature after the commencement of this Constitution which is inconsistent with this Constitution shall, to the extent of the inconsistency, be void. |

The Rule of Law as explored by **Dicey**, _An Introduction to the Study of the Law of the Constitution_ gives 3 meanings to the rule of law

*   Absolute supremacy or predominance of regular law as opposed to influence of arbitrary power
*   Equality before the law ensures all persons are subject to the law
*   The Constitution is the result of the ordinary law of the land

In **Thio**, _Lex Rex or Rex Lex_, the author explored two conceptions of Rule of Law:

*   Rule of Law as extending beyond the judicial process towards ensuring all branches act under the law
*   Rule of Law as the supremacy of law as opposed to the arbitrary exercise of power, and everyone is equal before the law

In _The Rule of Law in Singapore_ by **K Shanmugam**:

*   Ultimate aim for Singapore is a legal system that is uncorrupt, where the law is above everyone no matter the seniority of office
*   Specifically addressing criticisms of infringements to due process, Shanmugam notes that it is clearly an exception to due process with potential risk of abuse – thus for art 149 legislation, the government has been careful to build in safeguards in the ISA
*   Difference in approach depends on what society wishes to tolerate – in Singapore, there is a necessity for the statute, but a recognition that risks of abuse must also be minimised
    *   If there are exceptions to the Rule of Law, they must be closely scrutinised and strictly justified

In **_Tan Seet Eng_**, the court cited Lord Diplock “_Judicial Control of Government_” and emphasised that the judiciary’s responsibility is to declare and refuse to give legal effect to any executive or administrative act that fails to observe or applies the law _ie_ is _ultra vires_. The court also noted that for issues of national security, the courts have traditionally accorded defence to Executive’s determination, thus courts undertake a less intense standard of review.

*   Importantly, courts are careful not to read unenumerated rights into the Constitution as it would entail judges sitting as a super-legislature and enacting their personal views of what is just and desirable into law, which is not only undemocratic but antithetical to the rule of law (**_Yong Vui Kong_** \[2015\] (Caning))

**Limbach**, “_The Concept of the Supremacy of the Constitution_”

*   Parliament can do injustice, which led to fundamental rethinking of problem of the limits to State power – Constitution is used to ensure that unconstitutional acts can be conceivable (that Parliament could be checked)

Articles 4 and 5 of the Constitution guarantees and secures the prevalence of a person’s fundamental liberties over legislative and executive action which contravenes those rights (**_Taw Cheng Kong_** (HC)). It is the duty of the court to uphold and preserve those rights, and to impugn any Act of Parliament or any course of executive action which injures, detracts from or infringes those rights

To argue that Singapore has de facto Parliamentary supremacy, can start from _Lo Pui Sang_, where Choo J said: “in accordance with law” must mean law passed by Parliament – deference to Parliament. But perhaps, recent jurisprudence reflects a changing paradigm, for example in context of article 12 (equal protection), the CA in _Lim Meng Suang_ adopted a limited “illegitimacy” in impugning statutes by Parliament, which may reflect that not every law passed by Parliament is constitutional

### Basic Features Doctrine

*   Doctrine was first established in **_Kesavananda_**
*   In **_Mohammad Faizal_,** the court held that all constitutions based on the Westminster model incorporated the principle of separation of powers as part of their constitutional structure in order to diffuse state power among different organs of state
    *   In Singapore, the Constitution is supreme and this allows courts in SG to declare an Act of the Singapore Parliament invalid for inconsistency with Constitution
    *   Use of “basic structure” – semantics or invoking the BFD?
*   In _Yong Vui Kong_ (2015) (Caning), BFD was considered, Court in _dicta_ noted that for a feature to be considered as part of the basic structure, it must be something fundamental and essential to the political system that is established there under
    *   No conclusion as to whether the doctrine is accepted in Singapore
*   In **_Wong Souk Yee_**, the court once again in _dicta_ considered that even if the doctrine applies in Singapore, it was unclear what rights or concepts will be “fundamental and essential” to the Westminster model of government such that it cannot be departed from

# Constitutional Interpretation

Which body of government has and should have the primary role to protect fundamental liberties in Singapore?

*   In **_Rajeevan_**, the court considered that a judge’s duty is to adjudicate and interpret the laws passed with the aim of ensuring justice is upheld, and has no authority to expand the scope of or imply into the Constitution and other legislation his own interpretation of the provisions which is clearly contrary to Parliament’s intention
    *   Thus, the sensitive issues surrounding the scope of fundamental liberties should be raised through representatives in Parliament who are chosen by the people to address these concerns
    *   Strict textualist approach toward fundamental liberties
*   As noted in **Tan & Thio 4** at 11.090-94:
    *   Constitution has empowered the judiciary to decide any disputes and give effective remedies in cases of infringements of fundamental liberties lest the protection of rights becomes illusory
    *   Rights under Westminster Constitutions cannot be exhaustive, the drafting process must have left much to necessary implication

## How should the Constitution be interpreted?

*   In **_Colin Chan v PP_**, the HC considered the _sui generis_ approach to constitutional interpretation as laid down in **_Taw Cheng Kong_** (HC). The HC affirmed that in determining whether or not the scope of the constitutional provision is exceeded, the court is not limited by the normal rules and maxims applied to the interpretation of ordinary legislation
    *   A constitutional instrument … calls for principles of interpretation of its own, suitable to its character as already described, without necessary acceptance of all the presumptions that are relevant to legislation of private law
*   In **_Ong Ah Chuan_**, in the context of Part IV of the Constitution, the PC recommended giving this a generous interpretation to avoid the “austerity of tabulated legalism”, suitable to give to individuals the full measure of the fundamental liberties referred to
*   **TT4** at 10.042: VK Rajah in “Interpreting the Constitution”, as SG Constitution shares many basic ideals with other written Constitutions, helpful to consider how foreign courts interpret their constitutions. Though, the “four walls” approach considers that the Constitution must primarily be interpreted within its own four walls
*   In **_Chee Siok Chin_**, the HC did not accept the developments in UK where the doctrine of proportionality had been accepted into the jurisprudence by reason of the ECHR. Such a doctrine had never been part of the common law in relation to judicial review of the exercise of a legislative and / or an administrative power or discretion, nor part of Singapore law
*   Similarly, in **_Lim Meng Suang_**, the CA held that foreign cases had no persuasive weight in constitutional interpretations given that Singapore’s context must be considered for interpreting of what is suitable for Singapore
*   As for extrajudicial material, the court in **_BOM v BOK_** held that such pronouncements are obviously not binding on the courts – personal opinions even of a Supreme Court judge are only one element in a complex process of decision-making, and not necessarily the most important one
    *   Thus, in the context of constitutional interpretation, the logical extrapolation must be that such material is not binding on the courts
*   As for extralegal considerations, the court in _Lim Meng Suang_ emphasises that these are uniquely within the purview of the Legislature, and the court cannot and must not attempt to operate as a ‘mini-legislature’, lest the vital role of the court as an independent and neutral institution deciding objectively be reduced to naught
    *   Similarly, social attitudes are questions with answers best addressed by Parliament (_Yong Vui Kong_ \[2010\] (MDP))
*   See also _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\] for principles of constitutional interpretation of article 9 – that natural justice is not about residual substantive content of constitutional rights, but rather was the reasoning that implied the necessity of sacrificing natural liberty to government in a written constitution
*   The CA in **_Tan Cheng Bock_** affirmed that it is common ground that the Constitution should be interpreted purposively, which follows from the fact that Art 2(9) of the Constitution provides that the Interpretation Act applies in the Interpretation of the Constitution, which mandates the purposive approach
    *   In the context of interpretation, the distinction between the specific purpose of a provision and the general purpose is significant. How should the relevant purposes be discerned?
    *   Can consider the text of the relevant legislative provision itself and its statutory context, and extraneous material
    *   To interpret a provision of the Constitution, the court must start by ascertaining the possible interpretations of said provisions, having regard not just to its texts but also to its context within the Constitution as a whole. Then, the court must ascertain the legislative purpose or object of the specific provision and the part of the Constitution in which the provision is situated
*   In **Thio** (2009) 12 SAcLJ 326, the author discusses the “responsibilities discourse”, the idea is that there are different types of responsibilities – either owed to or by the state, or responsibilities owed to another human being
    *   Duties owed to the state are limits upon rights, as these rights entail responsibilities – in the context of _Chee Siok Chin_, the court held that free speech is neither impaired nor impeded by ruling out threatening, abusive and insulting speech
    *   Duties owed to other people include the duty not to impinge on the rights of others
*   What about constitutional or societal values? See the **Shared Values White Paper 1991**. In **_Vellama_**, the idea that our Constitution was a Westminster one was considered, regarding the timing of calling a by-election
    *   But where amendments have occurred, especially regarding GRCs, these factor into the nature of calling by-elections

## What about international law customs?

*   In **_Chan Kin Foo v City Development_**, the applicant invoked the UDHR to argue that the LTSA violated international law
    *   Court held that our Constitution does not protect a right to property, and thus the applicant had to point to some reason why Art 17 UDHR (which guarantees rights to property) should form part of local law
    *   Court observed that UDHR is not binding; for it to have legal effect in Singapore, it must form part of customary international law. Given that the widespread state practice is to allow collective sales, asserting that Art 17 of UDHR is CIL is wholly untenable and cannot support a valid cause of action
    *   Additionally, even if right to property were CIL, it would not be self-executing, and must be incorporated into Singapore law. Per _Yong Vui Kong_ (2010), a right that has been explicitly rejected by Parliament cannot now be part of the Constitution without explicit legislation to the effect
*   See also _Nguyen Tuong Van_, where court considered that the MDP was not contrary to the prohibition in CIL against cruel and inhuman treatment or punishment

## What are rights?

*   **Thio**, “_Two Conception of Rights_” in _A Treatise on Singapore Constitutional Law_ (Academy Publishing, 2012) at pp 604-608
    *   Rights as trumps – they are political trumps that protects against a collective goal, to prevent the collective desire to deny them or justify imposing some loss or injury
        *   Such a conception can be viewed as antisocial and tends to produce self-centred individuals blind to civic duties
    *   Rights as restraints – a judicial tool to maintain “appropriate structural relationships of authority”, and help determine the boundaries of what constitute legitimate reasons for constraining state action
        *   This is in lieu of the approach that seeks to balance the strength of individual rights against the strength of collective interests. Rights are not viewed as imposing on the state a general duty not to interfere with individual rights, but ‘a duty not to interfere for certain reasons’
        *   Rights aren’t simply just to protect interests of individual-holders, but also secure the interests of others, particularly through the construction of a political culture of a certain character
*   **Thio**, “_Written Representation to the Select Committee on Deliberate Online Falsehoods_” Parliament of Singapore (27 February 2018) at para 7.1
    *   false to characterise the balancing process as always involving an individual’s right to free speech against a state-defined and defended public interest. Process could be far more complex
*   types of constitutional rights identified by the CA in **_Review Publishing_**:
    *   in the context of freedom of speech vs protection of reputation, there are two main approaches:
        *   “preferential right” approach, under which preference is given to freedom of speech over protection of reputation (if reasonable) under _Reynolds_
        *   “fundamental right” approach, under which freedom of speech is given fundamental protection that trumps protection of reputation (except where malice is proved) under _NYT_
    *   A third “co-equal” approach appears to exist where freedom of speech may be preferred, neither taking precedence over the other. One may trump the other in a particular case if such an outcome is justified under the “ultimate balancing test”
*   Common law vs constitutional rights (_Yong Vui Kong_ (2015) (Caning))
    *   In the context of use of common law prohibition of torture, it was only recognised to be prohibited in the context of torturing suspects to extract evidence and confessions, and did not cover the treatment of criminals after they were found guilty of their crimes
    *   Constitutional rights are found in the Constitution, and where there have been proposed provisions that were explicitly rejected, they cannot thereafter become part of the Constitution without explicit amendment
        *   Where a right cannot be found (whether expressly or by necessary implication), the courts do not have the power to create such a right … reading unenumerated rights would entail judges sitting as a super-legislature and enacting their personal views of what is just and desirable into law
        *   Perhaps, implication by necessity is about ensuring that certain functions can be carried out, without which is simply impossible or irrational
*   Can rights be implied into the constitution?
    *   In **_Hinds v The Queen_**, Lord Diplock suggested that drafting process left much to necessary implication
    *   In Singapore, even though there is no express declaration of the right to vote, Parliament has recognised that the right to vote is implied within the structure of the Constitution, fundamental to a representative democracy and Acts must give effect to this right (Wong Kan Seng, “_Is Voting a Privilege or a Right_” _Singapore Parliamentary Debates_, _Official Report_ (16 May 2001), vol 73
    *   See _Yong Vui Kong_ directly above; see also _Rajeevan_

Does the approach in religion cases give effect to the _Ong Ah Chuan_ recommendation that Part IV fundamental rights be given a more generous interpretation?

*   The HC in **_Napalli_** suggested that in the context of the interest of the State in the education system and religious beliefs of the plaintiff, there had to be a careful balance
*   Similarly in **_Colin Chan_**, the court held that the right of freedom of religion must be reconciled with the right of the state to employ the sovereign power to ensure peace, security and orderly living
    *   Court also held that sovereignty, integrity and unity of Singapore are undoubtedly the paramount mandate of the Constitution and anything, including religious beliefs and practices, which tend to run counter to these objectives must be restrained

## Proportionality Argument by Marcus Teo

In **Marcus Teo** “_A Case for Proportionality Review in Singaporean Constitutional Adjudication_” \[2021\] SJLS 174, the author argues that proportionality can be accepted into Singapore jurisprudence, on 3 arguments — (1) First, as a matter of precedent, proportionality's four enquiries are already latent in Singapore's constitutional jurisprudence. (2) Second, as a matter of principle, Singapore's courts have the constitutional authority to adopt proportionality as a ground of constitutional review and are not institutionally incompetent to answer its enquiries. (3) Third, on grounds of policy, proportionality is desirable because it helps ensure the cogency and rationality of legislative or executive acts within Singapore's burgeoning political culture of justification

### Precedent

*   Rejecting proportionality appears to be instinctual, but it is clearly an unsatisfactory position. Proportionality review is a method of reasoning which courts can use to enforce constitutional rights, as it aligns with existing questions that are being asked
    *   (1) whether the impugned legislative or executive act purports to further a proper purpose, one which the Constitution itself recognises as legitimate
    *   (2) whether the impugned legislative or executive act is rationally related to, or suitable to achieve, its stated purpose, _ie_ would the means chosen go some way to achieving that purpose?
    *   (3) whether the legislative or executive act is necessary, in the sense of it being the least restrictive means, to achieve the stated purpose _ie_ whether there is an alternative means the government can use to achieve the same purpose which would be equally effective while infringing the claimed right less
    *   (4) if all three questions are answered with ‘yes’, then courts will carry out “balancing” in the strict sense, between the marginal addition of the act and the realisation of the stated purpose against the marginal injury incurred by infringement of the right – a normative and empirical sub-enquiry
*   Proportionality review, in contrast with the categorical approach based on _GCHQ_ grounds, involves fact-specific teleological reasoning: it views constitutional rights and countervailing purposes as constitutional principles, which courts must balance on the particular facts of a case while keeping in mind the consequences that might eventuate
*   One precedent-based argument in favour of proportionality is the idea that proportionality does not belong to the common law
    *   But commentators like Paul Craig have disagreed with such claims as ahistorical in the context of UK, as early as the 16th century. The point is that proportionality review is not alien to English law
    *   In Singapore, Jack Lee has argued that case law on fundamental rights in Singapore employs certain concepts and reasoning structures that are analogous to proportionality's four enquiries per above
    *   After drawing the mirror, the author argues that all four of proportionality’s enquiries already exist in Singapore’s constitutional jurisprudence, and is not some completely alien concept to Singapore law

### Principle

*   Would proportionality contravene the separation of powers? Author argues that it is justifiable to use proportionality review in constitutional adjudication — 2 levels based on 2 understandings of the separation of powers:
    *   Separation of powers in a categorical sense where Singapore’s court are derived of constitutional authority to develop an intrusive test for the enforcement of constitutional rights, and so courts cannot use proportionality review
    *   Separation of powers in a functional sense where courts can develop tests as needed but should be cognisant of limited institutional competence, which discourages them from using proportionality
*   As for constitutional authority, author submits that categorizing the powers into the three branches do not reveal that the constitutional authority to develop and adopt proportionality review is barred
    *   As noted by Jaclyn Neo in (2018) 5 J Intl & Compartive L 461, there are areas of overlap that can be said to fall within the functions of different branches of government
        *   When the courts assert that proportionality comes close to exercises of legislative or executive power, such as in **_Colin Chan_** **_v MITA_**, the court rejected proportionality’s use in an Art 15 challenge because it would involve a usurpation of power and responsibility that rightly belongs to the Minister
        *   But as Neo notes, the test can easily fall into more than one constitutional competence
    *   The only way to operationalise categorical understanding is to sidestep the impossible task of untangling overlapping competences by simply defining the scope of the "judicial power" as whatever the provisions of Part IV of the Constitution, properly interpreted, allow the judiciary to do
        *   As noted in _Taw Cheng Kong_ (HC), it is not within the scope of the powers of the legislative / executive branches to act in a manner prohibited by fundamental rights provisions
        *   Per _Tan Seet Eng_, because the Constitution gives the judiciary the exclusive power to interpret and apply law and because the Constitution is itself law, only the judiciary can interpret and apply the provisions of the Constitution
        *   Together, these 2 propositions state that the Constitution expressly limits the constitutional authority of the legislative and executive branches in accordance with the fundamental rights provisions in Part IV and vests the judiciary with the constitutional authority to interpret and apply those provisions
    *   Thus, the only question at this point is really whether a proper interpretation of the provisions in Part IV of the Constitution would allow the adoption of a test like proportionality
        *   As the text in most of these fundamental rights provisions do not preclude the adoption of proportionality review, the formulation of the appropriate test is left entirely to the courts
*   As for institutional competence, there are 4 arguments and 4 answers:
    *   Democratic credentials: Thus, when courts address proportionality's normative enquiries through constitutional interpretation according to the text and legislative intent behind constitutional provisions (when provisions have clear text or legislative intent) or through use of value-judgments (when provisions contain broadly-worded value-laden terms), courts act within their competence despite their lack of democratic credentials, just as they would when engaging in ordinary statutory interpretation
    *   Subject-matter expertise: important to differentiate legislation and regulations on one hand, and isolated administrative decisions on the other, as they raise different quantities of empirical uncertainty
        *   Court’s lack of subject-matter expertise in matters of socio-economic policy would appear more objectionable when they apply proportionality empirical enquiries to legislation or executive regulations, as law has immediate macro effect on socio-economic policy
        *   But when it comes to complex ordinary litigation, courts are not confined only to relying on their own subject-matter expertise – no reason why courts cannot make up for their lack of subject-matter expertise as they do in ordinary non-constitutional litigation
    *   Fact-finding capabilities: courts in common law jurisdiction always lack fact-finding capabilities across ordinary non-constitutional adjudication, but nevertheless consider themselves competent to be triers of fact
        *   In this regard, a distinction must be drawn between the court's fact-finding capabilities and its competence as a trier of fact. To say that courts lack the latter competence simply because they lack the former capabilities is plainly ridiculous: if that were so, the police force, the organisation equipped with fact-finding capabilities for criminal investigations, should also discharge the role of trier of fact in criminal trials instead of the court
        *   As to whether the evidence necessary to satisfy proportionality’s empirical enquiries can be retrieved, accessed or admitted, the ordinary rules of evidence and civil procedure can deal with this
    *   Policy-reforming capabilities: accepting that constitutional review amounts to policy reform requires a certain value-judgment about the role of the Constitution in Singapore’s political system — this implies that the Government rather than the Constitution should set the policy agenda, such that enforcing the Constitution against that policy agenda is seen as an attempt to "reform" it rather than merely keep it on track.
        *   Author in turn notes that proportionality review operates very fact-specifically, it will generally generate narrowly tailored constitutional rules and rarely generate broad constitutional rules. Proportionality only generates broad constitutional rules when a legislative or executive act is declared unconstitutional at the proper purposes or rational relation stages, but empirical evidence from comparative constitutional studies has shown that such findings of unconstitutionality are uncommon
*   One argument that is raised is that courts, if getting cases ‘wrong’, will render negative effects more irreversible than with ordinary litigation — untrue in Singapore’s case, political argument that PAP can simply overturn judicial decisions relating to Constitution should they wish
    *   This is relating to Singapore’s context only, however

### Policy

*   SG culture emphasises trust in the government, prioritising the separation of powers-based paradigm of constitutional theory and therefore hostile to intrusive grounds of constitutional review like proportionality review
    *   But author suggests the exact opposite: modern political culture of justification, where legitimacy of legislative or executive act is assessed according to the cogency and rationality of reasons given for them, justifies proportionality
*   Two accounts of political culture
    *   Political legitimacy in terms of authority, which assumes the existence of distinct institutions for distinct spheres of public life, each best equipped to act in its sphere, and accountable for its actions within that sphere
        *   This provides normative support for a theory of constitutionalism and judicial review based on the separation of powers as defined by principles of constitutional authority and institutional competence, but provides little support for a theory of constitutionalism and judicial review that accommodates proportionality review
    *   Political legitimacy in terms of justifications, where a legislative or executive’s act’s political legitimacy depends on the cogency and rationality of the reasons given for it
        *   This does not require legislative or executive acts to be justified in line with any particular substantive political theory in the abstract, but rather reasons that adhere to a given political community’s localised notions of rationality and reasonableness
        *   This culture will prioritise proportionality review
*   Question is which account is more accurate to Singapore today?
    *   Author obviously argues that the shift to culture of justification is evident in both governmental and judicial discourse
        *   Modern PAP leaders have increasingly been justification-based, not simply acting as “austere technocrats” – no longer leading from the front but being more consultative and inclusive
        *   This shift means that leaders will depend significantly on justification-based notions of political legitimacy
    *   Shift in judicial can be seen from Chan (2010) “_From Angst to Empathy_”, where he set out a broad vision of constitutionalism and judicial review involving justification-based notions of good governance in which proportionality review was welcome in appropriate circumstances
        *   Doctrine was also considered in _SGB Starkstrom_, where court in _obiter_ suggested that the doctrine could be defensible if, instead of ordering the Government to give effect to substantive legitimate expectations, courts merely required the Government to "give reasons for its assessment" that the public interest outweighed the expectation in the circumstances.
        *   In _UKM v AG_, the HC (with Menon CJ as part of 3-person coram) felt compelled to develop a novel conception of the judiciary’s proper role and function” within Singapore’s constitutional order. In a subsequent extra-judicial speech “_Taming the Unruly Horse: the Treatment of Public Policy Arguments in the Courts_” (19 February 2019), Menon CJ emphasised a vision of "good governance" which required courts to further a "constitutional dialogue" that helps uphold rational and cogent decision-making in situations involving clashes between rights and public policies-in short, a culture of justification in constitutionalism-and opined that this could be achieved through an "analytical framework" which was, essentially, proportionality review
*   Author is also of the view that _Jolovan Wham_ introduced proportionality review under article 14, if at all, only for executive rather than legislative acts
    *   Author is not convinced that the court did actually employ proportionality review to assess the constitutionality of the legislation under Article 14, rather than a standard resembling that in _Wednesbury_, which also requires the weighing and balancing of competing values and interests
    *   Difference between proportionality and _Wednesbury_, then, lies in the identity of the balancer: "\[w\]hereas under \[proportionality\] it is for the court to determine objectively, for itself, whether a rights-interference is justifiable... courts do not have such a role under _Wednesbury_, where they are exercising a supervisory jurisdiction
    *   But the court in _dicta_ noted that the Commissioner could exercise his discretion, granting a permit with certain conditions attached – suggests a resemblance to proportionality’s necessity and strict balancing enquiries

# Art 14 – Freedom of Speech

| Freedom of speech, assembly and association14.—(1)  Subject to clauses (2) and (3) —(a)every citizen of Singapore has the right to freedom of speech and expression;(b)all citizens of Singapore have the right to assemble peaceably and without arms; and(c)all citizens of Singapore have the right to form associations.(2)  Parliament may by law impose —(a)on the rights conferred by clause (1)(a), such restrictions as it considers necessary or expedient in the interest of the security of Singapore or any part thereof, friendly relations with other countries, public order or morality and restrictions designed to protect the privileges of Parliament or to provide against contempt of court, defamation or incitement to any offence;(b)on the right conferred by clause (1)(b), such restrictions as it considers necessary or expedient in the interest of the security of Singapore or any part thereof or public order; and(c)on the right conferred by clause (1)(c), such restrictions as it considers necessary or expedient in the interest of the security of Singapore or any part thereof, public order or morality.(3)  Restrictions on the right to form associations conferred by clause (1)(c) may also be imposed by any law relating to labour or education. | Freedom of speech, assembly and association | 14.—(1)  Subject to clauses (2) and (3) —(a)every citizen of Singapore has the right to freedom of speech and expression;(b)all citizens of Singapore have the right to assemble peaceably and without arms;
