In Singapore, the right to free speech is **not an absolute right** – is it qualified by broader societal goals enunciated in Art 14(2)(a). This is especially so in the context of Singapore’s subscription to communitarian constitutionalism, where there is an emphasis on community good over individual rights, and society above self (_Shared Values White Paper_). As such, from time to time, for the common welfare and good, individual interests have to be **subordinated to wider community’s interests** (_Chee Siok Chin_).

*   The balance Singapore has opted for a **regulated model of free speech**, balancing democratic values with societal order. Laws like the POFMA (_Protection from Online Falsehoods and Manipulation Act_) and the _Sedition Act_ are often cited as constraints—but from the government’s perspective, they’re necessary buffers against harm.
*   **History**: Ethnic and religious sensitivities in Singapore makes unregulated speech potentially explosive. The risk of social fragmentation is not theoretical – SG’ history (e.g. 1964 race riots) underscores the stakes. Falsehoods and hate speech, if unchecked, erodes trust, polarize communities, and can undermine public institutions. The amplification effect of social media makes disinformation faster and harder to correct than ever before. The assumption that truth will “naturally prevail” is increasingly questionable in today’s algorithm-driven echo chambers.

## Why is freedom of speech important?

*   **Lee Hsien Loong**, “_Building a Civic Society_” (6 January 2004)
    *   SG values diversity, people debate issues with reason, passion and conviction, and not be passive bystanders in their own fate
    *   Civic participation by way of debates, focused on issues based on facts and logic, and criticism is good to improve ideas

### Philosophical justifications (_LHL v Roy Ngerng_)

*   *   Defendant argued that being made to pay a large sum of damages for defamatory speech made would have a detrimental effect to free speech in Singapore
    *   Philosophical justifications for the right of free speech: Truth and Democracy; Self-Fulfillment and Distrust in the Government.
*   **Truth**: Speech, both true and false, should be protected on the premise that the absolute truth will eventually emerge. This follows academic Mill’s 1859 paper against depriving society of the “opportunity of exchanging error for truth.
    *   **_However_**, the merits of this rationale, the “competition of the market”, was discussed by CA in _Review Publishing v LHL_ where the point was made that no benefit is derived where false statements of fact are freely disseminated, especially if only relying on the competition of the market to expose them.
    *   Furthermore, in **_Ting Choon Ming_** _and_ **_TOC v AG_**, the SGCA held, in considering how the swift spreads in falsehoods “in an unregulated internet sphere”, that the preservation of public order can be threatened, even undermining the trust in the Government.
    *   **Connectivity Fuels Disinformation**: algorithms and search engines often create “echo chambers” and “filter bubbles” which entrench people ideological silos (Tan, Teng “Fake News, Free Speech and Finding Constitutional Congruence” (2017)).
*   **Democracy**: the process of democracy requires the unfettered exchange of ideas between individuals, an exchange that would be constrained where these views and opinions are given under the threat of sanction. Essentially, this moulds public opinion, which in turn fuels representative government and acts as a form of check-and-balance on the government.
    *   *   *   Argument epitomised by Australian case of _Lange v Australian Broadcasting Corporation_ is that political speech is of a protected category
            *   Similar pronouncements from England indicate the similar premise that the argument from democracy justifies freedom of expression in _Reynolds_
            *   **_However_**, in Singapore, per _Review Publishing_, Lange and Reynolds were not considered insofar as to elevate freedom of speech as a fundamental right unless it can be shown evidentially that Singapore’s “political, social and cultural” value” warrants such a shift to enshrine it in Art 14. Too uncertain.
        *   **Ext Factor 1: Self-fulfillment**: the right to free expression forms an intrinsic part of the “human spirit" towards their individual personality (_Tan, Whither the Autochthonous Narrative of Freedom of Speech?"_). _Barendt in “Freedom of Speech” (1985)_ expresses it, people “will not be able to develop intellectually and spiritually unless they are free to formulate their beliefs and political attitudes through public discussion.”
            *   **_However_**, this perspective promotes of a strong or radical individualism engendering the “**corrupting asymmetry of rights without civic duties**” (_Weiler in “Individual and Rights” (2010)_).
            *   Following Singapore’s subscription to communitarian constitutionalism, where an emphasis is founded on society above self, little weight should be given little weight.
        *   **Ext Factor 2: Distrust of Government**: Arises from an “appreciation of the fallibility of political leaders” and a “distrust of the ability of the government to make determinations of truth and falsity” (_Barendt, Freedom of Speech_). This theory, as pronounced by _Fredrick Schauer_, is negative insofar as it highlights the evil of regulations rather than the good of free speech.
            *   **_However_**, this theory relies on the bare suspicion that the government will act in bad faith**.**
            *   Furthermore, Singapore’s heavy **emphasis of honesty and integrity in public discourse** would not warrant any form of distrust per SGCA’s finding in _Review Publishing_.

### Should _Reynolds_ apply? SGCA in _Review Publishing_ suggested can be given effect

If the _Reynolds_ rationale were to be applied in Singapore in the context of publication of matters of “public interest”, such that a new balance has to be struck between constitutional free speech and protection of reputation, the defendant will not necessarily be excused or immunised from liability.

*   The _Reynolds_ rationale can equally be given effect by holding the defendant liable for defamation but adjusting the quantum of damages payable, with the exact amount to be paid in each case being calibrated by the court in proportion to the degree of care which the defendant has taken (or failed to take) to ensuring that what he publishes is 'accurate and fit for publication' (per lord Bingham in _Jameel_ at \[32\]). Such an approach has the merit of deterring irresponsible journalism. There is no reason why a defendant who has published a defamatory statement should be allowed to get off scot-free for injuring the plaintiff's reputation simply because he has satisfied the 'responsible journalism' test.

Thus, in this case as the defendant acted out of malice, even if _Reynolds_ were applied in the manner as elucidated above, it could not serve as a defence to the defendant’s actions.

**_However_**, it is submitted that the adoption of _Reynolds_ Privilege would be **incompatible with Singapore’s** emphasis on honesty and public discourse. Through Singapore’s communitarian ideology or neo-Confucian ethos in community’s interest in social cohesion and stability (_Thio, “An I for an I”_), an extensive free speech privilege would deter honourable men, junzi, from seeking public positions of trust and responsibility (_SGCA JBJ_). _That said_, it is my opinion that the application of Reynolds reflect a preventive approach to deter these honourable men from adopting immoral or corrupt practices. Furthermore, there is an argument in ensuring parliamentary democracy. As academic _David Tan_ argues, where the Government accepts the obligation to be accountable to the electorate through regular elections, this accountability necessarily presupposes that citizens are able to freely communicate and exchange views on matters relating to good governance. Such communication must occur both horizontally among members of the electorate and vertically between citizens and their political representatives. In this way, freedom of speech facilitates informed electoral choice and meaningful political participation, which are essential to the proper functioning of a parliamentary democracy. Thus, Reynolds should be adopted in SGCA’s position in _Review Publishing_.

## No difference in “Speech” and “Expression” in Art 14

In **_Tan Seng Kee v AG_**, the Court of Appeal applied a purposive approach to determine that "speech" and "expression" in Article 14(1)(a) are indistinct. The ordinary meaning of

*   "**speech**" is any form of communication expressed in the form of words, whether spoken or written.
*   "**expression**" which can encompass any means of conveying meaning, opinions, beliefs or ideas, whether with or without the use of language.

The court noted that a literal reading of "expression" could encompass any act conveying meaning (including sexual intimacy, necrophilia, or bestiality), leading to a "**plainly absurd result**". By applying the noscitur a sociis principle (derived from context) and reviewing marginal notes from the WCJ Consti Commission Report, the court concluded that "expression" serves merely an ancillary function to qualify and describe the fundamental right of "speech" (words, spoken or written, that convey meaning). As such, expression was not mentioned as a free-standing right and was thus not meant to expand the scope of Art 14(1)(a).

*   **_However_**, this renders the term "expression" **obsolete**, as "speech" is clearly a form of expression. While the SGCA applied the principle of noscitur a sociis, the SGCA in _Tan Cheng Bock_ also held that Parliament "shuns tautology and does not legislate in vain". Furthermore, marginal notes only serve to provide a "brief indication of the content" of the relevant provisions (_Bennion on Stat Interp 7th ed_). It is not meant to provide the comprehensive summary of the provision it covers. Additionally, limiting what might count as speech or expression **removes the crux of Art 14 in addressing consequences** of such rights from an _a priori_ (earlier) position.

## Legal test for the constitutionality of the limitations in Art 14(2)

| Threshold TestThe Court of Appeal in Wham Kwok Han Jolovan v PP [2021] 1 SLR 476 set out a three-step framework for determining whether a legislation impermissibly derogates from Article 14:Whether legislation derogates from or restricts any of the rights conferred by Art 14;If yes, the court should determine whether Parliament considered the derogation or restriction to be “necessary or expedient” in the interests of one or more of the enumerated purposes set out in Article 14(2);Whether there is an objective nexus between the purpose of the legislation and one or more of the permitted grounds for derogation or restriction under Article 14(2).Note (Chee Siok Chin):“necessary or expedient” confers on Parliament an extremely wide discretionary power and remit that permits a multifarious and multifaceted approach towards achieving any of the purposes specified in Art 14(2) of the ConstitutionThe expression “public order” is synonymous with public peace, safety, and tranquility. Depends in each case on the degree of disturbance that may be caused to the life of the community. |
| --- |

### Standard for determining whether the restrictions are “necessary or expedient”

The phrase "necessary or expedient" under Article 14(2) confers upon Parliament an **extremely wide** discretionary power and a broad legislative remit.

Despite the presence of the supremacy clause in Art 4, in a consistent line of cases, the SGCA has interpreted Art 14 with **deference** to the "government's assessment of the needs of public order without requiring that the restrictions be informed by substantive standards of reasonableness, proportionality or necessity within a democratic society (_Thio "Singapore : Regulating Political Speech_). That essentially leaves Parliament with a carte Blache to dictate the boundaries of free speech (_Tan Eng Seng_).

*   Albeit the 2021 SGCA _Jolovan Wham_ clarified the test not wholly subjective, as that would render the constitutional right "purely symbolic and without any real force", a strict **proportionality test was rejected** (which inquire whether the restriction is the least restrictive means of achieving its objective). Instead, it is generally sufficient that the restriction is rationally connected to a broadly framed legislative purpose, which may be inferred from materials such as _Hansard, long title and legislative purpose_, to enact pre-emptive restrictions on speech, even in the absence of imminent harm. While I agree on the government’s lens in using such materials, there needs to be a form of constrain to balance the wide discretion on Parliament without the court acting like mini-legislature.

### Case Examples

*   In **_Chee Soon Juan_**, the HC emphasised that freedom of speech is not an absolute right, and broader societal concerns such as public peace and order had to be engaged in a balancing exercise with the enjoyment of this personal liberty.
    *   In this case, given the place being Istana grounds on Labour Day, it was a highly visible event with strong public participation (close to 5,300 people in the grounds on the day). Court held that it did not take a great stretch of imagination to conclude that a political rally on the grounds that day could have resulted in a threat to public order and safety.
    *   Without evidence, Chee could not support his contention that provisions of PEMA were applied in a discriminatory fashion. He also did not apply to the Minister to require reasons for the refusal of the grant of a licence under the Act
    *   As to whether the sentence was manifestly excessive, because he was a habitual offender and treated the legal system without regard, and had the sheer arrogance to purport to speak on behalf of the people of Singapore … remarkable impertinence, considering that he was not a member of Parliament, and clearly had no mandate to speak on behalf of the people of Singapore.
*   In **_Chee Siok Chin_**, the CPF protestors unlawful assembly case, the HC had to deal with the issue of whether the Act could restrict or curtail Art 14 rights as declared in the Constitution
    *   As to the tension between freedom and competing interests of the state, there is a delicate balancing exercise that involves several imponderables and factors. Consideration of such public policy is exceedingly complex and multifaceted as individual rights do not exist in a vacuum. Court is wary to permit unfettered individual rights in a value-neutral process, as it would be against the rule of law
    *   Thus, the right of assembly cannot be absolute and may be subordinated to public convenience and good order for the protection and the general welfare whenever it is ‘necessary and expedient’
        *   The framework of the Constitution deems it crucial and necessary to authorise the imposition of restrictions in the wider and larger interests of the community and country.
    *   Court also held that from time to time, for the common welfare and good, individual interests have to subordinated to the wider community’s interests. There is also nothing inherently wrong or unreasonable in requiring permission from the relevant authorities to be sought prior to the holding of a public meeting or assembly
*   In **_Jolovan Wham_**, applicant was charged under s 16(1)(a) of Public Order Act for organising and holding a public assembly without obtaining the permit for it that POA required.
    *   Issue: Is s16(1)(a) of POA a constitutionally valid derogation from Art 14(1)?
    *   **s 16(1)(a) POA** (legis): prohibits the holding of a public assembly in certain circumstances where no permit has been obtained or is in force. Subjects the exercise of this right to **criminal prosecution and punishment.**
    *   Objective framework used: Use of subjective approach would render the right under Article 14 illusory as the constitutional right would be purely symbolic and without any real force or effect.
    *   Court reiterated _Tan Seet Eng_ (which endorsed _Chng Suan Tze_’s objective approach in the context of CLTPA) that Minister’s exercise of discretion should be done on an **objective basis** (_ie_ whether the applicant’s detention / not approving the licence to speak was necessary on the basis that he was a threat to public peace, safety and good order)
        *   Sources: Court may have regard to the relevant legislation, parliamentary material and contemporary speeches as well as documents to determine whether Parliament had considered it “necessary and expedient” to restrict the constitutional right in question … court may infer from the general purposes for which Parliament passed the relevant legislation that it had considered it “necessary and expedient” to restrict the constitutional right in question.
        *   Purpose of the POA from **long title** and **Hansard** evinces that the bill was to maintain public order and would be the interest of society for Parliament to operate a permit scheme.
        *   In considering the nexus between the legislation and Art 14(2) grounds, the Commissioner had reasonable grounds for apprehending to prevent undesirable results from occurring, which pertains to public order and achieves a careful balance in allowing a right and imposing restrictions upon it.
    *   On the **administrative law** point, a speaking permit may be sought under the Act, which the Commissioner has discretion to grant. The Commissioner may refuse to grant the permit where he apprehends on reasonable grounds that one of the listed circumstances may occur
        *   The converse may too happen. Thus, in the context of this case, a foreigner was seeking a permit to speak at a public assembly, under which he has no guaranteed constitutional rights. In fact, the Commissioner may, if he believes that there is a risk to public order, still allow the public assembly to carry on but with certain conditions attached
        *   There are also prescribed guidelines for the discretion in the Public Order Act, which the Commissioner must exercise in accordance.
            *   Minister must similarly consider the merits of any appeal in accordance with the guidelines established by the POA and may, if the facts and circumstances warrant, either (a) reject the appeal and confirm the Commissioner’s decision; (b) allow the appeal in whole or in part and vary the Commissioner’s decision; (c) set aside the Commissioner’s decision and make a decision in substitution of it; or (d) direct the Commissioner to reconsider his decision (s 11(3) of the POA)
        *   In fact, the applicant did not even apply for the permit, and thus cannot now argue that the permit.
*   In **_Tan Seng Kee_**, the CA addressed a different question – whether s 377A violates art 14’s protections on freedom of expression and free speech
    *   the term “expression” in art 14(1)(a) protects the right to freedom of speech – that conveys meaning, opinions, beliefs, or ideas. Since acts of sexual intimacy are not “speech” to begin with, Art 14 constitutional challenge to s 377A is without merit
        *   extremely difficult to delimit the acts that are constitutionally protected under art 14(1)(a) if “expression” is read expansively, thus decided that the term should be interpreted as a qualifier of the right to freedom of speech, _ie_ words, whether spoken or written, that conveys meaning, opinions, beliefs or ideas
    *   gross indecency =/= expression

## Compelled Speech

In **_TOC 2021 SGCA_**, on POFMA CDs, TOC argued that a necessary corollary of the right to free speech is the negative right to not be required to speak, relying on jurisprudence of the US in _Becerra_. Otherwise, compelled speech can distort influence on the marketplace of ideas by diluting the persuasiveness of speakers coming from the compelled speaker’s “forced utterance of the diametrically opposed position” (_Redish, 2019_). **Held**: POFMA CD does not prevent the recipients from displaying their disagreements with the CDs, and hence it was not capable of being unconstitutionally compelled speech.

## Traditional and online media, and false speech

### How should free speech laws apply in media?

*   Guiding words on role of media (**_Lee Hsien Loong_** “Role of Media” Speech (6 October 2006))
    *   Good government and responsible journalism both ultimately exist for the people they serve. Media occupies a position of power and responsibility. As with the political system, each country will have to evolve its own model of the media that works for it.
    *   With the advent of the internet, the free flow of information on the internet is universally a good thing, though not an unmitigated boon. The internet short circuits and undercuts the model of media which is filtered, processed and verified
    *   There is a role for serious journalism because people will still seek out information sources that are reliable, verified and insightful. Singapore need anchor points that reflect our values, our vulnerabilities and our ambitions. The media in Singapore must adapt to these changes, do their best to stay relevant, and continue to contribute constructively to nation building
*   **Shanmugam**, “_A Free Press for a Global Society_” (4 November 2010)
    *   Modern media reality is about “impact reporting” to maximise readership rather than accurate reporting
        *   Thus, confusion of news and commentary, especially in US
        *   But the assumption is the “marketplace of ideas”, that society is strong enough to withstand the possible harmful consequences arising from such an approach
    *   In Singapore, the calculus is different, as the role that the media plays in US for example is too disruptive
        *   Media here is not a political actor, nor is it partisan
        *   Even though press freedoms appears bad, it doesn’t reflect that Singaporeans mostly trust their media which cannot be said for the US
    *   we stick to our own model because it works
    *   it also cannot play politics as it involves accountability and responsibility to the people. our political context **militates** **against** applying _Reynolds_

### What impact does the Internet have on free speech?

Online speech arguably requires more regulation and less protection when it involves falsehoods, given its amplified capacity to threaten public order. Because the largely unregulated internet sphere allows falsehoods to spread swiftly, these platforms portend abuse that can quickly undermine trust in key public institutions.

*   **Thio** \[2008\] SJLS 25 at 53-56
    *   Internet has a dark side, exacerbating the simply social fact of the tendency of individuals and confirmation bias, and may lead them to adopt more extreme views of original views, and heightens social fragmentation by insulating them from competing views
    *   Public debate is undermined by irrational speech
    *   To promote democratic debate, citizens must agree to disagree on morally contentious questions in deference to ideological diversity, and to use democratic channels to influence public opinion, through deliberation rather than hate-mongering and harassment
*   In **_LHL v Roy Ngerng_**, the SGHC held that it was trite law the wider the extent of publication, the greater the award of damages for defamation
    *   The court looked at number of factors to determine the extent and outreach of readership, and how the post was written and structured to assess its impact
    *   Additionally, the higher end of spectrum for damages is awarded in defamation because of the high standing that political leaders have in society

### Media outlets as defendants

**Are they treated differently?**

*   No, media defendants are not granted special privileges in Singapore. In _Review Publishing_, the court explicitly rejected the notion that the media serves as an activist "watchdog" that investigates every matter and sets its own political agenda. Citing government officials, the court maintained that the media's role is strictly limited to traditional activities of reporting the news factually and objectively. Therefore, the media is subject to the same strict defamation laws as any private individual.

**Should they be treated differently?**

*   There is a strong argument that they should be afforded protections like the _Reynolds_ privilege (responsible journalism). The media plays an essential role in disseminating information on matters of public interest. Denying them a qualified privilege defense—even when they take reasonable steps to verify information—forces the media into extreme self-censorship, depriving the electorate of information crucial to holding leaders accountable.

## False Speech

**TLDR**: In **_TOC v AG 2021_**, the SGCA held that a statement that has been identified by the Minister as a false statement of fact under **_Part 3 of POFMA_** still enjoys protection under Art 14(1)(a) of the Constitution until it is judicially determined to indeed be a false statement of fact.

The 2017 SGCA in **_Ting Choon Meng_** dealt with this issue:

s 15 POHA does not contemplate a take-down or any other remedy beyond the following:

1.  a **finding of falsehood** by the court on a balance of probabilities; and
2.  contingent on such finding, the **right** to have such notification as deemed necessary to **draw attention** to the falsehood and the true facts.

*   s 15 POHA does not inhibit or prevent free speech at all or even materially limit it…. A speaker may continue to publish that falsehood even if a **court of law** has found it to be false. What s 15 contemplates is that in that event, the court may require him to draw attention to the falsehood if the court is of the view that it is just and equitable to do so. s 15 thus does not restrict, but merely constrains the publication of speech that has been proven to be false without a notification that is has been so proven and / or without a direction to where the truth may be found.
*   A wholly unrestricted right to free speech **does not extend** to a wholly unrestricted right to deceive or maintain a deception by not drawing attention to the falsehood
    *   The underlying rationale for free speech in Singapore is the collective search for truth, falsehoods have zero constitutional value. They do not contribute to the marketplace of ideas; rather, they are "destructive of the democratic society" by eroding trust in public institutions.
*   _Even if_ the court was to hold false speech as protected, it would likely be restricted when it is necessary or expedient, in the interest of public order.

## Political defamation (against free speech)

### Lack of Public Figure Doctrine

The rationale is that a politician must be more tolerant of criticism because in choosing this career, he knowing lays himself open to scrutiny which **entails assuming the risks** of “occasional falsity that comes with vigorous discussions of issues” (Frederick Schauer, Free Speech, a Philosophical Enquiry). This doctrine was also respected in _Lingens v Austria_ in pursuant to the concept of democratic societies.

*   **_However_**, the SGCA in JBJ rejects this holding that public figures should be entitled to protect their reputation too. It is hence submitted that the balance struck by the defamation laws in Singapore lies closer to the protection of reputation rather than the protection of free speech.
*   **State v Private individual asymmetry**: Strangely, the Courts do not acknowledge the disparity in standing and access to resources between public officials (who have the backing of state machinery to clear their reputations) and critics who are often private individuals lacking the same access.
*   Indeed, in _Peter Lim_ it was expressly stated that the court will take as a “dominant consideration” the “position and standing of the plaintiff in society” in calculating damages for defamation, where the court acknowledges that public leaders are generally “entitled to higher damages” as damage to their reputation would not only damage them personally but also have ripple effects on the “reputation of Singapore. In the recent TOC case of Terry Xu, the defamation suit resulted in an exceedingly high $420k in damages.
*   With such an unduly high protection for public officials in defamation suits, a real risk of **oppressively “chilling” speech** can arise and may be used as a tool to silence critics, which could hinder public discourse (_JBJ Hansard 1998_). Thus, a recurrent criticism levied against Singapore is that its **government relies on “a ‘compliant’ judiciary to bankrupt opposition politicians”** through defamation suits (_Lingle, The Smoke Parts of Asia Obscures some Profound Concerns_).
*   It doesn’t help that then-Foreign Minister Yeo expounds on how the ordinary man must not presume to be able to address Singapore’s political class as “equals”, rejecting the idea of democratic equality in favour of an antiquated form of feudalism. While LHL leans the Government’s in its pledge to seek voices and criticisms that reflect “sincere contributions to improve Government policies”, it remains to be seen how a balance can be struck to promote free speech when reputational interests continue to prevail.

## Justifications for POFMA being Constitutional

### Falsehoods are not constitutionally protected speech.

As noted by **_Milo in “Defamation and Freedom of Speech” (2008)_** “history has taught us that falsehood frequently prevails over truth with ‘deleterious societal consequences”. In the modern context where digital speech is exercised, "falsehoods can be rapidly disseminated in an unregulated internet sphere" (**_Ting Choon Meng SGCA 2017_**), such that “it is entirely conceivable that their swift spread online could threaten the preservation of public order by (among other things) undermining trust in the Government and other key public institutions, even though disorder has yet to break out physically in a public space” (**_TOC v AG SGCA 2021_**). There is also “no benefit in permitting the free dissemination of false assertions of fact that destroy a person’s reputation” (**_Roy Ngerng_**).

It is clear then that “free speech, or the liberty to communicate, relates to the communication of ‘information not misinformation’”. Therefore, “false speech which has been proven as a matter of fact to be false in a court of law, can contribute little to the marketplace of ideas or to advances in knowledge for the benefit of society as a whole.

### Falsehoods can result in public order threats

One of the 8 grounds of derogation from free speech in Art 14(2)(a) is that of “public order” and this gives Parliament constitutional basis for legislation restricting the freedom of speech. \[Public Order read broadly\]. Though it might be argued that virtual falsehoods cause no actual public order disruptions (to “communal tranquillity” or the “even tempo of the life of the community”) (**_TOC v AG, SDP v AG_**) this argument holds no weight. This is because the derogation clauses in Art 14 are **phrased in a more open textured manner** (“in the interest of”, rather than “in the maintenance of”). “Interest” connotes that as long as there is a “nexus” (**_Chee Siok Chin_**) between the restriction and the public goal/outcome identified that the court will give effect to it, whereas “maintenance” entails something more than a nexus, a relationship that would ensure that the interest is actually upheld, and therefore may demand considerations of reasonableness or proportionality. In Chee Siok Chin the court noted that the wording “in the interest of … public order” should not be confined to “the maintenance of public order and therefore affords Parliament a “much wider legislative remit” to not only enact laws for the ‘immediate or direct maintenance of public order” but also extends to laws that strike **pre-emptively** (**_Tan, Teng “Fake news, Free Speech” (2020)_**), allowing the government to take a **prophylactic approach in the maintenance of public order**. Ostensibly POFMA would fall under the Government’s wide remit to legislate in the broad interests of safeguarding Singapore from falsehoods that “threaten the preservation of public order by (among other things) undermining trust in the Government and other key public institutions, even though disorder has yet to break out physically in a public space” (**_TOC v AG and SDP v AG at \[99\]_**). The low cost of the internet in facilitating political organisation and lobbying as well as the spread of information may allow small groups whether belonging to sensible or noxious groups to enjoy an asymmetrical amplification of their views by dint of volume not merit and result in public order concerns. Therefore POFMA’s regulation of online falsehoods is entirely within the ambit of the derogations allowed in Art 14(2)(a) and falls within the category of a pre-emptive regulation against actual physical disorder, preventing the escalation of online incitement to actual physical disorder in a public space.

### Media Companies are not incentivised to engage in self-regulation

Unique to POFMA the Select Committee on Deliberate Online Falsehoods justified legal intervention on two grounds.

1.  Indirect measures such as the raising of media literacy, promotion of quality journalism and implementing fact checking initiatives cannot overcome the cognitive biases of human psychology that makes us susceptible to fake news.
2.  That technology companies **do not have the incentive** to take active steps against the promulgation of online falsehoods if such regulation is merely voluntary.
3.  Media companies and technology companies not incentivised to take steps against the promulgation of online falsehoods if these regulations are merely opt-in, and companies are even less willing to disclose their proprietary algorithms and how they target individuals for better engagement, which if known could help states counter fake news (Jaipragas & Sim).
4.  For example, Facebook has repeatedly voiced rejection against fact checking the content of its political advertisements. Its algorithms sort users by their political dispositions, and feeds them data (including falsehoods) that suit their inclinations so as to enhance traffic user volume (Richter, “The case for government regulators in fighting disinformation”)

## Justifications for POFMA being Unconstitutional

### Loose Wording of Statute, hard to identify what constitutes a false statement = Chilling

\[Short\] It is submitted that the Minister is granted with **wide discretionary powers** under the vague wordings of POFMA, engendering a low threshold for the Minister to issue part 3 directions that leads to a **chilling effect** on the population -> undermines democracy.

*   As a starting point, what amounts to a false statement of fact for the purposes of issuing Part 3 Directions or for the offence of communicating a false statement of fact in s 7 is an extremely low threshold. In s 2(2)(b), a statement is false if it is "false or misleading" whether "wholly or in part" and whether on its own or in the context in which it appears.
*   This requires an **objective construction** of the subject material that "regardless of the subjective intention of the statement maker", there would be at least an "**appreciable segment**" (TOC SGCA 2021) of the population or a particular class of the "**potential**" readership or audience of the subject material and even though a subject material may include multiple interpretations, **as long as the subject statement is "one reasonable interpretation"**, and that interpretation is false, a Part 3 Direction may be issued.
*   While it is unclear as to what amounts to an "appreciable segment" of the population, it appears that a **mere "likelihood"** of "part of the subject material" being taken out of context will suffice. Additionally, the fact that an "appreciable segment" of the population includes that of "**potential**" readership further lowers the threshold by allowing Part 3 Directions to be issued as a pre-emptive strike of sorts.
*   The SGCA also clarified that the construction of the subject material is objective, such that “regardless of the subjective intention of the statement maker”, as long as the subject statement is “one reasonable interpretation” and that interpretation is false, a Part 3 Direction may be issued.
*   **Chilling Effect**: the prospect or potential of being on the receiving end of a CD could lead to a chilling effect. A CD notice could have the effect of impugning the credibility of the author and the concern is that it could lead to self-censorship that would make participants in online debates refrain from raising their opinions or otherwise making valid comments for fear of governmental sanction.
*   Additionally, s 4 of POFMA defines "public interest" broadly and in a manner that practically and intentionally mirrors the derogations stipulated in Art 14(2) of the Constitution. This means that it is not only easy to find a false statement of fact, but also for the Minister to find that it is in the public interest to issue a Part 3 Direction, and once any false statement of fact is found, the derogation from free speech would be Constitutionally sanctioned. The ease with which Part 3 Directions can be ordered does exert a chilling effect on a civil society in Singapore.

### Minister is granted too much discretionary power under the Vague wording of POFMA

Further, POFMA’s vague formulations have provided Ministers with an expansive, perhaps almost absolute power of discretion. It is the Minister who decides to issue the correction direction. It is also the Minister who decides to approve a request challenging the correction direction at first instance (s 19). Reality suggests that the chances of ministers overturning their own decisions unilaterally is low or virtually zero. While an appeal can be made to the Courts, very few participants, citizens, news sites will have the resources or the appetite for litigation in the Courts.

**“Public interest” is too wide**: POFMA is phrased broadly such that the Minister may issue a correction if it is “in the public interest” to do so. This includes cases of influencing the outcome of an election, and diminution of public confidence in key public institutions (s 4(f) POFMA). Such areas are not expressly provided for in the Constitution under Art 14(2). The SGCA held that this was constitutional insofar as there was a **nexus with “public order”** (which was broadly defined). Parliament is therefore afforded "a much wider legislative remit that allows Parliament to take a prophylactic approach in the maintenance of public order. This necessarily will include laws that are not purely designed or crafted with immediately or direct maintenance of public order" (TOC)

Whilst there is an appeal process for statement makers, and the ultimate remit lies with the Courts the court can only hear the appeal under **_s 17 POFMA_** after the statement maker has first applied to the Minister in **_s 19 POFMA_** to vary or cancel the Part 3 direction and the Minister has refused the application whether in whole or in part (**_s 17(2) POFMA_**). Even when the case goes before the court the court can only examine 3 limited grounds of appeal (1) whether the person communicated the subject statement in Singapore (2) whether the subject statement is not a statement of fact or is true, or (3) whether it is technically possible to comply with the Direction (17(5) POFMA).

This means that Ministers can technically act against falsehoods where there is a strong argument that the statement does not actually undermine public interest. This raises the fear that political office holders will use POFMA to **quash political dissent** where a falsehood is perpetuated not to undermine faith in government institutions (although this may be a corollary) but rather to disagree with government policies. A potential area for reform is to accord judicial oversight over whether there are bona fide public interest grounds that warrant action against the falsehood in order to better secure political legitimacy.

*   **Counter: Although cannot assess if in public interest this is due to separation of powers**

However a counterpoint would be that it is simply not within the institutional competence of the courts to pronounce on what the “public interest” is as such questions are beyond the remit of the courts, who “however eminent in the law, are not the ultimate repositories of human wisdom in answering the kinds of social, economic and political questions with which parliament and administrators have to deal” (**_Tan Seet Eng_**).

Aside from institutional competence issues there is also the matter of democratic legitimacy, and the courts are clear that they are in no place to sit as a “super-legislature” (**_Yong Vui Kong_**), issuing elite judicial diktats whilst being unaccountable to the wider public and so it is not for them to pronounce on what the public interest in. It is therefore submitted that the current balance in terms of allowing appeals on the falsity of the statement and not on whether it is actually in the public interest to curb it **strikes a balance** that reflects fidelity to the separation of powers, especially since the courts must “exercise restraint and take cognisance” of the fact that it exists as part of three “co-equal branches” (**_Review Publishing_**) and cannot overstep its own boundaries as much as it serves to ensure that other branches do not act ultra vires.

### Burden of proof to set aside Part 3 direction is on Statement Maker– Effect of Chilling

**Information Asymmetry**: The burden of proof lies on the **recipient** of the Part 3 Direction to prove one or more grounds listed in s 17(5) upon which the Direction may be set aside **_TOC v AG 2021 SGCA \[at 183\]_** .It is difficult for “the applicant seeking to appeal the Minister’s decision to prove that the impugned statement is true if it relates to governmental matters, because of “information asymmetry between the Government and individuals” (_Sylvia Lim, POFM Bill Parliamentary Debates)_. Unlike the Minister who is able to rely on the machinery of state to procure the relevant evidence of falsity, the maker of the statement has to contend with far more limited resources (**_Ang Cheng Hock J in SDP v AG_**). Such a situation raises the question of whether Parliament intended to place such an onerous burden on the statement maker. If that was parliament’s intention, it should have been expressed clearly. The fact that it was not expressed clearly further buttresses the argument that the burden should fall on the Government.

**Chilling Effect**: the prospect or potential of being on the receiving end of a CD could lead to a chilling effect. A CD notice could have the effect of impugning the credibility of the author and the concern is that it could lead to self-censorship that would be participants in online debates refrain from raising their opinions or otherwise making valid comments for fear of governmental sanction. Indeed the same logic appears to have been accepted albeit in the context of political leaders where in _Jeyaretnam_ it was articulated that if political leaders and their reputations were not strongly protected this may deter “sensitive and honourable men from seeking public positions” if “so wide an extension of the privilege” of free speech were accorded in cases of public officials, leaving them “open to others who have no respect for their reputation” (Jeyaretnam affirming Gatley on Libel and Slander). Likewise if free speech is not robustly protected, and the burden of proof lies on the statement-maker to challenge a Part 3 Direction, this may deter would be critics from speaking out in the first place. Hence the Art 14 argument in SDP should be followed.

### Efficacy of Part 3 Directions

However, there are questions as to the efficacy of a Part 3 Corrective Direction in particular, if a large segment of the population has already been affected by the falsehood. This is because a Part 3 Correction Direction only requires the one person who communicated the subject statement, to communicate a statement that the subject statement is false. As mentioned, "falsehoods can be rapidly disseminated in an unregulated internet sphere" (Ting Choon Meng SGCA 2017) and given how much of the internet is unregulated, information and falsehoods can be disseminated at an exhilarating rate. As such, by the time the maker of the subject statement makes the corrective statement, most or at least an "appreciable segment" of the public would have been affected by the falsehood. For example, a fake social media post of a man looking like PM Lawrence Wong smoking in public may have been forwarded or reposted many times on social media platforms. Such posts may subsequently be edited with deepfake technology for the man to look even more like Lawrence Wong himself. Despite the corrective direction being issued, netizens may not read the new statement by the subject statement maker in correcting his statement. In this sense, although a corrective direction is preventive in nature and that it does exert a chilling effect, the efficacy of the corrective direction itself does repair the damage done to society such as the loss of trust in public institutions

## POFMA: Constitutional Application

| Hansard: Shanmugam, Second Reading of POFMA bill: important to preserve trust in public institutions, and to prevent erosion of trust in the state / government institutions of the day.Long title (Purpose of Act, s 5 of POFMA): prevent the communication of false statements; safeguard against information manipulation, and enable to enhance transparency of online political advertisements and for related matters.Communication of false statements in SG, s 7(1) of POFMA: speaker in communicating must not have known or have reason to believe the false statement of fact, and the communication of that statement is likely to, inter alia, prejudice national security, public health, public safety, or public tranquillity, or incite feelings of enmity, hatred, or ill-will between different groups of persons. individuals who contravene s 7(1) may receive a fine not exceeding $50,000 or an imprisonment not exceeding 5 years or both (s 7(2)(a)).As will be seen, the latter half of this provision (especially the part on incitement of feelings) essentially refers to the consequence of hate speech, while the former half refers to the act that causes it (a false statement of fact) (Chen Siyuan "Regulating Online Hate Speech" 2023)Part 3 Directions: Any minister may instruct the Competent Authority to issue a Part 3 Direction (s 10(1))Public Interest, s 4: “necessary and expedient”…Correction Direction, s 11(1): requiring the person who communicated the subject statement to (a) put up a statement explaining how the subject statement is false or contains a false statement of fact; and (b) include a specific statement of fact. |
| --- |

In **_TOC v AG_**, the court dealt with the issue, holding that the applicable framework was _Jolovan Wham_, but before applying the framework the court must first consider the question whether statements are false statements of fact for the purposes of Part 3 POFMA

*   The court held that the question with POFMA orders is whether speech that is considered by the Minister to constitute a false statement of fact, but that has yet to be proven to be so in a court of law, is **nonetheless protected** from restriction by Art 14(1)(a) of the Constitution
    *   under a POFMA order, the communicator of the subject statement is not required to modify the content of the communicated material; nor is it prevented from modifying, if it so wishes, the content of that material… the communicator .. remains free to speak in respect of the communicated material, save that it must also put up the specified correction notice in respect of that material
*   under a POFMA challenge, the applicant may argue that the subject statement is true, per s 17(5)(b) Act:
    *   In that case, if the court finds the subject statement to indeed be a true statement of fact, the correction order may be set aside pursuant to s 17(4) read with s 17(5)(b) POFMA
    *   If the court finds the statement to be false, then the issuance of the CD would **not** have violated the right to freedom of speech of the communicator of the subject statement, given the view we have expressed on occasion that, arguably, this constitutional right might not apply to speech that has been judicially determined to be false
    *   The applicant is the effective plaintiff because the Minister has already justified his decision in a sense via the corrective direction as justification for the order being given – so long as the applicant can show a _prima facie_ case of reasonable suspicion, the evidential burden will then shift to the Minister to show that none of the grounds under s 17(5)(a) and/or s 17(5)(b) that are relied upon by the recipient of the Part 3 Direction are made out … SOP is balance of probabilities

### The applicable framework to determine falsity in speech

*   *   Determine the Minister’s intended meaning in respect of the subject statement identified in Part 3 Direction
    *   Determine whether the subject material in fact makes or contains the subject statement (or statements) identified by the Minister in the Part 3 Direction
        *   Interpret subject material objectively in its proper context, and as a matter of impression rather than of fine-grained or unduly technical analysis
        *   Asking whether, on that approach to the subject material, there would be at least an appreciable segment or a particular class of the potential readership or audience of the subject material in Singapore who would construe it as making or containing the subject statement, or regard the subject statement as a reasonable interpretation of the subject material
        *   While considering the subject material as a whole, the court should be alive to the likelihood that the particular part of the subject material that is said to be or to give rise to the subject statement may be taken out of context
    *   Determine whether the identified subject statement is a “statement of fact” by an objective approach
    *   Consider whether the identified subject statement “has been or is being communicated in Singapore”, in the sense that it has been or is being published on or through the internet, and has been or is being accessed by at least one end-user in Singapore
*   Once determined to be false, the _Jolovan Wham_ framework applies to determine whether necessary or expedient to impose the restriction, and whether there is a nexus between purpose of the legislation containing the restriction and one or more of the Art 14(2) grounds
*   Court held that it was entirely conceivable that words spread online could threaten the preservation of public order by undermining trust in the Govt and other key public institutions

### Setting Aside CD

Upon receipt of a Part 3 Direction, the communicator of the subject statement may **apply to the Minister** under _s 19(2) of the POFMA_ to **vary or cancel** the Direction. If the Minister declines to vary or cancel the Direction, whether in whole or in part, the communicator of the subject statement may **apply to the High Court** under **_s 17_** of the POFMA to set aside the Direction.

*   Placing BoP on Minister from the outset opens the way for abuse

**3 Grounds to Set Aside**:

_s 17(5) POFMA:_ The High Court may only set aside a Part 3 Direction on any of the following grounds on an appeal:

(a) the person did not communicate in Singapore the subject statement;

(b) the subject statement is not a statement of fact, or is a true statement of fact;

(c) it is not technically possible to comply with the Direction.

### Hate Speech (covered by POFMA)? – No.

Interestingly, ever since POFMA was passed into law, it has yet to be deployed in regulating online hate speech. As of June 2022, it was invoked 38 times since it came into force in 2019, with most instances targeting the correction of COVID-19 Misinformation (POFMA Office 2022). As for online hate speech, they have been punished under the Penal Code instead. For example, s 298 punishes any persons who utters words with the deliberate intent to wound the racial feelings of any person. While POFMA mainly targets the communication of false statements of fact, the latter half of s 7(1) and especially on the part on "incitement of feelings of enmity, hatred, or ill-will between different groups of persons" targets the consequence of hate speech, while the former half refers to the acts that causes it (**_Chen Siyuan "Regulating Online Hate Speech" 2023_**).

# Art 15 – Freedom of Religion

| Freedom of religion15.—(1)  Every person has the right to profess and practise his religion and to propagate it.(2)  No person shall be compelled to pay any tax the proceeds of which are specially allocated in whole or in part for the purposes of a religion other than his own.(3)  Every religious group has the right —(a)to manage its own religious affairs;(b)to establish and maintain institutions for religious or charitable purposes; and(c)to acquire and own property and hold and administer it in accordance with law.(4)  This Article does not authorise any act contrary to any general law relating topublic order, public health or moralityReligious Group RightsArt 16(2) every religious group has the right to establish and maintain institutions for the education of children and provide therein instruction in its own religion, and there shall be no discrimination on the ground only of religion in any law relating to such institutions or in the administration of any such law.Art 16(3) no person shall be required to receive instruction in or to take part in any ceremony or act of worship of a religion other than his ownMinorities and special position of MalaysArt 152(1) It shall be the responsibility of the Government constantly to care for the interests of the racial and religious minorities in Singapore.Art 152(2) The Government shall exercise its functions in such manner as to recognise the special position of the Malays, who are the indigenous people of Singapore, and accordingly it shall be the responsibility of the Government to protect, safeguard, support, foster and promote their political, educational, religious, economic, social and cultural interests and the Malay language.Muslim ReligionArt 153 The Legislature shall by law make provision for regulating Muslim religious affairs and for constituting a Council to advise the President in matters relating to the Muslim religion. | Freedom of religion | 15.—(1)  Every person has the right to profess and practise his religion and to propagate it.(2)  No person shall be compelled to pay any tax the proceeds of which are specially allocated in whole or in part for the purposes of a religion other than his own.(3)  Every religious group has the right —(a)to manage its own religious affairs;(b)to establish and maintain institutions for religious or charitable purposes;
