## Scope of Art 15

*   Dealing with “right to profess”, the court in **_Ong Kian Cheong_** dealt with the right in the context of Christians who distributed the Chick Publications tracts, and intentionally targeted Muslim people and sent the tracts to them
    *   Court recognised that evangelism is a paramount Christian duty, but case law recognised that this right cannot be unfettered right
    *   Distributing tracts with callous, denigratory, offensive and insensitive statements on religion with aspersions to race do have a tendency to cause social unrest thereby jeopardising racial and religious harmony
    *   In this case, the Christian accused persons distributed the tracts intentionally, reflecting their intolerance, insensitivity and ignorance of delicate issues concerning race and religion
*   But, why permit it if it creates problems?
    *   **Thio** (2011) _Fides et Libertas_: The Journal of the International Liberty Association 127: as people, we are all proselytisers, seeking to convince our hearers to our side
    *   **Fink**, _Critics of Conversion_ (22 September 2003): asking people not to evangelise is to tell them to forsake their religion, which is impossible for the faithful
        *   Supports evangelism as not about imposing worldviews on others, but convenience others of their truth
        *   Argues that without evangelism, communities would remain isolated and separated, thus a sectarian balkanisation of sorts
        *   Criticises those who seek to prevent evangelisation to be motivated by their own intention / agendas on their own
        *   As to the argument that religion causes conflict, there are myriad of other reasons that justified atrocities
        *   Extreme measures are the catalyst for more violence

## How does our Constitution and Constitutionalism blend with religious freedom?

The Singapore model of religion-state relations has been described by the SGCA to be one of "**accommodative secularism**" (_Nappalli_) or '**quasi-secular**' (_Thio, Control, co-optation and cooperation_). This means that Singapore’s brand of constitutional secularism is not anti-theistic, but framed along agnostic or benevolent lines, where the state does not favour or disfavour religions, nor favour irreligion over religion. Singapore's brand of secularism does not connote **state hostility** towards religion, but rather a **respectful attitude** towards religion (_MRHA White Paper, para 5; Thio, Religion in Public Sphere_).

**\[Quasi-Secular\]**

Thio argues that the Singapore polity may be better understood as being a quasi-secular state instead. This is because of the constitutional obligation under Art 152(1) of the government having to ‘_constantly care for the interests of the racial and religious minorities in Singapore_’. Furthermore, in recognizing the _‘special position of the Malays’_ as the ‘indigenous people of Singapore’, the government is obliged under Art 152(2) to promote and foster their political, educational, religious, economic, social and cultural interests and the Malay language (_Thio, “Control, Co-optation and Cooperation (2006)_).

**\[Theocratic elements\]**

In **Thio**, _Treatise_ at pp 48-50, Thio considers if there are **theocratic elements** in Singapore’s practice of secular democracy

*   Such elements include religious institutions, religious law being the or a basis for state law, or religious affiliation being a basis for membership
*   Additionally, in **Thio** (2006) 33 Hastings Constitutional Law Quarterly 197, Thio suggests that SG model is not fully secular
    *   Art 152 requires the government **constantly to care for the interests of the racial and religious minorities in Singapore**, with 152(2) obliging the government to foster (specially) the Malay language
    *   Art 153 also mandates legislature to make laws to regulate Muslim religious affairs and constitute MUIS to advise in relation to Muslim religion. AMLA also establishes Sharia courts with its own jurisdiction and whose decisions are generally free from judicial review
    *   In a way, Muslims are given privileged treatment through government sponsored policies.
*   **Examples**: Muslim marriages are not subject to the Women’s Charter and polygamous marriages are allowed.

## Racial and Religious Harmony

### What is it and how do we promote / preserve it?

*   K Shanmugam, “Staying United against the Terrorism Threat (Motion)”, _Singapore Parliamentary Debates, Official Report_ (3 October 2017) vol 113
    *   Religious teachings must be aligned with national values, and if engage in discrimination it will not be acceptable
    *   It is important for religions to coexist harmoniously, tied to the value of religious harmony
    *   SG’s approach is to build an identity that can accommodate diversity and celebrate different racial and religious identities, while creating a broad common space that all can share as Singaporeans and build sense of solidarity as one nation
*   K Shanmugam, speech at International Conference on the Role of NGOs in Promoting Culture of Peace (13 October 2017)
    *   To SG, religion is inextricable part of culture but not essential. State has active role to play in maintaining religious harmony and freedom
    *   **Racial and religious harmony is a necessary** condition for flourishing, given our history and proclivities / sensitivities to racial and religious tensions. It is a potential faultline that can threaten public order. As such, the call for harmony is about peaceful coexistence, and to prevent divisive speech that, at worst, denigrates those of other beliefs. It is this extremely cautious approach to divisive speech that leads to the MHA’s role in, for example, banning foreign speakers who do not understand / treat seriously the values here
*   With 2019 amendments to MRHA, the statute addresses new threats such as foreign influence over local religious organisations and enhance ministerial powers to deal with the impact of the Internet as a vehicle for religious disharmony
    *   Government can now **issue restraining orders** to anyone who threatens religious harmony. Lastly, the Community Remedial Initiative introduced which is a new tool focused on restoration and rehabilitation
        *   Restraining orders issued under ss 8 and 9 now take immediate effect when issued, rather than 14 day notice period, enabling Minister to act swiftly
        *   Minister can also require an offensive post be taken down, but must give a copy of the restraining order to the relevant religious leader or group who can still make representations to the Presidential Council for Religious Harmony (PCRH)
    *   **_However_**, the problem of religious speech on the Internet is speech intended to cause indignation, a political tool (**Cherian George**)
        *   Such speech, which he terms hate spin, is a performative act, righteous indignation that incites anger deliberately – thus, if the law is used against such speech, that is the intended outcome
        *   This results in the law being a poor instrument to deal with the issue due to inherent asymmetry in the offence
        *   Singapore’s approach may appear to be working, as it results show a citizenry that has embraced and internalised the vision of a harmonious society. Thus, when the words that are inflammatory appear, there is an immediate response to censure / police the words, which ironically is the intended outcome
        *   Author’s view is more cynical: Conditioning the public to internalise a low threshold for insult and to reflexively reach for the law helps to perpetuate the sense of vulnerability, the distrust in social conciliation and the dependence on strong government that have served well as ideological supports for the PAP's brand of illiberal democracy

## MRHA

### What is MRHA?

While MRHA is a form of **hard law** in form, the MRHA also contains elements of soft law that is persuasive in its orientation. While religious harmony is used as a controlling legal principle to empower the government to issue pre-emptive restraining orders, in practice, no restraining order has been issued by the government. Hence, the MRHA is not strictly enforced and is preventive in nature.

**Purpose**: The original rationale of the MHRA was ostensibly to “prevent the mixing of religion and politics”, and "to prevent religious leaders from using their offices as a platform for mounting political agitation and making inflammatory political speeches" (**_TT4 at \[18.106\]_**); it was pre-emptive in nature (_Goh Chok Tong, Parliamentary Debates 1990_). It was motivated by instances of aggressive proselytization that had made people from other religions uncomfortable and offended.

**2019 Amendments**: The 2019 amendments were motivated by the following changes: (1) **rising religiosity**; (2) **increasing violence** from conflicts fuelled by religious hate speech; (3) **Internet/social media**; and (4) **foreign interference**. These created the risk of religion being exploited by bad actors and being used to mobilise hatred and violence, destroying social cohesion.

### Good and Bad of MRHA

**GOOD: MRHA Calibrated approach, sensitive to Offender’s Position.**

*   The MRHA in its amended form provides for a more nuanced restriction to religious liberties sensitive to the status and platform that the offender has. For example, _s 17F MRHA_ makes a distinction between religious leaders and religious followers who incite hatred or ill-will against another religious group.
    *   For **religious leaders** the threshold for an offence appears to be **lower** in that one must (1) knowingly incite hatred (2) the subject of that leader’s speech is distinguished by religion and (3) the leader must have knowledge that feelings of hatred or ill will are likely to arise (s 17F(2)(a)-(c)).
    *   However, if one is **not a religious leader**, an additional fourth element that the feelings of “hatred, ill-will or hostility … of the target group” must “threaten the public peace or public order in Singapore or any part of Singapore” (s 17F(3)(d)) must be fulfilled.

**BAD: Subjective Standard of Feelings 🡺 Hate Spin + Speaker is hostage to victim’s tolerance levels**

*   As a starting point, it is an offence for a person to engage in conduct that incites "**feelings**" of hatred, ill-will and hostility (_s 17F(1)(a)_). Additionally, a Minister may issue a restraining order against any religious leader or person who committed or is **likely** to commit an act that causes "feelings" of hatred, ill-will or hostility between religious groups (s 8(1)).
*   It is submitted that a **subjective standard for “feelings”** would (1) hold the speaker **hostage** to potential irrational sensitivities of segments of society as different persons have varying levels of tolerance, acceptance and sensitivities towards religious speech. (2)
*   It is also submitted that a subjective standard potentially engenders "**hate spin**" (_Cherian George, "Hate Spin: The Manufacture of Religious Offence..."_) which refers to the deliberate use of outrage and offence to mobilize political support, by stirring up or pretending to be outraged by supposed insults to religion per se, which creates a chilling effect on public discourse on religious issues, which is undesirable in our democracy.

**BAD: Lack of Proper Safeguards**

*   Notably, the Minister may issue a restraining order under _s 8(1)(a) of the MRHA_ even if the Minister is merely satisfied that the person is only "likely to commit" an act that causes feelings of hatred, ill-will or hostility between different religious groups. Such an order is **not amenable to judicial review** per s 18 of the MRHA, thereby ousting the jurisdiction of the court to review such orders. While it may be argued that such issues pertaining to religious harmony should be non-justiciable as a "secular court deciding on religious disputes" would be "explosive" (John Chen, MRHA second reading 1990), the MRHA does not have adequate safeguards.
*   The **only check on the restraining order is the president** (s 12 MRHA r/w Art 221) which states that every restraining order shall cease to have effect unless it is confirmed by the President within 30 days from the date the Presidential Council for Religious Harmony's recommendations are received by the President. The President is expected to act in his discretion if the advice of the Cabinet is contrary to the Council's recommendation. However, if the Cabinet and Council are in agreement, the President does not provide an independent safeguard. This places much weight on the independence of the PCRH.
    *   **Defence for PCRH**: As Council members are majority representatives of the major religions in Singapore, and distinguished themselves in public service or community relations in Singapore, academic **_Thio_** believes this reflects the cooperative model envisioned in MRHA whitepaper – symbolises the trust in the maturity of the religious leaders and consequently, participation from the bottom of the system. It has the potential of being a tension diffusion mechanism.

**GOOD: MRHA actually conciliatory in practice**

*   However despite the lack of safeguards and also a possibly low threshold to make out offences post-amendments, the restraining orders under the **MRHA have never been used since 1990** and thus it could be argued that the wide discretion and powers to use these restraining orders is **more symbolic**, and should be seen as bolstering existing legal safeguards through the courts and other legislation like the Sedition Act , the Undesirable Publications Act etc and rather heightening the “signalling effect of the MRHA in response to the increased weaponization of religion” (Shanmugam “Second Reading MRHA”).

### Should the MRHA be subject to judicial review?

Ouster clause (s 18 MRHA) currently insulates restraining orders from court challenge. Two competing positions in 2019 parliamentary debates:

*   **FOR** judicial review (_Murali Pillai_): JR focuses on process, not replacing political judgment. Minority interests are not adequately protected by electoral accountability alone. Procedural safeguards (e.g., in camera hearings) can address concern about public proceedings inflaming tensions. Constitutional supremacy under Art 4 requires judicial oversight where Art 15 rights are infringed.
*   **AGAINST** judicial review (_Shanmugam_): Public trial processes can deepen fault lines and make offenders into 'martyrs.' Religious harmony questions are polycentric — legislators and the executive are better placed to judge. Existing non-judicial safeguards: Presidential Council of Religious Harmony (PCRH) can review restraining orders and make recommendations to uphold or cancel them.

The broader question is **what the approach toward religious issues** ought to be?

*   **Thio** (2012) Oxford Journal of Law and Religion 1 at 14-15: SG has a phenomenon she ascribes as “relational constitutionalism”, a form of constitutionalism where inter-religious disputes are reconciled. This method asks for non-legalistic methods, preferring soft, non-binding hortatory norms. It draws form the idea that when dealing with religious issues, we are dealing with Emotional and not Rational Men
*   thus, if fear of the law cannot be the basis for religious harmony, the solution must be in these non-legalistic methods.
*   As illustrated in **Thio**, “_Irreducible Plurality_, _Indivisible Unity_” (2019) German Law Journal 1007, the performative politics entails almost a ritual, where parties take centre stage and reconcile publicly, with the legal framework and government receding and only re-emerging after the settlement, which sustains the perception that government is not acting in a heavy-handed interventionist manner, which can alienate faith communities
    *   optics and the impression of religious harmony is crucial to restore harmonious equilibrium
    *   process facilitates an environment where social trust and strong relational bonds can be cultivated through dialogue and diplomacy
*   s 16H MRHA also promotes this idea of “rehabilitation”, with the offender being given an option to enter community remedial and ideally correct their offending perspectives
    *   communal vs individual aims?
    *   Voluntary in that the offender can reject the offer to engage in CRI
    *   How should the CRI be administered?

## The role of judiciary in religious matters

### What is religion?

It first entails – what is a religion? There is no constitutional or statutory definition of "religion".

*   As identified in **Adler**, “_Religion_” (1992), one working definition of religion could be that:
    *   To a man of faith, Religion is much more than the acceptance of a creed. It means acts of piety and worship, recourse to prayer, partaking of sacraments, observance of certain rituals, performance of sacrifices and purifications
    *   To one who is not religious, religion is conceived as nothing more than a set of beliefs which men have adopted, restricted to merely one part of life.
*   \[**Expert opinion to ascertain Religion Practices**\] In **_Vijaya Kumar_**, the court had to determine what qualifies as a ‘religious practice’ – which would lead into the broader question of what a religion was given the prevalence of practices
    *   In the case, a **Hindu expert was called** by the appellants, who testified to impugned practice. The court accepted his expert opinion, despite his unconventional expertise, as he was involved with it and could at least speak with a fair measure of authority on the subject matter.
    *   Courts **should not adopt a legalistic approach** to who could be an expert in religious matters, and even if practices vary, unless they are completely not part of religious practices, they should be permitted.
*   \[**Nature** **of a Religious Right**\] was raised in the case of **_Nappali v ITE_**
    *   “Religion” referred to Art 15 is not about a system of belief in one’s own country but about a citizen’s faith in a personal God that can sometimes be described as supernatural being.
    *   Appellant was a JW, refused to take the pledge or sing National Anthem and thus dismissed by ITE. He sued for unfair dismissal, arguing that he had informed the ITE interview panel of his refusal to salute the flag etc and that their acceptance of this was part of the employment contract, which his dismissal breached. He further argued that ITE's policy requiring participation in the pledge and anthem ceremony was unconstitutional.
        *   Court held that religious rights are Constitutional, and may not be contracted out. That said, the issue was whether the acts in question had religious significance
        *   Court held that taking pledge and nation building acts were secular, since they were prescribed by Constitution which was secular
        *   Additionally, court said that religious belief in Singapore is a narrow definition, as having a broad view could lead to situations where one’s religious symbols become offensive to another person
        *   Court at \[28\] expressly views JW as **not** a religious belief, albeit a strong **philosophical choice**
            *   **My concern**: Rationale of “secular tone” and philosophical choice is unpersuasive. The fact that the flag is not inherently a religious symbol, or that the anthem is not a “prayer hymn” (as distinguished in Donald), does not preclude such practices from engaging religious conscience. For some believers, acts of pledging allegiance or expressing reverence toward state symbols may conflict with core doctrinal commitments—such as the prohibition against worshipping false kings or sovereigns.
            *   Thus, reiterating the concern: the court did not deal with what a religion is or what weight to give a person’s belief, rather simply prejudging the issue
        *   Importantly, the court held that as the Constitution is a secular document, and guarantees religious freedom, it cannot simultaneously be a violation of religious rights
    *   In contrast, in the Philippines case of **_Ebralinag_**, similar case where JW students were expelled for refusing to salute the flat etc which were mandated by the Act
        *   There, the SC held that religious freedom is a fundamental right in the Constitution, which consists two aspects**: freedom to believe and freedom to act on one’s belief**. The first is absolute so long as the belief is confined within the realm of thought, while second is subject to regulation where the belief is translated into external acts that affect the public welfare
            *   The court was **more sympathetic to the applicants**, arguing that when general laws conflict with scruples of conscience, exemptions ought to be granted unless some ‘compelling state interests’ intervenes
            *   By requiring the petitioners to participate in the flag ceremony, the State has declared _ex cathedra_ that they are not violating the Bible by saluting the flag. This is an unwarranted intrusion into religious beliefs, which tell them the opposite. The State has no competence in helping or telling them how to interpret or derive meaning from their religious beliefs
        *   This adoption of a **subjective method**, giving primacy to beliefs of applicants, is more lenient on what a religion is
*   \[**Proportionality Approach** cf. categorical in _Colin Chan/Nappali_\] In **_Vijaya Kumar_**, perhaps a new balance was struck by the HC
    *   While police showed legitimate public order concerns, the appellants argued that the extent to which the police order restricted their religious freedom had to be assessed. The court appeared to engage in a evaluation of the substantive content of the order, evoking a proportionality-based balancing exercise
    *   In this regard, a **stronger measure of what a religious belief was afforded**, and the extent to which the freedom was curtailed was also considered
    *   This jurisprudential development would give in theory greater primacy to religious protection – even if the act fell within the 3 categories, and would automatically be subject to limits per _Nappalli_ or _Colin Chan_ (1994) and (1995), the HC in _Vijaya Kumar_ has heralded the possibility to considering the extent of blanket ban vs targeted restriction and still afford the right to be exercised, curtailed but not completely curbed

### Propagation may lead to Civil Strife; Must be controlled.

Refers to the freedom to **spread religious knowledge** which is in effect "**religious free speech**" (_Treatise_) that benefits the individual seeker and serves the missionary mandate integral to many religions. The objective of religious propagation is to **persuade voluntary changes to religious belief**; thus propagation is closely tied to conversion and the right to profess a faith, which includes the right to change faiths voluntarily.

Religious propagation **may upset religious sensitivities or disrupt religious communities** and involve "a clash between rights" (Lerner, Religion, _Secular Beliefs and Human Rights_).

*   In **_Ong Kian Cheong_**_,_ a Christian couple was charged with sedition for sending tracts which were intended to convert Muslims that they had identified through names on residential directories to Christians, but consisted of material that **denigrated Islam as a false religion** which upset various Muslim recipients. The case was treated **primarily under the Art 14 free speech** guarantee even though it implicated **_Art 15(1)_** specifically the right to propagate one’s religion.
    *   In 2009 _PM Lee Hsien Loong_ highlighted _Ong Kian Cheong_ during his 2009 National Day Rally speech as an “**extreme**” instance of the negative effects of insensitive and aggressive proselytization which can upset religious sensitivities of various communities, possibly endangering social harmony. Such aggressive proselytization was characterised as situations where “you push your own religion on others and **cause nuisance and offence**”.

### “Public Order” too broad? ≠ National Security

**_As an evaluation_**, a particularly striking feature of Yong CJ's reasoning in **_Colin Chan_** is the apparent equation of "public order" with "national security," treating the phrases "threat to national security" and "contrary to public peace, welfare and good order" as interchangeable. With respect, this conflation is both **confused and likely erroneous**.

As articulated in **_Tan Boon Liat_**, "public order" denotes the "tranquility and security which every person feels under the protection of the law, a breach of which is an invasion of the protection which the protection of the law affords." This is qualitatively distinct from national security, which refers to graver threats of the kind that justify the exercise of special legislative powers, including anti-subversion legislation. Tellingly, the same language used in Colin Chan to characterise JW pacifism appears in the anti-subversion provisions of Article 149, which contemplates acts "prejudicial to the security of Singapore." It is, however, difficult to characterise a minority religious group's conscientious refusal to participate in national service as an existential threat of that magnitude.

The result is that "public order" in Colin Chan was conflated with national security and, in effect, judicially supersized to carry the same normative weight as national security considerations (_Treatise_). This conflation becomes even harder to sustain upon closer examination of the constitutional text: Article 149 does not include Article 15 within its list of liberties from which it may derogate with immunity. The equation in Colin Chan therefore lacks textual foundation and forecloses the kind of nuanced, proportionate balancing exercise that the protection of minority religious rights demands.

### Whether a policy like NS is ‘sacred’ or ‘secular’? Old categorical approach.

In **_Nappali_**, the court merely declared that because the Constitution adopts accommodative secularism, and that policy like NS is by nature a secular and fundamental tenet and thus cannot bear religious significance to engage Art 15. **This approach, in my opinion, is flawed**. In holding a policy secular without engaging the claimant’s Art 15 religious belief, Art 15(4) can render a key constitutional right meaningless or even reads unenumerated rights into our constitution.

*   In Yong CJ's reasoning in **_Colin Chan_**_,_ it is concerning that he elevated of national service to a status superior to enumerated constitutional rights, by insisting that "anything which detracts from this should not and cannot be upheld". Fundamentally, this formulation sits uneasily with the SGCA's own warning in **_Yong Vui Kong_** against reading unenumerated rights into the Constitution for fear of the court acting as a "**super-legislature**." National service, however vital as a matter of policy, is a creature of statute subordinate to, not co-ordinate with, constitutional rights (_Thio, "The Secular Trumps the Sacred" (1995)_).
*   Similarly in **_Nappalli_** the court appeared to try to balance the national unity and freedom of religion but ultimately ended up preferring one over the other, holding unequivocally that “the Ministry of education is entitled to take reasonable steps to ensure that future generations of Singaporeans understand the importance of preserving the sovereignty, integrity and unity of Singapore” despite _Ong Ah Chuan’_s articulation that the Constitution be **interpreted to give full effect to fundamental liberties**.

**_Instead_**, it is suggested that the courts should acknowledge the claimant’s rights before balancing it with the derogation grounds within Art 15(4). As _Thio (1995)_ observes, safety and security are not ends in themselves but are "facilitative of other ends like individual self-fulfilment and the ability to make choices in a stable framework," and the "beneficiary of fundamental liberties is the individual rather than the state." Essentially, this approach does not deviate in prior judgements, but it will enable the courts to engage with a more principled basis instead of merely denying a right prima facie.

### Policy Cases involving Religion

*   **E.g. Military Uniform**: In **_Pte Chai Tshun Chieh v Chief Military Prosecutor_**, the refusal of the AP to put on military uniform, draw rifle and engage in military training constituted a wilful disobedience of a lawful order under s 17(1) of the Singapore Armed Forces Act. **Held** : JWs were "engaging in conduct which was **prejudicial to national security**".
    *   Conscientious objection does not apply in Singapore and national service is a "secular issue" subject to government laws.
*   **E.g. "Tudung Controversy"**: Education policy of SG government in public primary schools is that students should wear the same uniforms, as a reminder of "the **common ties** that bind us in a common destiny" (**_Straits Times : Lim Chee Hwee "Uniform Reminds Students of Common Ties"_**) for the purpose of promoting integration in a common space between different races and religions to promote a shared national identity. In Feb 2002, 4 primary schoolgirls were suspended for flouting common uniforms policy by wearing tudung or Muslim headscarf to school. It was argued by foreign human rights organisations like Karamah and local Muslim group Fateha that the policy violated religious freedom and practice.
    *   In response, the Government argued that Sikhs can wear turbans "because of a colonial legacy that is one of the historical anomalies of a young nation", pointing out that Sikhs had to compromise as they cannot carry kirpans (daggers) which is part of their religious dress. The fear was that allowing religious dressing in public schools would be alienating, heighten differences and increase demands for special treatment such that schools would "become polarised along racial and religious lines" (**_Goh Chok Tong "By Barring Religious Garb" New York Times_**). While this issue was not litigated, it demonstrates from the Government perspective how "public order" construed broadly as entailing the protection of national integration policies, as defined by the Government, would be raised as a restraint on religious freedom.

## Relational Constitutionalism Evaluation (Soft Law)

As an extension to the accommodative aspect of secularism that Singapore espouses, Singapore also adopts a model of **relational constitutionalism** in which rights are not oppositional to the common good. Conflicts are thereby resolved **dialogically with a view to reconciliation**. This is because a rigid insistence on rights may breed animosity and thwart the restoration of relationships between estranged community sectors (**_Thio "Secularism With a Soul Model" 2012_**). It is suggested that we align with how the aftermath of the **_Lighthouse Evangelism_** controversy was handled.

*   In that case, a video placed on a Christian church website offended Buddhists and Taoists. Pastor received a warning from Internal Security Department. Notably, the matter was addressed not by prosecution before courts, but through public censure, eliciting a public apology to the relevant religious leaders.
    *   Argue authenticity: There is still a risk that an offender is merely apologising and complying with terms merely to avoid prosecution (s 16H(6)) and is not doing so out of a genuine sense of remorse

**_However_**, not all cases should have such leniency. Perhaps, an intransigent insistence on rights should be applied in the most egregious of offences such as **_Benjamin Koh_** where 2 bloggers were prosecuted under **_s 4(2) of the Sedition Act_** for posting anti-Malay and anti-Muslim remarks as well as Amos Yee, where another blogger was prosecuted for burning the Quran.

Arguably, a relational approach is better at rebuilding social trust as it assumes that individuals are not atomistic in nature, but inherently inextricable from a web of social relations, directing attention not only to rights as important legal safeguards, but also as part of a scheme to "restore the constitutional commitment to racial and religious harmony" (**_Thio 2012_**).

# Art 12 – Equality

or(b)any provision or practice restricting office or employment connected with the affairs of any religion, or of an institution managed by a group professing any religion, to persons professing that religion. | Equal protection | 12. — (1)  All persons are equal before the law and entitled to the equal protection of the law.(2)  Except as expressly authorised by this Constitution, there shall be no discrimination against citizens of Singapore on the ground only of religion, race, descent or place of birth in any law or in the appointment to any office or employment under a public authority or in the administration of any law relating to the acquisition, holding or disposition of property or the establishing or carrying on of any trade, business, profession, vocation or employment.(3)  This Article does not invalidate or prohibit —(a)any provision regulating personal law; ## What is the idea of “equality”, and is it a good thing?

As a preliminary, in **_Lim Meng Suang_** \[2015\], the SGCA acknowledged that the very concept of equality is **'thorny'**, because it is desirable in theory, but **struggles with the factual reality** that inequality is an 'inevitable part of daily life'. It is therefore **unclear** how the law is to ensure a basic level of equality applying the principle of equality generally, and to determine which situations such a level of equality should be legally mandated in.

**\[View: Equality Not Relevant\] _Peter Westen_**, “_The Empty Idea of Equality_” (1982) agues that equality is an empty, entirely formal concept with no substantive moral content of its own, that does not aid in analysis of legal problems. 3 main reasons:

*   \[**Relative**\] Firstly, equality is about **relative** **deprivation**, in the sense that the deprivation of something is assessed in relation to something else. Hence, equity is comparative in nature and does not have any substance to itself. Hence to say that “two people are equal and entitled to be treated equally is to say that they both satisfy the criteria of a governing rule of treatment” but yet equality simpliciter says “nothing at all about the content or wisdom of the governing rule”
*   \[**Tautologous**\] Secondly, equality is about treating alike things alike, but defining 'people who are alike' and **'like treatment'** necessarily requires a moral standard, as no two people are really alike. This also results in equality being entirely circular – people who are alike are people who should be treated alike. Without moral standards, 'equality remains meaningless'.
*   \[**Derivative**\] Lastly, equality is **derivative of rights**. Equality analysis logically collapses into rights analysis (see _Carey v Brown_), hence any analysis of legal problems in terms of equality is redundant.

**Possible Counters for Equality:**

1.  Ensures some **degree of consistency** in the concept of “like should be treated as like” and hence places the burden of proof on those wishing to depart from the rule.
2.  By requiring the government to apply laws equally, we can **prevent the arbitrary exercise of discretion** which can render equality meaningless (SoP).
3.  **_Even if_** equity has no clear substantive content, there is formal equality, which can be determined by the **reasonable classification test** (RCT) in the context of Singapore. RCT supplies the governing rule (intelligible differentia + rational relation) that gives equality its content. Albeit the **threshold is low** since no inquiry into substantive content.

## What is the scope of article 12(1)? How courts interpreted the two clauses?

12.—(1) **All persons** are **equal before the law** and entitled to the **equal protection of the law**.

### View 1: No difference per Lim Meng Suang

Notably, the SGCA in **_Lim Meng Suang_** held that the two limbs of Art 12(1) are no more than **declaratory statements of principles** that offer no specific legal criteria to guide the courts in assessing constitutional validity. Consequently, the SGCA held that any statute must nevertheless pass (threshold) the ‘reasonable classification’ test before it can be found consistent with Art 12. This effectively treats both limbs as a unified standard. Even in deference, the SGCA rejected the test of illegitimacy directed at the object of the impugned legislation, in that courts are separate from the legislature; and without mandate to amend laws, **courts cannot usurp legislative functions** or become a “mini-legislatures”.

**_That said_**, a counterargument emerges from **_Tan Seng Kee_**: determining the constitutionality of legislative and executive acts is clearly and exclusively within the judicial purview. Where legislation unduly encroaches upon constitutionally protected rights, courts, as **guardians of fundamental liberties**, should retain the power to scrutinise and strike down such legislation. This reflects a functionalist conception of the **separation of powers**, in which judicial review operates as a meaningful check on the other branches of government.

\[**Extra**\] Indeed, the court's proper function is confined to statutory interpretation and the development of common law and equitable principles. Permitting courts to strike down legislation on the basis of an illegitimate object would, absent clear legal standards, cross that boundary.

### View 2: Chan Sek Keong 🡪 Limb 2 applies if Limb 1 applies.

Further critics on _Lim Meng Suang_’s holding on Art 12(1)’s RC test can be evaluated on **Chan Sek Keong**’s 2019 “_Roads Not Taken_” paper. Chan observes that the RCT was adopted by Singapore courts largely by following Malaysian precedent, which in turn adopted Indian decisions interpreting Art 14 of the Indian Constitution. Art 14 is, however, framed in negative terms, which is structurally different from Art 12(1) of the Singapore Constitution. This genealogy was adopted without adequate consideration of those structural differences.

On this basis, Chan argues that Art 12(1), though declaratory in form, **must be given effect as a substantive right** rather than an aspirational ideal. The RCT, he submits, was designed for the second limb's equal protection inquiry and is ill-suited to give full effect to the first limb's right to equality before the law. The absence of concrete legal principles in Art 12(1) does not render the right unenforceable however; rather, **it reflects a constitutional delegation to the courts** to formulate the applicable standards. This aligns with the functionalist conception of the separation of powers: it is the courts' duty to interpret the Constitution and develop legal principles that give meaningful content to constitutional rights, including as a safeguard against undue encroachment by the legislature and executive.

\[**Extra**\] Chan draws a structural distinction between the two limbs:

*   The **first limb** confers a first-order constitutional right to equality before the law, enjoyed by all persons as against the state.
*   The **second limb** confers a second-order entitlement to equal protection, which is **contingent on the existence of legislation that grants rights or imposes liabilities differentially**. The question it raises is whether the state is justified in enacting a law that treats people unequally, with reference to whether a legitimate state interest is advanced by that differentiation.

## Article 12(1) Case Law History

*   In **_Eng Foong Ho v AG_** \[2009\] 2 SLR(R) 542, the CA dealt with an alleged unconstitutional acquisition of land against a temple.
    *   Argument was that the temple was not accorded equal treatment as other religious institutions located in the same proximity were not acquired by the authorities
    *   As to the equality in result, the question is whether there is a **reasonable nexus between state action and the objective to be achieved by law**. Law is clearly set out in _Ong Ah Chuan_: equality before the law and equal protection of the law require that **like should be compared with like**… Art 12 prohibits laws which require that some individuals within a single class should be treated by way of punishment more harshly than others; it does not forbid discrimination in punitive treatment between one class of individuals and another class in relation to which there is some difference in the circumstances of the offence that has been committed
    *   Court held no discrimination other than in the consequence, and no allegation of arbitrary action on the part of the Government. Acquisition was proceeded with in good faith with grounded rationale.
    *   An executive act may be unconstitutional if it amounts to intentional and arbitrary discrimination
*   \[**Prosecutorial Discretion**\] In **_Ramalingam Ravinthran_**, applicant was charged with drug trafficking for full amount of weight of drugs (death penalty), while partner charged with lesser amount than found and thus no death penalty.
    *   Question was whether the AG’s prosecutorial discretion exercised in a manner that violated article 12?
    *   Prosecutorial discretion permits the **Prosecution to take into account many factors** when bringing the charge, including available evidence, public interest considerations, personal circumstances of offender, offender’s degree of capability etc. there must be no bias on the Prosecution’s part and irrelevant considerations not taken into account
    *   The court held that it was **on the applicant to prove** that the there was a breach on the prosecution’s part in a _prima facie_ abuse of prosecutorial discretion, only then must the Prosecution justify the breach or be held to have been in breach
    *   How does article 12 apply to penal legislation? it was concerned with equal punitive treatment for similar legal guilt. So, while the offenders of a class all fulfil the specified ingredients of the same criminal offence, **there may be substantial variations of moral blameworthiness among offenders**. Just because the legislation does not distinguish based on moral blameworthiness is not reason to render such legislation in breach of art 12(1).
        *   In contrast, Prosecutorial discretion must consider not only legal guilt of offender and his moral blameworthiness, but also the gravity of harm caused to the public welfare, and a myriad of other factors such as sufficiency of evidence, cooperativeness of accused with authorities, whether willingness to testify against co-offenders, or even compassion
    *   That some differentiation has occurred in preferring charges does not necessarily mean that Prosecution has not given unbiased consideration to the offender or offenders in question, or that Prosecution has taken into account irrelevant considerations – _prima facie_ Prosecution has carried out its functions properly.

*   In **_Lim Meng Suang_**, CA reiterated the test for determining the constitutionality of a statute under art 12 is the “reasonable classification” test. It is not engaged if the impugned statute is not discriminatory in the first place. Thus, the first (of a **three-stage inquiry**) stage of the test asks simply if the law is discriminatory. Strictly speaking, the reasonable classification test comprises only two closely related stages.
    *   **Is the law discriminatory?** If it is, it will be good law if:
        *   The classification is founded on an intelligible differentia which distinguishes persons that are grouped together from others left out of the group; and
        *   The differentia has a rational relation to the object sought to be achieved by the law in question. The classification may be founded on different bases such as geographical, or according to objects or occupations and the like. What is necessary is a **nexus between the basis of classification and the object of the law** in question
    *   The court also discussed the theoretical underpinnings of the test:
    *   The “reasonable classification” test does not give effect to any normative or analytical impetus needed to address the question of a potential basic level of equality
        *   The RC test provides a useful and important threshold, without which any attempt to determine if article 12 has been violated cannot occur. If a particular statute cannot even pass legal muster under the test, it means that the statute is so legally illogical and / or incoherent that it would be repugnant to any idea of legal equality to begin with
        *   A rational relation means no need to be complete or perfect, but it does mean that the purpose and object of the statute must be **determined before** the rational relation must be demonstrated
        *   Threshold nature of the test balances the need to accord as much legislative leeway as possible to the Legislature against the need to ensure that laws which are patently illogical and or incoherent do not pass legal muster. Test simultaneously **prevents courts from becoming “mini-legislatures”**
    *   **Test is not purely procedural**, comprises substantive elements. Court does recognize that the RC test in itself does not really aid the court in ascertaining whether or not the concept of equality under Art 12(1) has been violated
    *   **CA also rejected another potential test of illegitimacy** – certain types of law are not the primary remit of the judiciary (_ie_ statute law), thus the courts must be circumspect. If courts have power to declare a statute unconstitutional because the object of that statute is illegitimate would precisely be to confer on the court a license **to usurp the legislative function** in the course of becoming a “mini-legislature”
        *   To argue that the court has the duty to declare a statute unconstitutional if its object is illegitimate does not explain how the court can find the statute illegitimate to begin with – it would amount to receiving by judicial backdoor what they cannot obtain by legislative front door
        *   However, if there are serious flaws with regard to intelligibility of the differentia embodied in a statute, or clear disconnect between that differentia and purpose and object of the statute, the court will hold that the statute does not pass legal muster under article 12(1) – a limited element of legitimacy is embodied in the RC test, but court reiterated that it is **not** an additional test
    *   **_Wednesbury_ standard of unreasonableness was also not endorsed** in the context of a challenge to the constitutionality of a statute
    *   As to the two limbs of article 12 – equal before the law and equal protection of the law – the court viewed the entirety of 12(1) as declaratory and aspirational in nature, and thus both limbs are covered by the RC test
    *   As for presumption of constitutionality, the presumption does not operate as strongly to pre-Independence laws as it would compared to those promulgated in the context of an elected legislature which would have fully considered all views before enacting the laws concerned.
*   In **_Tan Seng Kee_**, the CA made a number of determinations as well as the constitutionality of s 377A.
    *   **Parliament’s actions not presumptively constitutional**, and doctrine of separation of powers is premised on the notion that the 3 branches of government are co-equal – deference to other branches where constitutionality is being challenged will be contrary to the doctrine and portend the institutional irrelevance of the judiciary
    *   In _obiter_, the constitutionality of s 377A was considered. Court considered there is no presumption that every differentiating measure enacted bears a rational relation to the object sought to be achieved; it would be relying on a presumption of constitutionality to meet an objection of unconstitutionality and essentially presume the very issue being challenged
    *   The _Lim Meng Suang_ approach has been in application ever since – the RC test. But **one version emerged in _Syed Suhail_, the difference in the two being**:
        *   Whether at the first limb of RC test, the court can consider the reasonableness of the differentia embodied in the statutory provision concerned
            *   Under _Lim Meng Suang_ at first limb, whether a differentia is “so unreasonable as to be illogical and/or incoherent” goes beyond whether that differentia is capable of being understood or apprehended by the intellect or understanding
            *   Under **_Syed Suhail_ in contrast**, the first limb of the “reasonable classification” test is never concerned with the reasonableness (or lack thereof) of the differentia in question, even in cases where the differentia is extremely unreasonable. Instead, the first limb of the “reasonable classification” test is only concerned with identifying the purported criterion for the differential treatment in question. It is only at the second limb of the test that the court considers “whether the differential treatment \[is\] reasonable”
    *   Under RC test, **no need for perfect relation or complete coincidence** between differentia embodied in the legislative object of statutory provision. But _Syed Suhail_ approach emphasises that while that relationship need not be perfect, it also must not be so tenuous as to be incapable of withstanding scrutiny
    *   **Court did not determine which test is preferable, however**
*   In **_Syed Suhail v AG_**, where death row inmate (applicant) sought leave on President’s decision, acting on advice from Ministry of Home Affairs, to schedule his execution earlier than another inmate sentenced before him, alleging unconstitutional invoking Art 12(1) equality since they were “equally situated” (both on death row, both denied clemency on the same day).
    *   Held: No arbitrary or irrational discrimination. The order of executions is not strictly determined by sentencing date. The State may consider multiple operational and legal factors, such as:
        *   Status of appeals or pending proceedings
        *   Clemency petitions
        *   Logistical and administrative considerations
*   In **_Xu Yuan Chen v AG_**, where AG sought leave order for Terry Xu (TOC editor) for contempt of court in posting FB post alleging judicial misconduct – risk of undermining public confidence.
    *   AG sought committal against Xu but not O'Connor (the author, resident overseas). Xu alleged breach of Art 12(1). Issue: **Were Xu and O'Connor "equally situated"?** Holding: SGCA dismissed appeal.
    *   Applying two-step **_Syed Suhail_** test, court found Xu and O'Connor were NOT equally situated because: **(1) difficulty in investigation/prosecution/enforcement** against O'Connor (overseas, outside jurisdiction**); (2) degree of harm** (Xu's TOC had far greater reach and credibility). Only ONE material differentiating factor was needed to defeat the Art 12 claim.
    *   Key principle: The "equally situated" requirement is a significant threshold. Courts are not quick to find persons equally situated where relevant contextual differences exist.
*   In **_Saravanan Chandran v PP_** \[2020\], where the applicant who was slapped with dual trafficking charges of cannabis and cannabis mixture – disputing sentencing range since cannabis mixture else unequal treatment.
    *   Held: No issue: Sentencing calibrated on gross weight of cannabis mixture was objectively an intelligible differentia. Purpose of MDA is to "_prevent and deter the distribution and consumption of illicit drugs_". As such, gross weight of the mixture is a **reliable independent indicia of the harm done** through the illicit import, export and trafficking, in terms of its gross weight it is still proportionate to the harm done to society. Prof Amirthalingam on risk of **diff % of mixture to harm rejected**.
    *   Also, realistically, the cannabis mixture cannot distinguished and quantified.

## Reasonable Classification Test: 2 Competing Approaches (Marcus Teo)

**Given that the SGCA in Tan Seng Kee at \[329\] left it open as to which approach ought to be preferred, it is crucial that we determine how the approaches in RCT.**

In **Marcus Teo** “_Refining Reasonable Classification_” \[2023\], the author sets out 3 aims: (1) courts must identify legislative purposes only from extrinsic materials when applying the test, to avoid circularity in its application; (2) when assessing the relation between differentiation and purpose, courts must require proof of the existence, and sometimes the sufficiency, of practical reasons that excuse imperfect differentiation and; (3) applicants should only bear the burden of showing that laws or decisions imperfectly differentiate, before the burden shifts to the Government to justify them. Importantly, author seeks to explain how the RCT should be structured to apply to both legislative and executive acts.

Author notes two approaches to the RCT, the version in _Taw Cheng Kong_ and _Lim Meng Suang_, and the version in _Syed Suhail_ and _Tan Seng Kee_. The two approaches differ on three issues: (i) how purposes should be identified at the **Purpose Identification Stage**; (ii) how the relationship between purpose and differentiation should be assessed at the **Relation Evaluation Stage**; and (iii) **where the burden of proof should lie:**

| LMS:Requires the classification prescribed by the statute concerned to be founded on an intelligible differentia;Requires a rational relation between that differentia and the object sought to be achieved by the statute.Syed:Court must assess applicant’s submission on whether differently treated individuals were "equally situated such that any differential treatment required justification". Here, the applicant bore the "evidential burden" of proving equal situation and differential treatment.Court would consider whether the differential treatment was "reasonable in that it was based on legitimate reasons", like whether it had "a sufficient rational relation to the object for which the power was conferred" and was not based on "irrelevant considerations". |
| --- |

### Limb 1: Purpose Identification stage (Differentia)

*   **Under _LMS_**: the validity of differentia may be called into question **in extreme cases** where the differentia is "so unreasonable as to be illogical and incoherent".
    *   This requirement was “a **relatively low threshold** that ought to avoid any consideration of substantive moral, political and/or ethical issues because these issues are potentially controversial” (_Lim Meng Suang)._
    *   **_Issue_**: **Chan**, “_Equal Justice and the Prosecutorial Power_” (2022) differentia tells us nothing about the purpose or object of an Act since it engages a circular reasoning, rendering the RCT redundant. **_Tan Seng Kee_** also held that this goes beyond whether the differentia is capable of being understood.
*   **Cf. _Syed_**: the first limb (differentia) is never concerned with reasonableness of the differentia, even in cases where the differentia is extremely unreasonable.
    *   Crucially, the courts are only concerned with **identifying the purported criterion** of the differential treatment in question. Essentially, restrictions exist on 'generality' of framing.
    *   However, it is crucial for the RCT to sieve out laws where the objectives of the legislation are themselves discriminatory (**Kevin Tan**_, Constitutionalism in Asia 2014_). Arguably, this better ensures that a purposive approach is engaged through extrinsic materials (not from the text of the provision itself, to avoid circularity).
*   **E.g.** Law banning all women from driving might fail first limb of Lim Meng Suang RCT as its differentia could be said to be patently illogical or incoherent that no reasonable person would contemplate it as being functional as an intelligible differentia, **_BUT_** pass muster under Syed's first limb, fails under Syed's second limb as the differentia bears no rational relation to any conceivable object of that law although.

### Limb 2: Relation Evaluation stage (Rational Relation/Nexus)

**Degree of “relationship”** required between object and differentia.

*   Under **_LMS_**: Courts only require a **loose connection** between the challenged law and its purpose. Essentially, a perfect relation or complete incidence is not required (**_LMS_**). Per SGCA obiter of **_Tan Seng Kee_**, Yong CJ reasoned that even if s 37(1) was “underinclusive” for excluding non-citizens, it “was not fatal” to the Section’s constitutionality because it would still “_go some way in capturing the corrupt acts of citizens abroad_”.
*   **Cf. _Syed_**: the relationship **must not be 'so tenuous as to be incapable of withstanding scrutiny**.' Essentially, Syed’s approach seems more grounded in that when life and liberty are at stake, the courts would _search in its scrutiny_ if the differentia treatment was “reasonable in that it was based on **legitimate reason**” in extrinsic materials. E.g. If s 377A's purpose was to disapprove homosexual conduct in general, it could fall afoul of the RCT for being under-inclusive and if the section was targeted specifically at male prostitution only, it might be over-inclusive and thus unconstitutional under Art 12. Against the **_LMS_** approach however, the loose connection in s377A’s nexus would be framed generally to “safeguarding societal morality”, retaining its constitutionality.
*   **_In my opinion_**, beyond engaging in the constitutional objectives of the section, this better accords with the functional conception of SoP, where the emphasis is on the constitutional duty of the judiciary as guardians of fundamental liberties, and as counter-majoritarian checks on other branches of government to ensure their compliance with the constitution and not unduly infringing on individual’s rights.

However, **framing the object** of the section is a concern. If one were to articulate the legislative object of a statutory provision in whatever terms would support one’s desired interpretation of the provision, then the RC test would be reduced to nothing more than an **exercise in legal formalism** (_Tan Seng Kee_).

*   **_Thus_**, compared to LMS’s “mere threshold” test articulated, Syed’s scrutiny approach is better in its sensitivity to each case’s context and hence avoids the “austerity of tabulated legalism” per _Ong Ah Chuan_. It may also be important to **calibrate legislations on updated social attitude considerations** before adopting an originalist stance on a provision’s construction (**Yap**, “_Back to 1938_”).

### Issue: Presumption of Constitutionality + Burden of Proof

*   Under **_LMS_**: There is a “strong presumption of constitutionality” such that the court will not find a statute or provision unconstitutional under Art 12(1) and can only be rebutted by “the person challenging the law” on material or factual evidence to show the legislation was enacted or operated arbitrarily (_Tan Seng Kee_). Arguably, this approach tips in the favour of the government and disadvantages the applicant’s case in placing them with a high BoP (**Jack Lee**, “_Rethinking the Presumption of Constitutionality_”). Further, it doesn’t help that Singapore doesn’t have freedom of information statues (Cf. UK) such that the government does not need to adduce justifications for their actions (**Neo**, “_Separation of Power_”) – which doesn’t emit the legal limits of powers between the branches.
*   In **_Syed_**: presumption of constitutionality was watered down, becoming not a high legal standard but merely the legal burden of proof which can be said to be more pro-applicant. In the first limb, the applicant would have to satisfy the court in adducing materials beyond bare assertions, that there is **_a prima facie_** case of reasonable suspicion in favour of granting remedies (**_Xu Yuan Chen_**). If this first limb is discharged, the burden will shift to the AG/Prosecution to justify the differential treatment by showing reasonable and legitimate reasons in bearing a sufficient relational relation to the object for which power was conferred (_Ramalingam_). Compared to _LMS_, Syed’s approach would be a **more substantive inquiry** that promotes executive accountability, and gels with the idea that “all power has legal limits” (**_Chng Suan Tze_**).

## Protection of minority interests

What is the best way to protect minority interests?

*   The **Wee Commission Report 1966** published its recommendations on this issue:
    *   In a democratic society, it is futile, impractical and dangerous for minorities to find security in so-called minority rights, as that simply means rights different from those or additional to those enjoyed by the majority
    *   Apart from the futility of trying to secure for themselves minority rights, there is also the danger in minorities seeking to buy security in terms of minority rights and what are often termed "special rights" and preferential treatment for themselves. Because once you start defining "minority rights" as special rights or preferential treatment, then you are **breaching an essential principle of democracy**, which is the principle of equal rights
        *   Greatest danger to the minority is their attempts to secure minority rights, because this will trigger a reactionary response by the majority to the same – leads to a higher likelihood of majority tyranny as a result
        *   What the minority should strive for is to ensure always that no one citizen, regardless of his race or colour or creed, enjoys more rights than himself. If that kind of constitution can be drawn up, the minority is always safeguarded. The real safeguard is equality always and not special rights written into the constitution
    *   In the 1967 _Singapore Parliamentary Debates_ on the Report of the Constitutional Commission, S Rajaratnam reiterated the above point: the essence of the matter is that, as the Commission sees it, **in a democracy there can be no distinction between majority rights and minority rights**. Once you tackle this as a constitutional problem, there is no getting away from the fact that there cannot be majority and minority rights. There can only be equality of rights, the same rights for all

### Group Representation Constituencies (GRCs) (Art 39A)

*   **Constitutionality (obj.):** Per DPM Goh Chok Tong (1988 Hansard): To ensure multiracial Parliament; compel political parties to adopt non-communal approaches; guard against 'tyranny of the majority.'
*   **Effectiveness**: The GRC scheme has ensured minority representation in every Parliament.
*   **Issues**: **_However_**, critics note: (a) makes elections more difficult for opposition parties; (b) may reduce authenticity of minority representation — minority candidates are 'carried' by anchor-person MPs; (c) increase in GRC size (up to 6 members) has amplified these concerns.

### HDB Ethnic Integration Programme (EIP)

*   EIP prevents ethnic enclaves from forming in HDB estates by setting racial quotas on flat ownership. This limits buying/selling of flats based on ethnic proportions.
*   **Issue**: Should EIP prima facie be unconstitutional since it engages Art 12(2)'s prohibition on discrimination on grounds of race in the 'acquisition, holding or disposition of property.'
*   **Arguments for constitutionality**:
    *   **_Article 152(1)_** (_“constantly care for the interests of racial and religious minorities_”): An express constitutional mandate that allows (or requires) race-conscious policies in some context.
    *   “_Except as_ **_expressly authorised_** _by this Constitution_” qualifier in **_Art 12(2)_** — Art 152(1)'s mandate could be argued as constitutional authorization;
    *   **_Art 12(1) RCT_** using Syed’s approach:
        *   Limb 1: Equal situation (all are public market participants), and Differential treatment (race-based quotas)
        *   Limb 2: **But…** The objective of the EIP is to: **(1)** Prevent racial enclaves, and **(2)** Promote racial integration and social cohesion.
            *   **And not arbitrary**: EIP applies SYMMETRICALLY — quotas apply to Chinese, Malay, and Indian/Other communities; no single group is systematically burdened.

### Reserved Presidential Elections (Art 19B)

*   In **_Ravi v AG_** (HC), the court dealt with the challenge that institutional safeguards against discrimination are in violation of article 12(1), namely the reserved election for the President
    *   Article 19B of the Constitution seeks **to ensure multiracial representation** and therefore could not be said to be racially discriminatory. However it was argued in Ravi v AR that Art 19B’s effectiveness was limited — the 2017 election was reserved for the Malay community and **Halimah Yacob ran uncontested**, **undermining democratic legitimacy**.
        *   In any event, the Constitution was not race-neutral and the point had been specifically considered by the Presidential Council for Minority Rights (“PCMR”). In addition, Art 152 of the Constitution expressly enjoined the Government to care for the interests of racial and religious minorities
    *   Held: Court agreed with PCMR; had scrutinised the “differentiating measure” in article 19B and deemed that it would not if enacted be a differentiating measure as defined under article 68 of the Constitution
        *   As the reserved elections framework applied equally to the three racial communities, it was fatal to the plaintiff’s case – same hiatus applies to all three racial communities **without exception**
    *   PCMR also stated that the **purpose of reserved elections is also not discriminatory**. It is to ensure that the office of President will be representative of our multiracial society and to foster multiracialism
        *   The reserved election is a long-stop measure that arises only after a 5-term hiatus, and is appropriately tailored to meet its purpose in a minimally intrusive way

# Art 9 and 149 Liberty

**_To start_**, Art 9(1)’s simplicity conceals two distinct but related inquiries: first, the **scope inquiry** — how broadly do the terms "life," "personal liberty," and "law" extend; and second, the **validity inquiry** — whether the courts' chosen interpretive method is constitutionally sound. These inquiries are not separable in practice. The interpretive stance a court adopts on the meaning of "law" directly determines the effective width of the constitutional guarantee, and the reasoning deployed to justify that stance engages foundational constitutional concepts: separation of powers, the rule of law, and constitutional supremacy under Art 4.

| Interpretation2.—(1)  In this Constitution, unless it is otherwise provided or the context otherwise requires —“law” includes written law and any legislation of the United Kingdom or other enactment or instrument whatsoever which is in operation in Singapore and the common law in so far as it is in operation in Singapore and any custom or usage having the force of law in Singapore;Liberty of the person9.—(1)  No person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty save in accordancewith law.(2)  Where a complaint is made to the General Division of the High Court or any Judge sitting in the General Division of the High Court that a person is being unlawfully detained, the General Division of the High Court shall inquire into the complaint and, unless satisfied that the detention is lawful, shall order him to be produced before the General Division of the High Court and release him. [38/2019](3)  Where a person is arrested, he shall be informed as soon as may be of the grounds of his arrest and shall be allowed to consult and be defended by a legal practitioner of his choice.(4)  Where a person is arrested and not released, he shall, without unreasonable delay, and in any case within 48 hours (excluding the time of any necessary journey), be produced before a Magistrate, in person or by way of video-conferencing link (or other similar technology) in accordance with law, and shall not be further detained in custody without the Magistrate’s authority.
