## Interpretation of Art 9

### “Life or personal liberty”

\[**Malaysia**\] CA in **_Tan Tek Seng_** \[1996\] 1 MLJ 261

*   Considered the history of Article 9, tracing it through the Indian Constitution and US Constitution for the definition of “life”
    *   US viewed “life” as something more than mere animal existence, with the inhibition against its deprivation extending to all limbs and faculties by which life is enjoyed
        *   The MY courts held to keep in tandem with the national ethos (“_keep with modern_ _and progressive change_”) to **adopt a “broad and liberal” approach** to “life” that implements the true intention of the framers of the Constitution.

\[**India**\] Other jurisdictions have recognised a **more extensive** definition of “life”

*   In India, the right to life includes to the right to live with human dignity free from exploitation (**_Bandhua Mukti Morcha_**)

\[**Singapore**\] In Singapore however, the courts take a **less liberal** position, with life and liberty confined to the freedom from unlawful physical detention or incarceration as held in **_Tan Eng Hong_** and affirmed in **_Tan Seng Kee_**. It does not extend to a substantive right to a dignified quality of life (**_Yong Vui Kong_**). Arguably, this limitation can foreclose the constitutionalism of socio-economic interests, labour rights as well as the right to live with dignity expounded by India’s Supreme Court in **_Maneka v Union of India_**.

**_That said_**, the **justification offered by our courts is structural**. The Singapore constitution contains no equivalent to the Indian Directive Principles of State Policy, which provided the Indian courts with the surrounding constitutional architecture for a more expansive reading of “life”. Hence, if our courts import such expansion without a supporting framework, it would be methodically unsound. By referring to the court’s development below, clarity is sought on Singapore’s interpretation of “law”.

*   In **_Lo Pui Sang_**, HC held that personal liberty under Art 9 **did not incorporate** the personal liberty to contract, but only to the personal liberty of the person against unlawful incarceration or detention
    *   **Facts**: Mr. Rajah argued ss 84A(1) and B(1) of **_Land Titles (Strata) Act_** (LTSA) had the effect of depriving the appellant of their personal liberty to contract.
    *   Constitution expressly permits deprivation of personal liberty if in accordance with law passed by Parliament
    *   CA did not deal with constitutional issues
    *   But court appears to endorse that “in accordance with law” is any law that is passed by Parliament (**very positivist**)
*   In **_Tan Eng Hong v AG_**, **rejected** defendant’s argument that protection accorded by Art 9 must extend to all faculties which life is enjoyed, including privacy, human dignity, individual autonomy and human need for an intimate personal sphere.
    *   **Facts**: Pf and another male person engaged in oral sex in toilet cubicle inside Citylink Mall and were charged under **_s 377A_** which criminalized acts of “gross indecency” between males.
    *   HC observed that the jurisprudence here **avoided wide interpretations** as such, **reaffirmed _Lo Pui Sang_** (HC) on the constitutional issues: _ie_ personal liberty only relates to unlawful incarceration or detention.
    *   \[**Right not to be detained under an unconstitutional law**\] Under **_s 377A_**, mere existence of provision does not violate art 9(1) rights. However, the court held that the existence of an allegedly unconstitutional **_s 377A_** may violate both **_Art 12(1)_** (equality before the law and equal protection of the law) and **_Art 9(1)_** (right to personal liberty); even if there is no subsisting prosecution.
*   In **_Lim Meng Suang v AG_**, the CA once again held that the right to privacy and personal autonomy ought **not be read** into the phrase “life or personal liberty” in article 9(1)
    *   **Facts**: Appellant argued s 377A violated Art 9(1) when given purposive interpretation to allow a person to enjoy affection and love towards another human being.
    *   _Art 9(1)_ phrase “life” should be interpreted narrowly in for 3 reasons:
        *   **Jurisprudence**: The appellant’s interpretation would be contrary to Singapore’s established jurisprudence, in affirming Lo Pui Sang and Tan Eng Hong that it only relates to unlawful incarceration or detention.
        *   **Purposive Approach**: According to Art 9’s “context and structure”. Art 9(2) to Art 9(4) provides various procedural safeguards in relation to the arrest and detention of persons. Hence, the established narrow reading of Art 9 is grounded.
            *   **_However_**, this is arguably inconsistent with the sui generis approach propounded by Ong Ah Chuan that fundamental liberties should be given a generous interpretation to give individuals the full measure of rights guaranteed in our constitution. A counter to _Ong Ah Chuan_’s position is that **generosity** **should not be a licence to ignore text, structure, and history**.
        *   **Legislative History – Originalist Approach**: Protection against unlawful detention is the "particular focus" of Art 21 of the Indian Constitution, from which our Art 9 is derived. The framers of India's Art 21 **consciously rejected the wider US formulation** "without due process of law" and enacted provisions similar to SG's Art 9(3) and Art 9(4) to protect India's citizenry against unlawful detention. India's Art 21 was the basis for "liberty of the person" provision in Art 5(1) of the Malaysian Constitution which was later adopted in Singapore.
            *   **_In consideration_** of other jurisdiction’s more expansive approach to “life and personal liberty”, the SGCA held that the courts should be cautious to adopt foreign cases without accessing their “**unique social, political and legal circumstances**”. While these cases may illuminate possible interpretations, they may not align with Singapore’s constitutional text, structure, and institutional context. If want change, ask if interpretation stems from:
                *   Social need? (poverty, inequality, and weak state capacity)
                *   Political history? (executive overreach)
                *   Legal text? (broad clauses like “due process”)
*   In **_Yong Vui Kong v PP_** \[2015\] (Caning: Unlawful use of force) – While the SGCA adopted a slightly wider interpretation of Art 9 cf. earlier cases, it was interpreting “law” in the context of punishments (caning) of convicted persons.
    *   **Facts**: YVK was sentenced under s 33B of _Misue of Drugs Act_ (MDA), to the mandatory minimum of life imprisonment and 15 strokes of the cane.
    *   **Contested**:
        *   Canning constituted a **form of torture** and torture was **contrary to Art 9**;
        *   Canning was **so irrational and arbitrary** that it could not be “law”
        *   Canning **discriminated against men under 50** since punishment could only be inflicted for <50 and not women, and thereby foul of Art 12.
    *   For Art 9(1) to be engaged, caning must involve a deprivation of “life or personal liberty”
    *   **Object and purpose of Art 9(1)** is to ensure that Govt acts in accordance with valid laws when depriving a person of his life or personal liberty. In _dicta_, court traced history of Art 9 to the **_Magna Carta_**, which encompassed more than just the right not to be unlawfully incarcerated, and also protected a person from unlawful seizure of his property and the unlawful use of force against him.
    *   **The lineage of article 9(1) makes clear**:
        *   (a) to the extent specific rights are dealt with elsewhere, for instance the prohibition of forced slavery and labour in **_Art 10_**, or of banishment and curtailment of freedom of movement in **_Art 13_**, these would not be included within the ambit of the protection conferred by Art 9;
        *   (b) to the extent potential rights were considered and excluded, for instance the right to property, these also would not be included in Art 9;
        *   (c) beyond this, Art 9 does not protect only against arbitrary execution or incarceration. It also **prohibits the unlawful use of force against a person**, including by way of amputations, mutilations, assaults, beatings, woundings, etc. Such acts would result in a deprivation of “life”, according to Blackstone’s definition.
    *   Thus, punishments like caning must be impermissible **unless done “in accordance with law”** – Art 9(1) will be engaged by the execution (or proposed execution) of a sentence of caning on the Appellant, and in order to pass constitutional muster it has to be “in accordance with law”
    *   **TLDR**: caning is not covered by the common law conception of torture. Even if caning does constitute torture under international law, **domestic statutes would take precedence** over any international law norms in the event of any inconsistency between the two. Since caning is **mandated by statute** and there is nothing to invalidate it, the challenge under Art 9(1) must fail.
*   The latest judgment in **_Tan Seng Kee_** makes clear:
    *   **Unenumerated substantive rights cannot be read into the Constitution** as it would entail judges acting as super-legislature
    *   Thus, in context of **_s 377A_**, the right to express one’s sexual identity is evidently not an express constitutional right. Nor is it a right that can be found in the Constitution. Therefore, impermissible to construe the unenumerated right to express one’s sexual identity as a right that attracts constitutional protection under Art 9(1)

### “In accordance with law”

As a preliminary, an **inconsistent approach** has been adopted in Singapore regarding the interpretation of “in accordance with law” in Art 9(1). However, more recent decisions reflect a _shift towards a more expansive understanding_ that beyond law validly passed by Parliament. It excludes laws that are unconstitutional, arbitrary, absurd or against RoL, so as to incorporate at the very least compliance with **procedural** **fundamental rules of natural justice** (FRNJ) which was built into Westminster-based constitutions. Below is the trajectory of Singapore jurisprudence — from the narrow positivism of **_Jabar_** and **_Lo Pui Sang_**, through the structured expansionism of **_Ong Ah Chuan_**, to the synthesized framework of **_Tan Seng Kee_** — which reflects a gradual but incomplete reckoning with what Art 4 demands

**Narrow approach chosen by _Jabar_ and _Lo Pui Sang_:**

*   The narrowest position was articulated by the SGCA in **_Jabar v PP_**. Yong CJ held that "any law which provides for the deprivation of a person's life or personal liberty is valid and binding so long as it is validly passed by Parliament. The **court is not concerned with whether it is also fair, just and reasonable**." On this reading, the constitutional guarantee is essentially exhausted by a demand for formal legislative compliance. If Parliament has enacted a statute in accordance with the required procedure, that statute constitutes "law" for the purposes of Art 9(1) and cannot be impugned on grounds of substantive unfairness. A similar textualist logic animated **_Lo Pui Sang_**, where the SGHC held that "law" means "law passed by Parliament," reasoning that to hold otherwise would permit the court to alter the clear wording of the provision.
*   **The Problem with the Narrow Approach**
    *   The narrow approach cannot survive scrutiny against Art 4, and its critics are right to press this point. If "law" in Art 9(1) means nothing more than a validly enacted statute, then the constitutional guarantee is rendered self-defeating. Art 4 provides that the Constitution is the supreme law of Singapore, and any legislation inconsistent with it is void. But if Parliament is free to enact any statute depriving persons of life and liberty provided the correct procedural steps are followed, then Art 9(1) adds nothing to the ordinary legislative process. The protection of fundamental liberties would be, as **Lord Diplock warned in Ong Ah Chuan, "little better than a mockery.**"
    *   Moreover, this narrow approach is incoherent in light of the court's own role under the constitution. As the SGCA noted in **_Tan Seet Eng_**, the judiciary bears responsibility for "the adjudication of controversies which carries with it the power to pronounce authoritatively and conclusively on the meaning of the Constitution." If the court refuses to assess the content of "law" against constitutional standards, it is not deferring to Parliament — **it is abdicating its constitutional function**. The proper role of separation of powers is not to render the court helpless before legislative action, but to define the appropriate mode and intensity of judicial review.
    *   The SGCA in **_Yong Vui Kong_** subsequently clarified that **_Jabar_** must not be read as a definitive statement about the meaning of "law" in Art 9(1) _otherwise_ it may become a high watermark of a positivist approach in its reading (**_Treatise_**). The facts of Jabar concerned the constitutionality of an **_administrative delay_** in executing the mandatory death penalty — a challenge directed at executive action, **not at the legislation itself**. The question of whether the relevant MDP legislation was "fair, just and reasonable" was therefore irrelevant to the particular issue before the court. Read in this way, _Jabar_ is entirely consistent with _Ong Ah Chuan_: it resolved a specific challenge to executive action without making any general claim about the meaning of "law." _Lo Pui Sang_ similarly involved a narrow challenge to deprivation of contractual rights, not an attempt to impugn legislation for substantive unfairness. Neither case should bear the weight of a general positivist constitutional theory.

**Expansive approach chosen by Ong Ah Chuan to recent Tan Seng Kee**

*   One pivotal turn started with the UKPC In Ong Ah Chuan, which lies the foundational authority for an expansive approach. The PC **rejected** the narrow contention that "law" in Art 9(1) means any legislative rule validly enacted by Parliament. Lord Diplock held that the Singapore Constitution — like others based on the Westminster model and drafted by lawyers steeped in its conventions — used "law" in certain contexts to refer to something more than a collection of rules. It denoted a system of law **incorporating "those fundamental rules of natural justice** that had formed part and parcel of the common law of England that was in operation in Singapore" at the commencement of the Constitution. This conclusion flowed from the sui generis interpretive approach: constitutional instruments call for "principles of interpretation of their own" suitable to their character, and fundamental liberties must receive a **generous interpretation** to grant individuals their "full measure" of rights.
*   However, the PC was careful, not to convert Art 9(1) into an open-ended proportionality review of legislative policy (Appellant argued MDP arbitrary: removes judicial discretion to calibrate punishment to individual culpability). The PC expressly declining to determine that the mandatory death penalty was constitutionally prohibited; the question should be whether capital punishment were "questions of social policy solely for the Legislature," and they held MDP was "nothing unusual" in a mandatory capital sentence. This caveat is significant — even as the PC expanded the meaning of "law" to include fundamental rules of natural justice (FRNJ), it **preserved legislative autonomy over the content of penal policy** by confining FRNJ to procedural fairness in criminal process, not to substantive policy choices about punishment. This was later affirmed by the SGCA in **_Yong Vui Kong_**, such that context that the constitutionality of carrying out the MDP **does not entail inquiries of “fair, just and reasonable”.**
*   At this point, it is suggested that the PC’s relativist approach in **_Haw Tua Tau_** in considering transnational materials such as comparative and international laws would allow the courts to evolve beyond the limited frozen 1963 FRNJ. However, reading such contemporary laws before the government gazettes it would risk judges acting as unelected super-legislators.
*   **The Synthesized Framework**: SGCA **_Tan Seng Kee._** The most authoritative and comprehensive statement of the current law is found in Tan Seng Kee, which synthesized the preceding authorities into a four-requirement framework. "Law" in Art 9(1) goes beyond formal validity and requires that:
    1.  The statute must **comply with fundamental rules of natural justice**, understood as **procedural rights aimed at securing a fair trial**.
    2.  The statute **cannot be colourable legislation** — legislation in form but in substance a **legislative judgment directed at securing the conviction** of particular known individuals (citing _Liyanage v The Queen_).
    3.  The statute **cannot be absurd or arbitrary** — though this is also procedural in character; an "absurd" law is one that cannot be understood or complied with, **making it impossible to mount a defence**, rather than one that is merely policy-wise unreasonable.
    4.  The statute **cannot be contrary to the rule of law**.
*   **_Notably_, (1), (2) and (3) are cast in procedural, not substantive character**:
    *   A test of substantive reasonableness "could require the court to intrude into the legislative sphere and engage in policy making." This formulation responds to a separation of powers concern: if courts assess whether legislation is substantively fair or reasonable, they are effectively performing a function that belongs to Parliament — evaluating the wisdom and merits of legislative choices.
    *   However, there is a risk in (3) where the courts are effectively exercising wide discretion with the vagueness of “absurdity” of statutes; which may be contrary against certainty in the rule of law. **_It is recommended_** that (3) be constitutionally grounded through the “searching scrutiny” approach in _Syed Suhail_ already established in SG.
    *   It is also noted that the SGCA left (2) underdefined to leave possibilities for expansive developments in future cases such as proportionality, substantial fairness seen in **Thio**’s _Lex Rex or Rex Lex_.
    *   **Essentially**, the SGCA created a middle ground in its Art 9(1) approach; it is not asking whether the law is good policy but whether it respects the constitutional architecture within which legislative power is exercised.

### Case History (Skip 1)

*   In **_Ong Ah Chuan_**, the PC made clear several fundamental principles to interpreting a Westminster Constitution
    *   A s_ui generis_ approach to interpretation is necessary to interpreting a constitution, suitable to its character without necessary acceptance of all presumptions relevant to legislation of private law
    *   PC advised a **generous interpretation** of the Constitution to accord individuals the full measure of the fundamental liberties referred to
    *   PC did not accept the narrow view of the effects of art 9(1) and 12(1) – “in accordance with law” refer to a system of law which incorporates those fundamental rules of natural justice that had formed part and parcel of the common law of England that was in operation in Singapore at the commencement of the Constitution … which ensures better protection for the individual in the enjoyment of his fundamental liberties
    *   On MDP, judges did not view it to be unconstitutional, not unusual in a capital sentence being mandatory
*   In **_Nguyen Tuong Van_**, the court held that the MDP prescribed under the MDA is constitutional as sufficiently discriminating to obviate any inhumanity in its operation
    *   The appellant made two arguments — (1) MDP amounted to arbitrary punishment for 2 reasons: (a) flouting equal protection guarantee in art 12(1); and (b) precluding proportional and individualized sentencing, which was protected by the prohibition against cruel and inhuman treatment or punishment; and (2) even if MDP not arbitrary, execution by hanging amounted to cruel and inhuman treatment or punishment
    *   On arbitrary punishment, the court reaffirmed that “in accordance with law” is more than positive law, but the version as stated in _Ong Ah Chuan_ above.
        *   While there have been PC decisions that ruled on the unconstitutionality of MDP from other jurisdictions, those decisions were decided in light of the various international norms that had been accepted by their jurisdictions, in line with their Constitutions. Thus, it was due to the international obligations that the jurisdictions had accepted that led the PC to rule that MDP was part of the prohibition against cruel and inhuman treatment
        *   **In Singapore, the common law has been developed by the Judiciary. Additionally, under the Constitution, Parliament as the duly elected Legislature enacts the laws in accordance and consistent with the Constitution of Singapore. If there is any repugnancy between any legislation and the Constitution, the legislation shall be declared by the Judiciary to be invalid to the extent of the repugnancy.** Any customary international law rule must be clearly and firmly established before its adoption by the courts – as Singapore has not ratified the convention against cruel and inhuman treatment, the law was thus constitutional
    *   Elaborating on CIL point, the court held that the appellant must show two elements for CIL to apply:
        *   The prohibition against cruel and inhuman treatment or punishment amounts to a customary international rule
        *   That a specific prohibition against hanging as a mode of execution is part of the content of that rule in customary international law
    *   In this case, court adopted a numerical analysis, looking at the number of countries that still practice death penalty, thus concluding that they were not a customary international rule
        *   Additionally, the court also held that even if the CIL could be found, the domestic statute if present would prevail in the event of inconsistency
        *   Thus for CIL to apply in our dualist system, the general rule is that it applies _ergo omnes_ (to all generally). Once identified, it comes into Singapore at the same level as the Common Law. It can be rejected by explicit statute with opposing law
*   In **_Yong Vui Kong_** \[2010\] (Mandatory Death Penalty), the court dealt with a number of developments since _Nguyen_
    *   On the apparent departure in **_Jabar v PP_** from _Nguyen_ and _Ong Ah Chuan_ when Yong CJ held “Any law which provides for the deprivation of a person's life or personal liberty ... is valid and binding so long as it is validly passed by Parliament. The court is not concerned with whether it is also fair, just and reasonable as well”, the CA explained that it was in the context that the constitutionality of carrying out the MDP does not entail inquiries of “fair, just and reasonable”, and not constitutionality of MDP per se.
    *   As for whether the MDP was inhuman punishment, appellants argued that as a matter of interpretation, art 9(1) should be read as incorporating some form of prohibition against inhuman punishment similar to the other jurisdictions
        *   Court in response reiterated the arguments per _Nguyen_, and held that domestic law should be interpreted consistently with Singapore’s international legal obligations. Development in human rights jurisprudence as manifested in PC cases relating to Art 9(1) is not relevant to the interpretation of Art 9(1) as those cases all concern the meaning of express constitutional prohibitions against inhuman punishment
        *   As SG Constitution does not incorporate an express prohibition against the death penalty, and expressly rejected incorporating such a prohibition in the 1966 Wee Commission Report
        *   This rejection thus makes it impossible to challenge the constitutionality of the MDP. It was not legitimate for the court to read into art 9(1) a constitutional right decisively rejected by the Government, especially given the historical context in which that right was rejected
    *   Does not mean, just because no prohibition against torture exists in Constitution, that laws can permit torture. Parliament recognises that torture is wrong. This explicit recognition stands in sharp contrast that MDP is inhuman punishment. Torture, in so far as it causes harm to the body with criminal intent, is already criminalised under Singapore Penal Code
    *   But accepted that “law” includes not just law passed by Parliament. Also accepted that absurd or arbitrary laws would not be “law” for the purposes of article 9
*   In **_Tan Seng Kee_**, reiterated that the restrictive reading of article 9(1) is supported by text, structure and history of the provision. Court recognised that “in accordance with law” has been interpreted to go beyond positivism, with following **requirements**:
    *   (a) statute must comply with fundamental rules of natural justice, which are procedural rights aimed at securing a fair trial
    *   (b) statute cannot be colourable legislation, such as those directed at securing the conviction of particular individuals
    *   (c) statute cannot be absurd or arbitrary
    *   (d) statute cannot be contrary to the rule of law
    *   Court also considered the argument that laws are absurd, and thus unconstitutional – CA reiterated that the test is procedural in nature and intended to secure the right to a fair process in the context of a possible deprivation of life or personal liberty. As such, absurdity relates to the ability of the law to be enforced, and whether the procedural safeguards (or lack thereof) comply with FRNJ

## Fundamental Rules of Natural Justice (FRNJ)

### FRNJ for Art 9(1); Not Art 12(1)

**FRNJ for Art 9(1):**

*   If the mere act of Parliament passing laws is sufficient to justify the deprivation of these fundamental liberties, the constitutional guarantee of life and personal liberty would offer very little assurance \[**_Tan Eng Hong_** at 30\]. _However_, “save in accordance with law” in Art 9(1) would entail a connection to the fundamental rules of natural justice (FRNJ). As expounded by **Prof Thio** in _Treatise_, FRNJ “relates largely to the conduct of a fair trial”.

**No FRNJ for Art 12(1):**

*   Unlike Art 9(1), adherence to “law” in Art 12(1) is to the protection of equality for persons and does not depend on the validity of a law. _Hence_, "law" in Art 12(1) **does not** have normative content that is independent from the substantive right accorded under the phrases "equal before the law" and "equal protection of the law". Hence, it is incorrect to import "law" in Art 12(1) a requirement that "law" in question must be in accordance with FRNJ.

### What are the principles of FRNJ?

Substantive Good or Bad Essay ref. academic Swati: Sherrick **Pg. 236**

**Procedural: Recognized FRNJs:**

In **_Yong Vui Kong v PP_** \[2011\], and recognizing _Ong Ah Chuan_, the SGCA held the requirement of **fair hearing** and **rule against bias** as (procedural) FRNJs. In particular, these rules required:

1.  Accused can only be convicted if the elements of the offence have been proven according to the applicable standard of proof;
2.  The accused was tried before an independent and impartial tribunal
3.  The accused was heard on his/her defence.

In **_Tan Seng Kee_** \[2022\], the SGCA held the **test of 'absurdity'** is procedural in nature and intended to secure the right to a fair process in the context of a possible deprivation of life or personal liberty. It does not permit the court to examine the substantive content of the law.

*   Eg. An “absurd” statute is one that **cannot be understood or complied with**.
*   It would be impossible to make a defence to any charge under such a statute, and it would thus be “absurd” to deprive a person of life or personal liberty on this basis.
    *   Absurd law **does not refer to legislation** that is so abhorrent that no reasonable person can contemplate it as being morally justified

| Presumption of innocence? No, per Jumaat v AG [2023] SGCA.In Haw Tua Taw, the UKPC appeared to recognise the presumption of innocence in MDA as forming a FRNJ, as it quoted Art 6(2) of the Convention "Everyone charged with a criminal offence shall be presumed innocent until proven guilty according to law" as an 'undoubted fundamental rule of natural justice'. However, this position was likely developed from the "unsworn statement from the dock" (a relic practice of jury trials before 1969) meant courts could not subject accused to cross-examination or perjury. This procedure was changed. |
| --- |

**Substantive Contents FRNJs:**

Singaporean courts have **yet to recognise any substantive content** of FRNJ (except a very narrow prohibition of torture at common law).

*   \[**Open Position**\] The substantive rights present in FRNJ continue to be left open as the courts are clear that it would be “imprudent … to attempt to make a comprehensive list of what constitute\[s\] fundamental rules of natural justice” (**_Haw Tua Tau_** as cited with approval in **_Yong Vui Kong_** 2015) as there are fears of unduly limiting the principles of natural justice prematurely and so the courts have left these rules to the incremental development following common law developments (**_Tan Eng Hong_**).

| 1. Prohibition against MDPOng Ah Chuan held MDP is not unconstitutional and does not contravene FRNJ"Nothing unusual" in a mandatory capital sentence — historically, all capital sentences at common law were mandatory; removing discretion may strengthen deterrenceWhether Singapore should have capital punishment is a "question of social policy solely for the Legislature" — SOP as deference; courts do not review the wisdom of penological choices, only procedural fairness in the trialNguyen held MDA weight threshold is a rational and intelligible differentia; it ensures only serious traffickers face MDP, which "obviates any inhumanity" by building a form of proportionality within the statutory scheme itselfEvaluation:The PC later departed from "nothing unusual" in Reyes v The Queen and Fox v The Queen, holding MDP for murder unconstitutional in Caribbean jurisdictions — the empirical premise was historically contingent, not constitutionally principledThe SOP deference rationale is more durable but renders FRNJ effectively toothless against the legislature's most serious incursions on the right to life2. Prohibition against cruel and inhumane punishmentNguyen SGHC, the court held that there was no customary international law rule that hanging was a cruel and inhuman punishment. Even if there was, domestic statute, insofar as it is unambiguous, would prevail over international law.Yong Vui Kong SGCA, where the court held that it was not possible to incorporate a prohibition against inhuman punishment through the interpretation of existing constitutional provisions.Textualist: No express prohibition against inhuman punishment in the Singapore Constitution - unlike Caribbean constitutions modelled on the ECHROriginalist / Constitutional history: Wee Commission proposed an express inhuman punishment clause; Parliament deliberately rejected it - courts have no legitimate basis to introduce through interpretation what Parliament consciously excludedFour-walls doctrine: Indian authorities (Mithu) rejected - India's text, social conditions, and judicial tradition diverge materially from Singapore's; a "fair, just and reasonable" test would require courts to engage in policy-making, violating SOPDualism: Even if CIL prohibited hanging as inhuman, unambiguous domestic statute prevails under Singapore's dualist system3. Prohibition against Torture (Yong Vui Kong)FRNJ (narrow): FRNJ recognises a prohibition on torture only when used to extract confessions or evidence for use in judicial proceedings — it has nothing to say about "the punishment of criminals after they have been convicted pursuant to a fair trial"; since caning is post-conviction, it falls outside FRNJ entirely.Dualism: Even jus cogens norms cannot override unambiguous domestic statute under Singapore's dualist framework; in any event no CIL rule against caning was establishedUnenumerated rights: Courts cannot read in a freestanding prohibition on torture — doing so would require judges to "sit as a super-legislature and enact their personal views of what is just and desirable into law, which is undemocratic and antithetical to the rule of law"; unenumerated rights can only be implied if they are basic structural features of the Constitution (e.g. right to vote, SOP) — torture prohibition does not qualifyOn the facts — caning ≠ torture: Holistic analysis of purpose, manner and effect:Rattan cane: ≤ 1.27cm diameter; maximum 24 strokes; single session only (no instalments - s 330(1) CPC); carried out in private; continuous medical supervision; exempt categories include women, men above 50, uncommuted death row prisonersDistinguished from Caesar v Trinidad (cat-o'-nine-tails, 15 lashes, forced to witness others being flogged, 2 months in infirmary) - Singapore's safeguards ensure caning does not "breach the high threshold of severity and brutality" required for tortureEvaluation:The pre-/post-conviction FRNJ divide is contestable — Swati Jhaveri criticises it as a "strange restriction"; constitutional protection is strongest during investigation but extinguished at the punishment stage, precisely when the state's coercive power is greatestThe better analysis would ask whether the punishment is so severe as to render the law "contrary to the rule of law" under Tan Seng Kee's fourth requirement — that pathway remains open in principle.4. Access to JusticeIn Tan Eng Hong [2013], the SGHC ultimately did not decide whether access to justice was part of FRNJ.The applicant argued that s377A undermined access to justice by criminalising victims of homosexual assaults and homosexual domestic abuseHowever, the court found that s377A did not deny access to justiceA victim of domestic violence cannot complain of being “deprived” of access to justice because he fears that the authorities will realise that he has previously engaged in male homosexual acts that constitute an offence under 377A.Male victims of sexual assaults may be outside the reach of s377A, considering a purposive interpretation of s377A, and the choice of words used in s377A |
| --- |

### Case History (Skip 2)

*   In **_Ong Ah Chuan_**, court did not give a definitive list of the fundamental rules of natural justice, merely the two established rules of the **right to a fair hearing**, and the **rule against bias**.
*   \[**Incremental Approach**\] In **_Haw Tua Taw_**, PC did not create a comprehensive list of what constitutes fundamental rules of natural justice applicable to procedure
    *   The **substantive rights** present in FRNJ **continue to be left open** as the courts are clear that it would be “imprudent … to attempt to make a comprehensive list of what constitute\[s\] fundamental rules of natural justice” (_Haw Tua Tau_ as cited with approval in _Yong Vui Kong_ 2015) as there are fea**rs of unduly limiting the principles of natural justice prematurely** and so the courts have left these rules to the incremental development following common law developments (_Tan Eng Hong_).
    *   _Dicta_ that suggest the presumption of innocence is recognised as a FRNJ
*   In **_Jabar v PP_**, the court dealt with the question of whether a death sentence that is not carried out for a substantial period **amount then to cruel and inhuman punishment**?
    *   Death row inmates should not be subjected to a prolonged period of imprisonment, anguish suffered while awaiting execution. This anguish is an inevitable consequence, which does not amount to a contravention of the constitutional rights of the prisoner
    *   Court does not have jurisdiction over the question of whether a prolonged delay amounted to ‘cruel and inhuman treatment’ and whether such ‘cruel and inhuman treatment’ is in accordance with law under art 9(1) because of the differences between art 9 and art 21 of the Indian Constitution
        *   Note the above comment in **_Yong Vui Kong_** about the apparent departure from _Nguyen_ in this case
*   In **_Tan Eng Hong_** (HC), based on _Yong Vui Kong_ (MDP), there is merit in the proposition that in order for a law to pass muster under FRNJ, it must **not be arbitrary or absurd**
    *   In _Yong Vui Kong_, Chan CJ suggested that FRNJ encompassed the notion that laws must not be absurd or arbitrary – the suggestion per _Ong Ah Chuan_ could be that legislation so absurd or arbitrary could not possibly be contemplated as being ‘law’ by constitutional framers
        *   But note _Tan Seng Kee_ above that confirms absurd and arbitrariness is not the substantive content but procedural aspect of the law
    *   Thus, in _Tan Eng Hong_, the judge had to deal with what is an arbitrary law
        *   Court held that when one speaks of a law as arbitrary, it would only make sense if one is speaking of the law in the context of its purpose
        *   Returning to _Yong Vui Kong_, the court there held that 15g threshold was not arbitrary, as it was for the legislature “to determine in the light of the information that is available to it about the structure of the illicit drug trade in Singapore, and the way in which it is carried on, where the appropriate quantitative boundary lies between these two classes of dealers”
    *   Returning to s 377A, judge reiterated what was explained in _Lim Meng Suang_ that s 377A is not arbitrary as there is a clear social purpose for which s 377A is its chosen and fitting mechanism
        *   Thus, the targeted undesirable act and the appropriate measure of that undesirability (and associated punishment) is for the legislature to determine. The courts will not inquire into how they determined the threshold. Since the purpose is not illegitimate, it cannot be arbitrary for the purpose of art 9(1)
    *   As for absurdity, court was simply not convinced on the balance of probabilities on the evidence that homosexuality was immutable and thus, it would be absurd to criminalise
    *   Importantly, on the two rules of natural justice, the court rejected suggestions that the law would violate the two rules, holding that s 377A “neither undermines nor restricts access to justice”
*   In **_Yong Vui Kong_** \[2015\] (Caning), court dealt with the **question that FRNJ contained substantive legal rights**, and reiterated that the basis of FRNJ is procedural protection.
    *   They are procedural rights aimed at securing a fair trial. Thus, torture in its narrow sense (used to extract evidence to be used as proof in judicial proceedings) would violate the FRNJ. But **FRNJ have nothing** to say about punishment of criminals **after** they have been convicted pursuant to a fair trial. Thus, even assuming the common law prohibition of torture extends to caning in the context of a punishment to be imposed on a convicted person after trial, it would not have constitutional force because this would not come within the ambit of fundamental rules of natural justice in the sense in which it was referred to in _Ong Ah Chuan_
    *   thus: torture used to extract confessions to secure conviction = violate FRNJ. Post-conviction, torture in the sense of causing physical / other hurt to the convicted felon is prescribed in the CPC / Penal Code. Even if common law prohibition of torture extends to caning, it does not have constitutional force like envisioned in Ong Ah Chuan
*   In **_Jumaat v AG_** \[2023\] SGCA, the court held FRNJ does **not** encompass the "**Presumption of Innocence Rule**" or "Balance of Probabilities Rule". FRNJ is historically determined, NOT dynamically evolving.
    *   **Facts**: Death row applicants challenged constitutionality of ss 18(1) and 18(2) of the MDA (presumptions of possession and knowledge of drugs), arguing these violated FRNJ under Art 9(1) by infringing the presumption of innocence.
    *   **Issue**: Whether the MDA presumptions violated FRNJ under Art 9(1) or the right to equality under Art 12.
    *   **Held**: No violation of FRNJ.

## Article 9(3) Right to Counsel

Art 9(3) – Where a person is arrested, he **shall be informed as soon as may be** of the grounds of his arrest and **shall be allowed to consult and be defended** by a legal practitioner of his choice.

### Nature of this constitutional protection?

**(a) Right to be informed of grounds of arrest as soon as may be**

*   This is necessary in order to enable the preparation of a defence, which entails or to seek habeas corpus to challenge an allegedly unlawful detention.
    *   This means that the offender should be **given sufficient details** to know what offence he allegedly committed. If he was not so informed, the arrested person should be entitled to be released (_Madhu Lima v State_).
    *   The arrested person should be **given such information "as soon as may be"**, which means not immediately but when reasonably practicable and certainly before he is brought **before the Magistrate, that is, within 48 hours**, where a complaint may be made, if any, against with respect to the arrest.

**(b) Right to legal counsel**

*   **_Treatise_**: The importance of having access to a lawyer provides some degree of transparency in ensuring that a detained person is **accorded due process** and not kept in incommunicado detention and abused, whether by physical or mental maltreatment.
*   The right may be breached where, for example, an accused is able to **show the police acted in bad faith** to prevent him from exercising his Art 9(3) right.
*   Provides “checks and balances required in the adversarial process” – ensures the robustness of proceedings and evidence: (_PP v Jayasangar_)

### Issue 1: Reasonable Time for Art 9(3)

*   It is settled law per **_Lee Mau Seng_** that this right “must be granted to him **within a reasonable time** after his arrest", hence it’s **not immediate**. This in my opinion is problematic.
*   In **_Jasbir v PP_**, two weeks was held as reasonable time on the facts that balanced the accused’s right to counsel to the time needed for effective police investigation – which equates to a “factual inquiry of all relevant considerations” as affirmed in SGCA **_James Raj_**.
*   However, strong critics on this holding can be found from academic **Ho Hock Lai** 2014 and **Gabe Tan** 2008.
    *   Ho expounded on how the restrictive reading of Art 9(3) in **_Jasbir Singh_** was not easy to reconcile when held against Lord Diplock’s view in _Ong Ah Chuan_ that Part IV liberties should be given “generous interpretation” for individuals. In that sense, _James Raj_ in adopting _Jasbir_ essentially deprives Art 9(3)’s legal significance and is arguably “worse than ungenerous”.
    *   If we follow a purposive approach, the constitution requires the arrested person to be produced **before a magistrate within 48** **hours**, with a rationale to allow the arrestee to consult and be instructed by his lawyer **effectively** before the magistrate. On the facts of **_James Raj_**, Mr Raj was denied the opportunity to speak to his counsel, **not even for five minutes**, during the hearing of the prosecution's application for him to be remanded at IMH. It is “perplexing” how the counsel can be expected to represent his client effectively at this hearing under such circumstances and **difficult to see how this is not a violation of his right** to legal representation.
    *   Per **Gabe Tan** \[2008\] “_Access to Counsel_”, allowing an arrestee access to a lawyer is to allow his right to be advised qua arrestee and as a safeguard against improper pressure to confess or as a form of deterrence against possible mistreatment or inaccurate recording of statements.
    *   **_Ho_** expounds on how **police questioning is not audio or video recorded and that no law requires this**. In the absence of other safeguards, early access to counsel serves as a critical check against coercion, psychological manipulation, and inaccurate recording of statements (**Thio Shen Yi**, "_Vulnerable Suspects_").
    *   In a way, if safeguards only kick in _after_ \[access to counsel\] has lost much of its relevance, the term ‘arrest’ will be deprived on any meaning role within Art 9(3) as argued by Ho.
    *   In the other camp, the SGCA in _James Raj_ is satisfied that the access to counsel “strikes a balance” between the arrestee’s undoubted right to legal representation and the public interest in enabling the police to discharge their duties and carry out investigations effectively and expeditiously. Albeit there are merits to this position, in that allowing a lawyer to advise his client on his right to silence (**_CPC s22(2)_**) may deprive the police of a confession which is vital evidence. That however should not deprive an arrestee of knowing that right exists since it would “run counter to upholding the rule of law” (Ho).
    *   **_As a suggestion_**, Choo J alternative “balance” reading in SGHC is preferred. His Honour offers that the right **be available immediately** upon arrest a but reasonable time should be allowed for “necessary or unavoidable delay occasioned by practical or administrative concerns”. This pushes the **onus on the police** to show that giving access to counsel would impede police investigation or the administration of justice. Effectively, this delineates the contents of the right to counsel rather limiting the right itself which is less invasive and more proportionate.
*   As a **comparative analysis**, Singapore appears to be the outlier in that we emphasize efficacy over protection as opposed to other jurisdictions (**Thio Shen Yi**, 2016).
    *   In **Malaysia**, the police must inform the suspect of his right to counsel _before_ any questioning commences or before any statement is recorded.
        *   Similarly in **Taiwan**, the police must wait 4 hours for a defence attorney to show up before investigations
    *   In **Hong Kong**, the right to counsel is not absolute; limited delays are only permitted where a senior officer has reasonable grounds that the right would interfere with investigations. With a similar 48-hour charge after apprehension to SG, the arrestee’s right will be triggered.
    *   In **India**, the Supreme Court case of DK Vasu set out guidelines where the suspect is entitled to meet, communicate and consult with his lawyer during investigations, albeit not entirely through interrogations.
*   It remains to be seen if any structural constrains will be implemented that aligns with Choo J’s approach in SGHC James Raj which alike the jurisdictions surveyed, provides burdens on the state to justify the specific need to delay an arrestee’s rights.

### Issue 2: Right to be informed of one’s right to legal counsel

The characterisation of **_Art 9(3)_** as a **negative right** in **_Rajeevan_** is contestable. The SGHC held “shall be allowed” meant no obligation imposed on the relevant authority to inform and advise the person under custody of his right to counsel. As such, for the courts to read in such a right would “be tantamount to judicial legislation”

*   However, on a logical front, an arrested person who does not know of the right cannot meaningfully exercise it, making the right functionally hollow for those without legal training. Given the informational and resource asymmetry between an accused and the prosecution or policy (**_James Raj,_** SGHC), the right to be informed one's counsel would augment the right to a fair trial.
*   There is also a need to balance the need for effective investigations and protection of the accused. With such a negative right, the uninformed accused being subjected to stressful interrogations **can lead to disastrous consequences**. In the case of **_Benjamin Lim_**, the police had arrested him at school on accusation, and with no teacher / parents / lawyers had interrogated him and hours later he had committed suicide.

## Article 149

| Legislation against subversion149.— (1) If an Act recites that action has been taken or threatened by any substantial body of persons, whether inside or outside Singapore —(a)to cause, or to cause a substantial number of citizens to fear, organised violence against persons or property;(b)to excite disaffection against the President or the Government;(c)to promote feelings of ill-will and hostility between different races or other classes of the population likely to cause violence;(d)to procure the alteration, otherwise than by lawful means, of anything by law established; or(e)which is prejudicial to the security of Singapore,any provision of that law designed to stop or prevent that action or any amendment to that law or any provision in any law enacted under clause (3) is valid notwithstanding that it is inconsistent with Article 9, 11, 12, 13 or 14, or would, apart from this Article, be outside the legislative power of Parliament.(2)  A law containing such a recital as is mentioned in clause (1) shall, if not sooner repealed, cease to have effect if a resolution is passed by Parliament annulling such law, but without prejudice to anything previously done by virtue thereof or to the power of Parliament to make a new law under this Article.(3)  If, in respect of any proceedings whether instituted before or after 27 January 1989, any question arises in any court as to the validity of any decision made or act done in pursuance of any power conferred upon the President or the Minister by any law referred to in this Article, such question shall be determined in accordance with the provisions of any law as may be enacted by Parliament for this purpose; and nothing in Article 93 shall invalidate any law enacted pursuant to this clause.Restrictions on preventive detention151.— (1) Where any law or ordinance made or promulgated in pursuance of this Part provides for preventive detention —(a)the authority on whose order any person is detained under that law or ordinance shall as soon as may be, inform him of the grounds for his detention and, subject to clause (3), the allegations of fact on which the order is based, and shall give him the opportunity of making representations against the order as soon as may be; and(b)no citizen of Singapore shall be detained under that law or ordinance for a period exceeding 3 months unless an advisory board constituted as mentioned in clause (2) has considered any representations made by him under paragraph (a) and made recommendations thereon to the President.(2)  An advisory board constituted for the purposes of this Article shall consist of a chairman, who shall be appointed by the President and who shall be or have been, or be qualified to be, a Supreme Court Judge, and 2 other members, who shall be appointed by the President after consultation with the Chief Justice. [38/2019](3)  This Article does not require any authority to disclose facts the disclosure of which would, in its opinion, be against the national interest.(4)  Where an advisory board constituted for the purposes of this Article recommends the release of any person under any law or ordinance made or promulgated in pursuance of this Part, the person shall not be detained or further detained without the concurrence of the President, acting in his discretion, if the recommendations of the advisory board are not accepted by the authority on whose advice or order the person is detained. | Legislation against subversion | 149.— (1) If an Act recites that action has been taken or threatened by any substantial body of persons, whether inside or outside Singapore —(a)to cause, or to cause a substantial number of citizens to fear, organised violence against persons or property;(b)to excite disaffection against the President or the Government;(c)to promote feelings of ill-will and hostility between different races or other classes of the population likely to cause violence;(d)to procure the alteration, otherwise than by lawful means, of anything by law established; or(e)which is prejudicial to the security of Singapore,any provision of that law designed to stop or prevent that action or any amendment to that law or any provision in any law enacted under clause (3) is valid notwithstanding that it is inconsistent with Article 9, 11, 12, 13 or 14, or would, apart from this Article, be outside the legislative power of Parliament.(2)  A law containing such a recital as is mentioned in clause (1) shall, if not sooner repealed, cease to have effect if a resolution is passed by Parliament annulling such law, but without prejudice to anything previously done by virtue thereof or to the power of Parliament to make a new law under this Article.(3)  If, in respect of any proceedings whether instituted before or after 27 January 1989, any question arises in any court as to the validity of any decision made or act done in pursuance of any power conferred upon the President or the Minister by any law referred to in this Article, such question shall be determined in accordance with the provisions of any law as may be enacted by Parliament for this purpose; and nothing in Article 93 shall invalidate any law enacted pursuant to this clause.Restrictions on preventive detention151.— (1) Where any law or ordinance made or promulgated in pursuance of this Part provides for preventive detention —(a)the authority on whose order any person is detained under that law or ordinance shall as soon as may be, inform him of the grounds for his detention and, subject to clause (3), the allegations of fact on which the order is based, and shall give him the opportunity of making representations against the order as soon as may be; and(b)no citizen of Singapore shall be detained under that law or ordinance for a period exceeding 3 months unless an advisory board constituted as mentioned in clause (2) has considered any representations made by him under paragraph (a) and made recommendations thereon to the President.(2)  An advisory board constituted for the purposes of this Article shall consist of a chairman, who shall be appointed by the President and who shall be or have been, or be qualified to be, a Supreme Court Judge, and 2 other members, who shall be appointed by the President after consultation with the Chief Justice. [38/2019](3)  This Article does not require any authority to disclose facts the disclosure of which would, in its opinion, be against the national interest.(4)  Where an advisory board constituted for the purposes of this Article recommends the release of any person under any law or ordinance made or promulgated in pursuance of this Part, the person shall not be detained or further detained without the concurrence of the President, acting in his discretion, if the recommendations of the advisory board are not accepted by the authority on whose advice or order the person is detained. | (a) | to cause, or to cause a substantial number of citizens to fear, organised violence against persons or property; | (b) | to excite disaffection against the President or the Government; | (c) | to promote feelings of ill-will and hostility between different races or other classes of the population likely to cause violence; | (d) | to procure the alteration, otherwise than by lawful means, of anything by law established; or | (e) | which is prejudicial to the security of Singapore, | any provision of that law designed to stop or prevent that action or any amendment to that law or any provision in any law enacted under clause (3) is valid notwithstanding that it is inconsistent with Article 9, 11, 12, 13 or 14, or would, apart from this Article, be outside the legislative power of Parliament. | (2)  A law containing such a recital as is mentioned in clause (1) shall, if not sooner repealed, cease to have effect if a resolution is passed by Parliament annulling such law, but without prejudice to anything previously done by virtue thereof or to the power of Parliament to make a new law under this Article. | (3)  If, in respect of any proceedings whether instituted before or after 27 January 1989, any question arises in any court as to the validity of any decision made or act done in pursuance of any power conferred upon the President or the Minister by any law referred to in this Article, such question shall be determined in accordance with the provisions of any law as may be enacted by Parliament for this purpose;
