### Context and History

Originally (Constitution of Malaya) Art 149 was drafted and included to **counter communist** threats, giving Parliament broad powers to enact legislation that may be inconsistent with other parts of the Constitution (eg fund. Liberties)

*   BUT there are safeguards in Art 151
*   To fall under Art 149, the Act in question must recite the elements in Art 149(1)

**Two Primary Legislation**

*   **_Internal Security Act 1960_** – internal security threats; promulgated pursuant to Art 149
*   **_Criminal Law (Temporary Provisions) Act 1955_** – criminal activity

### Thoughts on Art 149/150:

*   Under Art 149, legislation of a nature under one of the 5 recitals in art 149(1) would be given valid force **despite its inconsistency with either the Legislative Power** (Art 38), or any inconsistencies with Articles 9 (life and personal liberty), 11 (retrospective criminal laws and repeated trials), 12 (equal protection), 13 (freedom of movement), and 14 (freedom of speech, assembly and association)
*   **To protect against potential abuse, Art 151 acts as a check to preventive detention** – an advisory board staffed by high office holders who are advisors to the President. Detention beyond 3 months by the authority must be effected with approval by the advisory body, and the advisory body can advise the President in disagreement with the authority and recommend release of said persons
*   Per **_Tan Seet Eng_** at \[70\]: writ of _habeas corpus_ or order for review of detention (ORD) necessitates some basis be shown for the order to be issued. Thus, in a sense, the **Probably Cause Test** is concerned with the burden that the applicant must discharge at the initial stage of ORD application whereas the Traditional Test (per _Chng Suan Tze_) is concerned with the scope of judicial review or the subject matter the court can inquire into in circumstances where the primary decision-making power has been vested in the Executive.
*   Per **_Teo Soh Lung_**, the court’s ability to conduct judicial review subject to Art 149(3) and s 8B(2) ISA is restricted to compliance with procedural requirements for ISA cases. As to whether such laws are validly passed, the grounds upon which to impugn a law as unconstitutional are article 4 (Supremacy Clause). However, legislation that is of a character within article 149 will be given valid force per article 149. It goes that the Legislature remains the final arbiter on the validity of a statute. Afterall, per Parliamentary Debates (25 Aug 1994), the Prime Minister and Cabinet are ultimately responsible for Singapore’s defence and security, and thus they are the ones who can declare an emergency
    *   When Parliament **amended Art 149** in light of _Chng Suan Tze_, Minister **S Jayakumar**, Second Reading of the Constitution of the Republic of Singapore (Amendment) Bill, _Singapore Parliamentary Debates_, _Official Report_ (25 January 1989) vol 52 made the following comments about why the Legislature made the amendments they did
        *   If the subjective test was not restored, and if foreign case law and precedents allow the courts to be involved in an interventionist role, then the law on national security matters will be governed by cases decided abroad
        *   It will also render Singapore judges responsible for and answerable to decisions affecting national security of Singapore, but this was never the intention of the legislature as expressed in the ISA – it had been the settled principles of the subjective test that had guided ISA (and predecessor legislation) through many decades of various security threats
        *   Judges are also not equipped to investigate or decide whether preventive detention is warranted
    *   This amendment also removed appeals to the Privy Council for security materials, for clear reasons that SG is now Independent and should not let the conditions of UK determine our security concerns
*   Is ISA constitutional? Abrogates rule of law, Chan Sek Keong’s “From Angst to Empathy”
    *   But per S Jayakumar’s arguments above, perhaps this is a matter of rights vs necessity
    *   See Pg. 256-261 of **Sherrick’s Mini Notes**.

### Should courts have the power to review preventive decisions made by executive?

*   **_Chng Suan Tze_**: the notion of a subjective or unfettered discretion was contrary to the rule of law. **All power had legal limits and the rule of law demanded that courts examine the exercise of discretionary power**. If the Executive in exercising its discretion under an Act of Parliament exceeded the four corners within which Parliament decided it could exercise its discretion, such an exercise of discretion would be ultra vires the Act and a court of law must be able to hold it to be so
    *   Clear that where decision is based on national security considerations, judicial review would be precluded. It must be shown to the court in such cases that considerations of national security were involved
    *   Thus, the function of a court in the review of discretionary power depended on whether a jurisdictional or precedent fact was involved.
        *   Where no jurisdictional issue arose, the scope of review was limited to _Wednesbury_ principles;
        *   Where a jurisdictional fact issue arose, the scope of review extended to deciding whether the evidence justified the decision.
    *   Whether a particular discretionary power was subject to any jurisdictional or precedent fact **depended on the construction of the legislation** which created that power. In respect of ss 8 and 10 of the Internal Security Act (ISA), the scope of review of the exercise of discretion was limited to normal judicial review principles of “illegality, irrationality or procedural impropriety”
    *   Thus, in **ISA cases**, judicial review is only limited to normal judicial review of _GCHQ_ grounds of review, thus where any unlawfulness in conditions of detention was alleged, what should be challenged was the legality of the conditions, rather than the legality of the detention itself.
*   **_Tan Seet Eng_**: propositions from _Chng Suan Tze_ were legislatively overruled in regard to the ISA but not regarding CLTPA (or perhaps broadly to all laws barring national security ones).
*   Thus:
    *   **CLTPA uses the objective standard** (_Tan Seet Eng v AG_ \[2015\] (CA)). The standard is an objective review of the Minister’s exercise of discretion
    *   For CLTPA, note that judicial review is not excluded **even though the Minister’s decision is final**. The court can still review the Minister’s decisions under the CLTPA based on the traditional tests of illegality, irrationality and procedural impropriety.
    *   This creates an anomaly where ISA detentions are actually LESS reviewable than CLTPA detentions despite potentially graver national security concerns.

### Debates on CLTPA (Criminal Law (Temporary Provisions) Act 1955)

*   Is a form of preventive detention
*   Per Minister Shanmugam’s speech during the second reading, it is invoked against secret societies, gangsters, drug trafficking syndicates etc to allow people to testify against the accused. Ensure safety and security of Singapore.
    *   In 2018, law was reenacted, creating a 4th Schedule that sets out a list of activities of criminal nature that permits detention or Police supervision
    *   Finality clause 3 does not overrule grounds for judicial review in **_Tan Seet Eng_** _ie_ _GCHQ_ grounds for review
*   In opposition, MP Pritam Singh argues that such preventive detentions goes against the rule of law
    *   Worried that clause 3 acts as an ouster clause
*   In response, Min Shan says clause 3 codifies **_Tan Seet Eng_**

| Tan Seet Eng v. AG [2016] (Court of Appeal)Facts: Appellant detained without trial under CLTPA pursuant to ministerial power under s. 30 of the CLTPA; detention was then extended for another year, because Tan was deemed a threat to public peace, safety, and order (he was involved in a match-fixing ring).Argument at HC: Detention was illegal, irrational and procedurally improper.Argued (on appeal): (1) Match-fixing did not fall under category of offences under CLTPA; (2) CLTPA is a measure of last resort; and (2) CLTPA was not intended to target organized crime per se and there had to be other conditions such as violence and intimidation of witnesses to invoke CLTPA.Standard of review? Court will conduct an OBJECTIVE assessment of Minister’s exercise of discretion court had the power to assess whether the Minister DID IN FACT HAVE THE POWER TO ACT and whether there was reasonable basis for thinking that he was so acting Reiterated principle of legality – which according to the court – supports an objective standard of reviewScope of review: GCHQ / traditional grounds of review of illegality, irrationality and procedural improprietyClosely scrutinise the grounds put forward by the Minister.Court to examine whether Minister acted ‘lawfully’ within the scope of the lawOn the facts, the offences disclosed in the grounds of detention did not fall within the scope of the CLTPA. Activities of recruiting runners and agents, financing and/or directing unspecified match-fixing activities – corrupt practices, but not of the level of gravity required under CLTPA.Grounds of detention showed very few connections with Singapore. Nothing to indicate these activities were detrimental to the public safety, peace, and good order of Singapore. |
| --- |

# Administrative Law (+ Hypo Framework)

**Argument Building Rules**

| Rule | What It Means |
| --- | --- |
| Best case first | Lead with your strongest ground. Put weaker arguments later. |
| ≥2 cases per issue | “This case helps me, this part of this case also helps me, and this is my conclusion.” |
| Spell out the details | For every case: key facts → reasoning → holding → how the hypo maps to it. Never just name-drop. |
| Counter-arguments | For every major argument, state what the authority will argue back (1–2 lines) and your response (1–2 lines). |
| Advisory formula | “I would advise X to argue that the decision was [illegal] on the basis that [ground].” |
| Use obiter | Obiter dicta = invitation to make new arguments for your client. |
| Modest assumptions | Work with facts first. Then: “Assuming X, then Y.” Don’t invent new facts. |
| Aim 12–13 issues | Comprehensive coverage of 12–13 issues > superficial coverage of 16. That’s the A+ band. |

**Quick Issue Spotting Indicators**

| If You See This in the Hypo… | Think About This Ground… |
| --- | --- |
| Statutory condition not met / wrong definition | Ultra vires / Precedent fact |
| Fine/penalty exceeds statutory maximum | Ultra vires on quantum |
| Decision made by officer, not Minister | Delegation / Carltona |
| Minister relied on advice of external body | Abrogation (or consultation) |
| Policy requirement unconnected to Act’s purpose | Improper purpose |
| Consideration not mentioned in statute or policy | Irrelevant consideration |
| Expert evidence or key material ignored | Relevant consideration omitted |
| Appeal rejected instantly / “decision is final” | Fettering |
| Decision defeats the Act’s own purpose | Irrationality (futility — Mir Hassan) |
| Blanket presumption / pre-judgment | Irrationality (Ng Hock Guan) |
| Punishment wildly disproportionate | Proportionality via irrationality |
| Website says “will approve if…” then departs | SLE (Chiu Teng) |
| Promise → applicant complies → authority reneges | SLE + Fettering + Futility |
| Constitutional right implicated (liberty, property) | Lower irrationality threshold |
| Political embarrassment cited as reason | Irrelevant consideration (Padfield) |

## Step 0: Read statute, facts and decision

*   Read the **statute** first.
*   Identify every defined term, condition, and power. Read the **facts**.
*   Identify **each decision** separately — each one gets its own analysis.
*   For each decision, note: **who made it, under what power, and what happened**.

**TIP**: List every decision the client wants to challenge. State remedies (Quashing order, prohibiting order)

## Amenability to Judicial Review (Quick – Only if needed)

### What is the nature and source of the power of the administrative body

**Para:** The court will adopt a source and nature test to determine amenability to judicial review. Under the **source test**, the court examines if the exercised power arises from statute or subsidiary legislation (**_Linda Lai_**). Under the **nature test**, it considers whether the decision involves the exercise of a public law function (**_Yeap Wai Kong_**). As a caution held in **_Manjit Singh v AG_**, the mere fact that a power stems from statute should not necessarily mean that it is amenable to judicial review, though it will ordinarily be so without compelling reasons to the contrary.

*   If **public body**, _ie_ creature of statute, the source test used to be sufficient, but has since become extended to the nature test (**_Yeap Wai Kong v SGX_** **_ST_**). However, as held in **_PSC v Linda Lai_**, even though a public body is a creature of statute, whose powers and functions are statutorily underpinned (in this case, the Constitution of Singapore), courts only intervene where there is an element of public law.
    *   \[**EMPLOYMENT: PSC**\] In _Linda Lai_, the source test was not applied as the powers were held to be derived from the contract of employment, and not from the power conferred by the Constitution. The reasoning being that when statutory boards make certain decisions, it does not invariably follow that the statutory bodies are exercising a statutory power.

| In Linda Lai, Applicant held appointment in Land Office – Employment terminated, sought JR irrationality – Held: Not Amenable since relationship between PSC and the applicant 'was one of employer and employee” – issue not its performance of “public duties or the exercise of its powers as an authority |
| --- |

**_Eval_: (1)** Employment contract is **merely implementation mechanism** to regulate public service appointment/dismissal, not source of power; **(2) Nature test clearly met**: PSC recruits, trains, regulates, dismisses public officers affecting governance integrity – quintessential public function despite contractual form; **(3) Formalistic absurdity**: All public employment uses contracts; _Linda Lai_ creates accountability gap where PSC can claim "private HR discretion" to evade JR for whistleblower retaliation

*   *   \[**LEASES: JTC**\] A similar outcome on similar reasoning as to what powers were exercised by a statutory body was reached in **_UDL Marine v JTC_**. Although JTC is a statutory body, it was acting in a commercial capacity akin to a private landlord and not exercising any public law function.

| In UDL Marine, Applicant held lease under JTC – Application to renew was denied – Held: Not Amenable since leases were contracts – JTC could come up with ‘own criteria’ for leases ‘like any private landlord’ – Irrelevant that JTC considered non-commercial factors like jobs generated and value add to GDP. |
| --- |

**_Eval_: (1)** **Source test ignores statutory monopoly**: JTC wields exclusive statutory control over Singapore's industrial land (JTC Act) - lease decisions implement public land allocation policy, not merely private commercial choices; **(2) Nature test downplays public impact**: JTC's "own criteria"

*   If **private body**, source test is rendered ineffective. But if the body, despite lacking authority _de jure_, exercises power of a public function _de facto_, the decision may be subject to judicial review (**_Ex Parte Datafin_**).
    *   \[**PUBLIC DUTY: DATAFIN**\] In **_Ex Parte Datafin_**, the Panel, not a statutory body, acted as _de facto_ regulatory authority over mergers because it functioned as the central authority for policing takeover behaviours in the UK market.

| In Ex Parte Datafin, the Panel was an unincorporated private association, that promulgated Code on takeovers and mergers – was “unspoken assumption” that any breach of the rules 🡺 penalty via sanction by UK Government – Held: Amenable since UK Government made decisions on the Panel’s Code and advice, affecting legal rights – But-for the Panel, the Government would have to provide its own Code. |
| --- |
| [PUBLIC DUTY: SGX-ST] Similarly in Yeap Wai Kong, although SGX-ST is a private company, its actions had public law character since (1) it was an exchange registered under SFA and was required to discharge its obligations, (2) its reprimand powers found in its Listing Rules, were enacted pursuant to under SFA, (3) its reprimand function had significant financial and reputational implication to persons subject to it. |
| --- |

*   *   \[**LIVELIHOOD: ASSOCIATION**\] In the Malaysian case of **_Woong Kwok Cheng v HR Hochstadt_**, the court held that the Malayan Racing Association (MRA) possessed significant public powers and had the power to affect the livelihood of the claimant with its decisions.

| In Woong Kwok Cheng, Association licenced jockey for horse-racing – Applicant had licence suspended, reapplied after suspension term ended, but rejected – sought JR breach of NJ – Held: Amenable since Association ‘monopolised trade’ of horse racing, ‘taking on the character of an industry’ – affected Applicant’s ‘livelihood’ and ‘right to work’. |
| --- |

*   *   \[**PRIVATE HOSPITAL**\] This statement of law has been affirmed and endorsed in Singapore (**_Yip Man Hing Kevin v Gleneagles Hospital_**).

| In Yip Man Hing Kevin, the private hospital’s suspension of the doctor’s privileges was based entirely on their employment contract. Therefore, the SGHC held that the decision was not amenable to judicial review as the hospital was not discharging any public function and the applicant’s rights were grounded in private law. Accordingly, the decision failed both the Source and Nature tests. |
| --- |

*   *   For an incorporated association like a club, the basis of membership is contractual. **_Nevertheless_**, the rules of natural justice continue to apply, and are implicit in such contracts even if silent. This requires the club to act fairly against its members, especially in disciplinary proceedings that may result in sanctions (**_Khong Kin Hoong Lawrence v Singapore Polo Club_**)

### Jurisdictional Review Limits

*   **Supervisory Jurisdiction**
    *   Application to superior court is not for review of the merits, but the validity of the decision made by the authority (**_Marplan Pte Ltd v AG_**)
    *   The applicant must have an interest to protect and not merely an interested person. **Only the violation of a right personal to the applicant will suffice** (**_Tan Eng Hong v AG_**).
    *   Such violations can be alteration of rights or obligations of that person which are enforceable by or against him in private law, or depriving him of some benefit or advantage that was a legitimate expectation they had (**_Council of Civil Service Unions v Minister for Civil Service_**)
*   **Lack of Precedent Facts? (aka Jurisdictional Facts)**

Where the executive's power is CONDITIONAL on the existence of a specific fact, that fact must be established by the executive on the merits before the discretion is triggered.

*   *   Whether a particular discretionary power is subject to any jurisdictional or precedent fact **depends on the construction of the legislation** which creates that power (**_Chng Suan Tze v MHA_**)

| In Chng Suan Tze, Applicants detained under ISA s8(1) – factual judgement of “national security” not intended for judicial review – was outside precedent fact category as the detention must have been necessary to the satisfaction of the President / Minister – Order quashed. |
| --- |

*   *   For precedent fact categories, the test is **whether the decision was one that could be reasonably reached based on the evidence before him**, with a factual basis for making the evaluation (**_Re Fong Thin Choo_**)

| In Re Fong Thin Choo, Importer’s exported goods were challenged as non-export and subject to duty fees pursuant to Customs Regulations r 126 – Held (1) whether goods had been exported proofed objectively, and (2) “Director-General satisfaction” requirement in r 126 not met by signature (breach) = irrational. |
| --- |

*   *   **Burden of proof lies on the Executive** to establish that the precedent fact exists.
*   **Ouster + Immunity clauses?**
    *   Clause XX of the (Statute/Subsidiary legislation/XXX) appears to be an ouster clause as it purports to limit the court’s ability of judicial review. In Singapore, **ouster clauses usually do not bar judicial review** in light of constitutional safeguards and the supervisory role of the judiciary under Article 93 of the Constitution and the principle of separation of power (**_Nagaenthran_** CA).
    *   \[**IMMUNITY CLAUSES**\] **_However_**, XX may argue that the clause is not an ouster clause but instead an immunity clause. In **_Nagaenthran_** SGCA, the court distinguished between ouster clauses, which aim to remove judicial authority entirely, and immunity clauses, which shield decision-makers from liability **but do not affect judicial competence** (at \[51\]-\[59\]). In general, the court will uphold immunity clauses where the subject matter lies outside judicial competence.

| In Nagaenthran, the court held that s.33B(4) of the MDA was not an ouster clause but an immunity clause instead – only immunises PP from civil suit for failing to issue Certificate of Substantial Assistance, “unless done in bad faith or malice”.Therefore, judicial review for illegality, irrationality and procedural impropriety remains available, though the court refrained itself from adjudicating the merits of the decisions that involve operational or intelligence based judgements. |
| --- |
| [PRIVATE CONTEXT (Limited application)] In Hull University, the university Visitor had exclusive authority to resolve internal disputes under the domestic statutes of the university, which were not subject to the general public law framework. Judicial review was unavailable because the Visitor’s role arose from common law and applied a self-contained legal regime. |
| --- |

*   **Ultra vires actions**
    *   Acts in excess authority by doing something **not empowered to do**:
        *   In **_Wong Yip Pui_** **_v HDB_**, the legislation prevented authorised occupiers from leasing the flat. As the plaintiff’s son was not an authorised occupier, because there was no application from Wong to the HDB to buy the flat – instead HDB offered to sell him the flat. Compulsory acquisition was _ultra vires_ because HDB had no power to do such act

## Substantive Review Grounds

Following Lord Diplock’s three “I”s of judicial review held in **_GCHQ_**, affirmed in SGCA **_Chng Suan Tze_**, this current case would likely fall under the ambit of \[illegality/irrationality/procedural impropriety\].

### Type 1: Illegality

Illegality arises where a public authority exercises its powers of an **improper purpose**, takes into account **irrelevant considerations**, fails to consider relevant considerations, or otherwise **exceeds the limits** of its legal authority (**_Padfield_**).

**Improper Purpose(s):**

Improper purpose is where the authority exercised its statutory power for a purpose that **falls outside the scope of the statute**. Even where multiple motives exist the true and dominant purpose must align with the statutory objective (**_Shanmugam Manohar_**), otherwise, the decision is unlawful (**_Padfield_**).

*   \[**IMPROPER PURPOSE: PROJECTS**\]

In **_World Development Movement_**, the court held that the UK Secretary of State’s decision to fund the Pergau Dam project in Malaysia was unlawful. By the time the decision was made, economic appraisals had overwhelmingly shown the project was **uneconomic and no developmental promotion purpose existed**:. Funding it to **preserve political goodwill, without a genuine developmental purpose**, was hence an unlawful use of statutory power.

*   \[**PROSECUTORIAL POWERS**\]

In **_Ramalingam_**, prosecutorial power under Art 35(8) exists to serve the public interest in law and order. Using it arbitrarily, for improper purposes, or in bad faith falls outside the proper scope.

*   \[**SUBSEQUENT CHANGE OF PUPROSE: LAND**\]

In **_Teng Fuh Holdings_**, the SGHC considered whether a gazetted notification of compulsory land acquisition was made illegally, and held that even if the intended use of the land changed over time, from industrial to residential and recreational, this does not evidence improper purpose, as long as the original acquisition was made genuinely for a public purpose. **_Unless_**, applicant proves change was in bad faith.

*   *   There must be a _prima facie_ case of reasonable suspicion of bad faith for review to proceed
*   \[**MULITPLE PURPOSES**\]:

**True and Dominant Purpose test**

In **_Shanmugam Manohar_**, the applicant alleged that the police had recorded his statements for a collateral improper purpose outside of criminal investigation. The SGHC held that the dominant purpose remained investigative, which was the lawful purpose under the Act, and bad faith was not proved to show that the improper purpose was the actual “dominant” purpose in carrying out the investigation.

Similarly in **_MM_** **_Pillay_**, the SGHC upheld the Minister’s traffic regulations under _s 90(1) RTA_ because the true and dominant purpose of imposing the rule was to control congestion through a restricted zone scheme, which was within statutory authority. Charging fees were incidental or secondary motives did not taint an otherwise lawful exercise of power if the dominant purpose remained valid.

**Irrelevant Consideration(s):**

The foundational principle is that a decision-maker exercising statutory discretion must act in accordance with the **policy and objects of the enabling Act**, which is a matter of law for the court to determine (**_Tan Seet Eng endorsing Padfield_**). _On the facts, the authority appears to have disregarded the relevant consideration of…_

*   **_Padfield_**:
    *   UKHL held the Minister’s refused to refer a complaint by milk producers to a committee of investigation, fearing that an upheld complaint would embarrass the government and force a politically inconvenient price review. Political fear of parliamentary reprisal is not a relevant consideration.
*   **_Nagaenthran_** \[2019\]: the PP's discretion under s 33B(4) is at his "sole discretion" — but this applies to the manner and extent of inquiry, **not** to the substance of whether substantive assistance was rendered.
*   **_Ramalingam_**: Mere difference in charges do not raise prima facie inference of bias; PP’s discretion-based considerations like on evidence, willingness to testify and public interest factors.
    *   Prosecutorial power is also subject to legal limits, and cannot be exercised arbitrarily. It can only be used for purpose for which it was granted, and must give unbiased consideration to every offender, as well as avoid taking into account irrelevant considerations.
*   **_City Developments_** – neither irrational nor unreasonable for government agencies to adopt an integrated and holistic approach towards the formulation as well as the implementation of government policies. Thus, it was a relevant consideration for Chief Assessor to give consideration to planning considerations, which were normally under ambit of URA.

**Fetters their own discretion / Abrogation / Failure to exercise own discretion**

A public authority may formulate general policies to guide its discretion, but must retain the ultimate responsibility and the statutory function must be still exercised consistently with the purpose and framework of the statute (**_Asia Development_**; **_Komoko Motors_**). This means there must be application of the mind to the matter and not slavishly follow a position.

**THINK**: (1) Time: i.e., Send an appeal and but immediately dismissed / (2) Using someone else’s decision as a unauthorised proxy.

*   In **_British Oxygen_**, Lord Reid held that the Act had intended to grant discretion to the Board on whether to provide a grant or not. The question was whether reasonable for the Board to devise a policy or rules to structure its discretion.
    *   Nothing wrong in the Board adopting a policy to guide its discretion, also reasonable to publicise the policy to avoid fruitless applications
    *   But **what the board could not do was to shut its ears to an application or refuse to listen at all** – no need to be an oral hearing, but the tribunal / authority must have carefully considered what an applicant has to say.
*   In **_Komoco Motors_**, the CA, overturning the HC judgment, held that there was no fettering of discretion.
    *   Distinguishing this case from **_Lines_**, ROV adopting the Customs OMV was a decision made in the exercise of her power under LTA rules and **for practical reasons**
        *   **Customs had more data and resources** to determine the OMV of vehicles, thereby making its determination of OMV more reliable, and
        *   In any case, an aggrieved importer has the right to object to and be heard on Customs’ determination of a vehicle’s OMV and to appeal to the court
    *   General principle that was established in **_Lines_** is that an administrative agency must not fetter its own power or abrogate it to another administrative agency
        *   Did not apply in this case because ROV did not take instructions from another statutory agency
        *   Additionally, Registrar was found to have **given genuine consideration to Komoco’s representations**

### Type 2: Irrationality

To test irrationality, the decision made must be “_so outrageous in its defiance of logic or accepted moral standards that no sensible authority could have arrived at it_” (**_Lines Int’l, citing GCHQ_**). However, in deciding if the decision was reached irrationally, the court will avoid substituting their own views on the merits (**_Chee Siok Chin_**).

**_Tan Seet Eng_** CA viewed illegality and irrationality **as separate though overlapping heads of review**, as each serves a different purpose at their core:

*   Illegality examines the source and extent of the power + whether it has been informed by relevant considerations _only_. Irrationality looks at the decision made and asks if it was unreasonable considering the correct factors.

**Irrationality not found**

*   \[**GAMBLING**\] In **_Lines International_**, the SGHC upheld the guidelines restricting berthing rights, finding that the authorities’ concern over undesirable activities on Cruise to Nowhere (CNWs) was a rational objective. The guidelines were not outrageous or illogical, and thus not irrational **even if another approach might have been more effective**.
*   \[**PUBLIC SAFETY**\] In **_Kang Ngah Wei_**, the SGHC found that the Traffic Police’s decision to revoke a driver’s licence after an asthma attack-related accident was rational, **considering public safety**, and not outrageous defiant of logic.
*   \[**PAST HISTORY**\] In **_Vijaya Kumar_**, the Thaipusam permit case, the **history of racial tensions** in Singapore gave ample justification to the police to pay special attention to events involving a religious element. Thus, held that imposition of conditions subject to legitimate public order considerations was not irrational.
*   \[**ARBITRARY POWERS**\] In **_Chee Siok Chin_**, police arrested small group of CPF protestors with a large squadron of officers.
    *   The arrest was held to be justified under Misc. Offences Act, as **officers had discretion to arrest** if there are reasonable grounds for suspicion that law had been offended, and it was reasonable for the officers to conclude that the words and actions were “insulting” and / or “abusive” and intended to harass CPF board.
    *   **Test**: But court also recognised that **there must be limitations and safeguards to ensure power not arbitrarily exercised**. Courts must ensure all:

1.  latitude in assessing reasonableness on factual substratum;
    1.  from the officer's perspective **at the time of arrest**, not with hindsight.
    2.  whether a **reasonable officer, on the facts** **available** would applicant’s actions had grounds of arrest
2.  relief must be possible when legitimate grievances are shown to exist;
    1.  even if arrest was technically justified, **judicial relief must remain available** if arrest was beyond statute/scope of power.
    2.  **_As an eval_**, (2) is theoretically important but practically elusive. The **burden lies on the applicant** to establish bad faith or improper purpose — a **high threshold** that is rarely met in practice because the state's internal reasoning is not always disclosed and courts are reluctant to infer bad faith from ambiguous facts. The safeguard is real in principle but weak in application.
3.  **balance** between the liberty of a person and the need to uphold law and order must be struck
    1.  **_As an eval_**, court recognised that arrest power cannot be unfettered, but seem to stop short of articulating a positive content for the proportionality of police response. **_However_**, if proportionality of the officer’s action was considered by courts, the courts may no longer be supervising the legality of the decision, but substituting its own views on the merits.

*   \[**LAND TAX**\] In **_City Developments Ltd_**, court held that the higher tax on land hoarding policy was neither irrational nor unknown to property developments especially given Singapore’s land-scarce situation.
    *   **Not unreasonable** for the policy to target commercial land hoarding since developers are likely to be profit driven and thus hold land
*   \[**CALCULATING PREMIUMS**\] In **_Chiu Teng_**, there was a policy of calculating premiums paid on lifting restrictions on planning permissions, and the argument was that it was unreasonable for SLA to not adhere to public information in calculating.
    *   As for whether the mode of calculating was irrational, the court held that it would be **slow to review such polycentric matters**, and balancing of competing interests was not within the institutional competency of judiciary
    *   As for whether it was irrational that the SLA did not publicise the alternative policy, held that at common law **no legal duty** on the part of the Government **to publicize** the policies which it seeks to implement...no general rule that reasons must be given.
*   \[**ASSISTING OPERATIONS**\] In **_Pannir Selvam_**, although the applicant alleged that the Public Prosecutor acted irrationally in refusing a Certificate of Substantive Assistance, the Court found that the decision was rational. The information Pannir provided had been **materially incorrect**, leading to no useful disruption of drug trafficking, and the decision not to issue a certificate was rationally based on operational judgment.

**Irrationality found**

*   \[**EN BLOC SALE**\] In **_Mir Hasan_**, the STB approval for the _en bloc_ sale of Tampines Court. **STB had a calendar of their own making** which fixed the hearing for application for approval of sale long after date of completion designated in S&P agreement, **which would have sabotaged the sale**. Issue was whether action of STB moving the date so far into the future was rational
    *   **Held**: STB under LTSA supposed to facilitate _en bloc_ sales expeditiously, not stymie them. Based on the decision, any order made at the hearing would be futile as it would be after contractual deadline, and was held to be **unreasonable** in the _Wednesbury_ sense.
*   \[**IRRELAVANT CONSIDERATIONS?**\] While taking into account irrelevant considerations or failing to consider relevant ones ordinarily grounds a claim for judicial review under the head of illegality (**_Padfield; Nagaenthran; Tan Seet Eng_**), it is arguable that a such a distortion in the decision-making process may also lead the court to find irrationality.
    *   In **_CBB v Law Society_**, the SGHC held that the Council’s decision irrational because it **relied on an irrelevant factor** (the complaint’s personal capacity, although disciplinary proceedings exist to uphold the profession’s standards, not personal rights) and failed to consider relevant factors like the seriousness and merits of the complaint, which, following the SGCA decision in **_Chan Chun Hwee_**, are crucial especially where serious or prolonged misconduct may justify curing procedural irregularities.

### Analysis: Proportionality

Although the doctrine of proportionality is **currently not an accepted ground** for judicial review in Singapore (subsumed under irrationality in **_Chung Suan Tze_** dicta, **_Vijaya Kumar_**), in the unlikely event that the court departs from the current stance, the decision may be invalidated for being excessive in relation to its legitimate purpose in making decisions.

**_However_**, it is argued that proportionality is **structurally different from irrationality** — it requires decsino to have (1) a legitimate aim; (2) rationally suitability; (3) no more than necessary; (4) balance between the measure and the aim. In this sense, this is a more searching, structured review that examines the substantive merits — precisely what Wednesbury irrationality defers to the decision-maker on.

### Substantial Legal Expectations

The applicant may argue that the **public authority had made clear and unambiguous representations** giving rise to a substantive legitimate expectation (“**SLE**”) that \[X\].

Although the doctrine of SLE was first recognised in **_Chiu Teng_** as a “separate and standalone” ground of judicial review, its status in Singapore law remains uncertain following the SGCA decision in **_Tan Seng Kee_** which stated that there is only a “limited recognition of the doctrine” in exceptional case like **_s.377A_**.

Applying Tan Seng Kee’s limited framework, it must be considered whether the present case similarly involves (a) a **grave risk of deprivation of fundamental rights** if the representation is not honoured, and/or (b) an **overriding public interest** in preserving a deliberate legislative or governmental compromise. If such exceptional circumstances exist, the court may be more prepared to recognise a substantive legitimate expectation in this case. On the facts, the court is \[likely/unlikely\] extend the doctrine of SLE to the current case.

The requirements to be proved for SLE in **_Chiu Teng_**:

1.  The applicant must prove that the statement or representation made by the public authority was **unequivocal and unqualified;**
    1.  If the statement or representation is open to more than one natural interpretation, the interpretation applied by the public authority will be adopted; and
    2.  The **presence of a disclaimer or non-reliance clause** would cause the statement or representation to be qualified
2.  The applicant must prove the statement or representation was **made by someone with _actual or ostensible authority_** to do so on behalf of the public authority.
3.  The applicant must prove that the statement or **representation was made to him or to a class of persons** to which he clearly belongs.
4.  The applicant must prove that it was **reasonable for him to rely on the statement** or representation in the circumstances of his case:
    1.  If the applicant knew that the statement or representation was made in error and **chose to capitalise on the error**, he will not be entitled to any relief;
    2.  Similarly, if he suspected that the statement or representation was made in error and **chose to not seek clarification** when he could have done so, he will not be entitled to any relief;
    3.  If there is a **reason and opportunity to make enquiries** and the applicant did not, he will not be entitled to any relief
5.  The applicant must prove that he did rely on the statement or representation, and that he **suffered a detriment as a result**
6.  Even if all the above requirements aare met, the court should nevertheless not grant relief if:
    1.  Giving effect to the statement or representation will result in a breach of the law or or the state’s international obligations;
    2.  Giving effect to the statement or representation will infringe the accused rights of some member of the public;
    3.  The public authority can show an overriding national or public interest which justifies the frustration of the applicant’s expectation.

*   As applied in **_SGB Starkstrom_**, the issue was whether SGB had a SLE against Commissioner that a compensation claim (which was previously approved by the Commissioner under WICA) was valid and that it discharged SGB’s liabilities to the injured employee?
    *   Court held that **SLE not applicable on the facts** of the case:
        *   The doctrine was **not meant** to regulate disputes **between private individuals**
        *   Additionally, no such representations were made by the Commissioner
        *   No SLE from void / _ultra vires_ decisions
    *   Court did not affirm nor overrule _Chiu Teng_ on SLE point, and accepting the doctrine was a significant departure on the scope and limits of judicial review.

## Procedural Review Grounds

To **ensure a fair trial**, the current case may be subject to judicial supervision through procedural review.

*   If a statute prescribes a procedure, the authority must follow it or act ultra vires; if not, courts may imply procedural fairness through natural justice, which is the rule against bias and the right to a fair hearing (**Tan & Thio**).

### Fair Hearing Rule / Duty to Act Fairly

The Fair Hearing Rule requires that an individual who may be adversely affected by a decision must be given notice of the case against them and a fair opportunity to be heard (**_Kay Swee Pin_**).

**_However_**, before the court intervene for breach of the Fair Hearing Rule, it must be first considered what type of decision is in question. There generally three types of decisions: (1) forfeiture cases and (2) application cases; and (3) expectation cases (**_McInnes_**). On the facts, the decision falls within a \[forfeiture/application/expectation\] case because…

| Forfeiture casesHighest procedural protections: an unbiased tribunal, notice of charges, and a right to be heard in answer to charges | A decision that takes away an existing right or position (e.g., expulsion of a member or revocation of a licence). |
| --- | --- |
| Expectation casesIntermediate procedural protection. | Where the applicant has a legitimate expectation (basic; not to level of SLE) that their application will be granted due to prior representations or practice by authority |
| Application casesMinimal protections apply; no right to oral hearing or reasons. | A decision that merely refuses to grant something the applicant seeks (e.g., first-time membership or a new licence). |

**TIP**: Look at fact patterns to determine what happened in the hypothetical to draw parallels to find where there were breaches of rule

**Significance of “livelihood” and Socio-Economic Value**

*   In **_Kay Swee Pin v SICC_**, whilst the private club was not a public authority, court held that where the club expelled, suspended or punished a club member, where its membership held significant social and economic value, a more rigorous application of the rules of natural justice is required.
    *   Clauses to oust court jurisdiction is contrary to public policy and **void**.
*   In **_Woon Kwok Cheng_**, jockey disqualified for 5 years applied to MRA for reinstatement, but was **rejected application without reasons** and in his appeal, was told to justify before an MRA special committee. But he refused to do so, and was thus dismissed.
    *   Court held: MRA affects lives of **sizeable portion of the population**, and **monopolises the trade** which is significant to the public. Considering the plaintiff is a trained jockey where his **livelihood** is towards this nature of work, judicial review application was permitted.
    *   By rejecting the plaintiff without reasons attached would tantamount to a breach of procedural fairness and natural justice

**Right to Know the Case Against Applicant**

This requires that a party liable to be directly affected by a decision must know the allegations against him and be given a fair opportunity to meet them (**_Kay Swee Pin_**).

*   In **_Chiam See Tong v SDP_**, Chaim was invited merely to “explain”/ justify his statements at SDP’s disciplinary hearing. However, the **hearing turned confrontational** about his breach of party confidentiality obligations (not to talk to the press) and the hearing **comprised some of his opponents.** He sued for wrongful expulsion.
    *   The court found that such adversarial proceedings **deprived Chiam of a true opportunity to defend himself**, amounting to a breach of the right to fair hearing.
*   In **_Per Ah Seng v HDB_**, CA recognised that there was a general duty to disclose an adverse report to an affected party to enable a response in the context of an investigator determining if people were breaching regulations.
    *   **_But the rule is not absolute_**, must be weighed against potential prejudice to public interest, which in this case militated against the disclosure of the PI report – could result in investigative methods used by HDB’s PIs being made known to the public
    *   However, **non-disclosure of PI’s report did not breach** natural justice because, beyond policy consideration, determinative evidence, an inspection by the HDB officers, had been disclosed.

**Right to Fair Hearing**

*   In **_Tan Boon Chee David v Medical Council of SG_**, the court found that the hearing was in breach of the fair hearing rule
    *   **Not all members of medical council present during the hearing, without the hearing being adjourned** – some present 1st day, others 2nd day, and council members were walking in and out of the inquiry when witnesses gave evidence
    *   This was serious breach of the rule because “A person who has not seen and heard a witness is as a rule quite incapable of estimating the value of his evidence”.

**Right to Counsel**

While there is no general right to legal representation in administrative or disciplinary proceedings, the courts recognise that fairness **may require allowing legal representation** where the seriousness of the charge, the potential consequences, or the complexity of the issues so warrant (**_Kok Seng Chong_**) – **not trivial cases**.

*   In **_Kok Seng Chong_**, Racehorse trainer sought injunction to restrain a disciplinary inquiry into a positive post-race urine test, and sought right to legal representation at inquiry hearing, against rules of natural justice and / or public policy.
    *   Hence, plaintiff had a right to proper consideration of his request for legal representation on its merits
    *   **_But_ there were public policy reasons for NOT allowing legal counsel**
        *   Costs, protracting the issue
        *   **_But_** note where having legal counsel would affect one’s right to a fair hearing in **_Haron Mundir_** whose **limited education and grasp of English** resulted in need for legal counsel.

### Rule against Bias

As a subset of natural justice, decision-makers must not only act impartially but must also appear to act impartially so that public confidence in the integrity of decision-making is maintained (**_BOI v BOJ_**). This is the rule against **Actual** and **Apparent** bias.

**Actual Bias (V. High Threshold)**

Actual bias arises where the decision-maker is **subjectively prejudiced** against a party, or, in another word, has **pre-judged the case**. The threshold for proving actual bias is extremely high because it requires evidence of a decision-maker’s subjective state of mind. Nonetheless, where such direct evidence is available, the decision must be set aside without the need for further inquiry into appearance or perception.

*   In **_Sim Yong Teng_**, the SGCA found direct evidence of actual bias where members of the new management committee had **expressed in writing their support for a prior decision** to suspend the applicant, **before even hearing the matter afresh**. The court held that such pre-judgement of the issues, made prior to hearing evidence or arguments, constituted actual bias.

**Apparent Bias**

Absence actual bias, a decision may still be set aside for apparent bias if **a reasonable and fair-minded observer, knowing all the relevant facts**, would have a reasonable suspicion that the decision-maker might not have acted impartially (**_Jeyaretnam; BOI v BOJ_**). – Reasonable Suspicion test.

*   In **_Pek Lian Guan_** \[2026\] SGHC, CJ Menon held **extensive judicial copying** is not automatically unlawful, but it raises concerns about natural justice because it may suggest the judge failed to apply an independent mind to the materials or that the losing party was denied a fair hearing – which can undermine public confidence on the judiciary.
    *   Fundamental biases per **_Swire Shipping_** includes the judicial officer copying one party’s submissions **without dealing with the other party’s key arguments**, evidence of prejudgment, _rather than_ mere mistakes in reasoning that can be corrected on appeal.
*   In **_BOI v BOJ_**, applicant alleged that the judge’s interventions during cross-examination showed apparent bias.
    *   **Court held**: adversarial and not inquisitorial, thus judge should not descend into the arena. Whether or not judge truly 'descends into the arena' **depends on the quantity (no. of times) and qualitative impact of the Qs** – case by case assessment
    *   Reasonable observer:
        *   **Informed**: Apprised of all relevant publicly known facts;
        *   **Fair-minded**: Neither complacent nor unduly suspicious; would not jump to conclusions of bias based on isolated remarks taken out of context.
    *   Excessive judicial interference (not apparent bias):
        *   Judge slipped into the perils of self-persuasion … depends on whether the reviewing court deemed how the decision-maker acted was such as to impair its ability to evaluate and weigh the case presented by each side
*   In **_JBJ v LKY_**, applicant alleged apparent bias because the trial judge had previously worked at the same law firm as the plaintiff (LKY) and had delivered judgments against the applicant in earlier cases
    *   **Held**: SGCA rejected the claim, holding that **mere past professional association or adverse findings were insufficient to establish apparent bias** and a reasonable observer must not jump to conclusions based on background alone, but must consider whether there are objective facts raiing in a real concern about impartiality
*   In **_Ex Parte Pinochet_**, the UKHL held that the involvement of Lord Hoffmann, who was associated with an intervening party **(as unpaid director of charity arm)**, gave rise to apparent bias even though there was no financial interest_._
    *   Charity arm had **sufficiently close connection** to the legal proceeding because it directly concerns their core mission of advancing human rights accountability.

### Rule of Necessity (Defence for decision-maker)

The decision-maker, however, may invoke the rule of necessity to proceed despite the risk of bias, because no alternative competent tribunal or forum exists (**_Sim Yong Teng; Lawrence Khong_**). Necessity rule is **limited**: it does not apply to cases of actual bias, and it **does not apply to non-statutory bodies**.

The principle operates in two scenarios:

1.  where no other person or tribunal is available to decide the matter; or
2.  where a quorum cannot be formed without the participation of members who would otherwise be disqualified for bias.

**However**, even where the necessity _prima facie_ applies, the decision-maker must take all reasonable steps to minimise doubts about impartiality (**_Lawrence Khong_**).

*   In **_Lawrence Khong_**, the SGHC held that where alternative options existed, such as rescheduling the disciplinary meeting, co-opting neutral members or delegating disciplinary powers to sub-committee, the principle of necessity **did not apply**.
    *   The tribunal’s failure to exhaust these alternatives meant the decision was tainted by apparent bias and breached natural justice.
*   In **_Sim Yong Teng_**, the SGCA held that the principle of necessity **does not generally apply to private associations such as social clubs**.
    *   However in this case, members of the new management committee who had signed letters prejudging the matter proceeded to rehear the complaint. The Court found that they could have resigned and allowed untainted members to be elected, and thus necessity was not genuinely engaged.

## Executive Rule Making

The current case may be subject to judicial review **if the executive body acted unlawfully in exercising its rule-making functions**.

### Formal Rulemaking (through subsidiary legislation)

*   For subsidiary legislation to be validly made, they must:
    *   Under **_s23 of the Interpretation Act_**, subsidiary legislation must be **published in the Gazette** to have legal effect unless otherwise expressly provided.
    *   Not contain differentiating measures that have the practical effect of disadvantaging persons or groups of people along racial or religious lines
    *   Presented to the Clerk of Parliament
*   Thus, in **_Cheong Seok Leng_**, appellant charged with AWOL under Vigilante Corps Act argued that he was **not AWOL as he did not know the law**, since it was **not published.**
    *   SGHC held that the assignment of additional duties to the Vigilante Corps constituted subsidiary legislation because it determined the content of the Act as a rule of conduct.
    *   Although the assignment was within the Minister’s power, it was **invalid** because it had not been published in the Gazette, in breach of s23 of the Interpretation Act.
        *   The Court emphasised that **publication is a condition precedent to the validity of subsidiary legislation**, reflecting the need to ensure transparency and public access to the law.

### Informal rulemaking (through administrative policies)

Administrative bodies exercising statutory discretion are entitled to adopt informal policies or guidelines to structure their decision-making, but **such policies must comply with established legal principles** to avoid unlawful fettering discretion or acting ultra vires.

**Not Wednesbury unreasonable**

The policy **must not be irrational or so absurd** that no reasonable authority could have adopted it. Courts will not interfere merely because they would have preferred a different policy.

*   In **_Lines International Holding_** the SGHC upheld the PSA’s policy limiting berthing rights for cruise-to-nowhere ships to 30%, finding that it served legitimate port development objectives under the PSA Act and was not irrational. The court emphasised that even policies that restrict commercial activities are lawful if **connected rationally to the statutory purpose**.
*   Similarly, in **_City Developments_**, the SGCA upheld a policy discouraging land hoarding as a rational and legitimate consideration when assessing property tax values, even though it significantly increased the applicant’s tax liability.

**Policy was made known to affected persons**

Fairness requires that policies which materially affect rights or opportunities **must be communicated** to those affected.

*   In **_Lines International_**, the PSA and other bodies met with cruise operators and orally conveyed the 30% policy guidelines. The Court held that a written formulation is unnecessary if the policy is adequately conveyed and understood, giving those affected an opportunity to clarify or comment.

**The authority must not fetter its discretion**

Authorities must retain the **ability to consider exceptions and exercise discretion** on a case-by-case basis.

*   In **_Lines International_**, PSA’s **independent exercise of discretion in refusing berthing rights** to the applicant was upheld on the facts
    *   _Nevertheless_, one of PSA’s guidelines — automatically denying berthing rights if certain agencies objected — was struck down for fettering discretion. The Court found that PSA had improperly bound itself to external bodies' decisions without exercising independent judgment.
*   In **_Komoco Motors_**, the SGCA held that although the Registrar routinely adopted Customs' open market values (OMVs) to calculate registration fees, she did not fetter discretion because she **remained willing to consider objections** on a case-by-case basis.

## Remedies

While the default remedy in judicial review is a quashing order (**_Justices of Kingston_**), meaning that the decision made by the decision-maker would be nullified and the decision-maker would have to reconsider his decision, the court may in **exceptional circumstances grant a mandatory order** in specific terms, effectively compelling a public authority to act in a particular way.

*   In **_CBB v Law Society_**, the SGCA issued a mandatory order requiring the Law Society to apply for leave under the **_Legal Profession Act_**, holding that this was one of the rare cases where **only one lawful outcome** was available.
*   **Facts**:
    *   Complaint against lawyer Mr L (trust arrangements, Re BKR) >6 years old, requiring Council's court leave under **_LPA s 85A(4C)_**
    *   High Court quashed Council's initial refusal to seek leave as irrational (non-client status irrelevant; public interest ignored)
    *   Council took 11 months inaction after quashing order despite direction to reconsider

**Guiding factors to determine whether such a specific mandatory order is justified (_CBB_)**:

*   **Availability of objective evidence**: Is there public domain evidence bearing on the merits? (Here: Mr L's conduct was detailed in _Re BKR_ and other judgments.)
*   **Institutional competence of the court**: Does the decision involve polycentric policy matters outside judicial expertise? (Here: lawyer discipline is _within_ the court's aegis and supervision.)
*   **Decision-maker's conduct**: Has the decision-maker shown an intention not to reconsider, or been unduly dilatory? (Here: 11 months of inaction, counsel frankly stated they did not want to be seen "caving in.")
*   **Absence of other reasons militating against**: No need for constant supervision, no impossibility, no public inconvenience, etc.
*   **Absence of alternative ways of performing the duty**: By statutory construction, was there only one way the discretion could lawfully be exercised? (Here: proper construction of LPA s 85A(4C) and relevant factors — length of delay, prejudice, reasons, prospects — all pointed one way.)

**Why the test is stricter for Mandatory Orders:** Because Quashing Orders and Declarations leave the decision-making authority with the designated executive body. A Mandatory Order in _specific terms_ effectively usurps that authority and renders the court the primary decision-maker. This is only acceptable in the _rarest_ of cases where every relevant consideration has been assessed and there is only one legally reasonable outcome.

### Locus standi

To invoke the court's powers to review a case, the applicant must have locus standi or the right

of appearance before the court (**_TT4_**).

As laid down in **_Kenneth Jeyaretnam v AG_**, the law on _locus standi_ is:

1.  public duty breached; and 🡪 _prima facie_ made out if there is a violation of constitutional rights (**_Tan Eng Hong SGCA_** \[at 84\])
2.  which generates correlative private rights (**_Tan Eng Hong_**) or public rights (**_Vellama_** – narrow avenue in rate and exceptional situations requiring “special damage”).

\[Membership = Violation\] In **_Eng Foong Ho,_** held that applicants were granted standing because of their membership of the Buddhist association which was affected by the compulsory acquisition of temple property.

**In cases where non-correlative rights** generating public duty is breached, and the breach is of sufficient gravity such that it **would be in the public interest** for the courts to hear the case, an applicant _sans_ rights may be accorded _locus standi_ as well, at the discretion of the courts

# Year by Year Paper Analysis

**\[PAST PAPER ISSUES — 2021 (Wildlife Protection Act, Uncle Wozzer)\]:** (1) Whether the MWP Policy on the website created an informal rule or legitimate expectation that Wozzer relied on (Chiu Teng conditions); (2) Whether the rejection of Wozzer's application was ultra vires for failing to satisfy precedent facts — vaccination requirement applied to 'persons handling wildlife' but his front-desk clerk was NOT involved in handling animals (error of precedent fact/patent error); (3) Whether the decision was biased — Minister detests all reptiles after Komodo dragon bit him (apparent bias; BOI v BOJ reasonable suspicion test); (4) Whether the inquiry before Noi was procedurally fair — Noi was belligerent, continually demanded more evidence, did not allow committee members to question Wozzer, and refused to give reasons for aggravating factors (fair hearing rule; Re Shankar on descending into the arena); (5) Whether Noi was apparently biased — Wozzer had made multiple police reports against Noi (reasonable suspicion test; prior personal conflicts); (6) Whether the S$50,000 fine was within the Ministry's power — the Act does not grant the Minister a power to fine when making an Art 3 declaration (patent error of law); (7) Whether the direction to cease clinic operations was within the Minister's power (ultra vires); (8) Whether the Minister had failed to apply his mind to the matter when immediately replying to Wozzer's email within the hour (fetter of discretion/failure to consider new evidence).

**\[PAST PAPER ISSUES — 2022 (COVID-19 Pandemic Management Act, Norberto)\]:** Good scripts identified at least 9-10 factual patterns. Key issues: (1) Patent error in the decision to fine Norberto S$20,000 — the MOH website policy said 'fine not exceeding S$10,000' for first offence (patent error); (2) Ms Federdal's assurance that Norberto could travel to Singapore for on-arrival exemption — raised substantive legitimate expectation under Chiu Teng conditions; (3) Fetter of discretion — Ms Federdal refused to hear out Norberto and refused to read his supporting documents despite him having new information and evidence; (4) Apparent bias — Ms Federdal had announced at launch of national vaccination drive that those who reject vaccinations are 'irresponsible' and posts against vaccine-sceptics under 'Vaccine Vigilante' avatar (BOI v BOJ reasonable suspicion test); (5) Irrationality — banning Norberto for 5 years when the Act only permits 3-year ban; (6) Whether the Minister's decision that Norberto's 'public opposition to vaccination threatens national security' as advised by MHA was based on an irrelevant consideration (Padfield).

**\[PAST PAPER ISSUES — 2023 (Singapore Broadcasting Act, Gerry Lee)\]:** Good scripts identified at least 14 potential issues. Key issues: (1) AMENABILITY — whether SBA's decision was amenable under the Nature Test (SBA influential self-regulatory body that liaises with government; Datafin; Lawrence Khong); (2) Whether the Health Minister calling the SBA Chairman and saying Gerry 'should be disciplined' was an improper direction under the Singapore Broadcasting Act amounting to unlawful interference with the SBA's independent disciplinary function (cf Komoco — decision-maker cannot take instructions from another body); (3) Fair hearing rule breaches at the SBA meeting — quizzed for four hours about irrelevant topics (his talkshow content, global tennis events) rather than the disciplinary matter; four other SBA Board Members looked bored and tapped on phones; three left at noon and told the Chairman to decide for them (audi alteram partem; Chiam See Tong — person must be told of his case clearly); (4) Apparent bias — Board Members had 'a tennis club buddy ready to replace him' — pre-decided outcome (Sim Tong Yeng — actual prejudgment); (5) MDBC rejection: Requirement (3) of having a diploma in audio equipment repair — was this a relevant consideration for someone who has been a radio presenter for 10 years? (Padfield — irrelevant consideration); (6) Whether the Minister's rejection based on the 'Minister of Health's advice' that Gerry's views 'are contrary to Singapore's national interest' was an improper purpose under the Broadcasting Act (WDM v Secretary of State approach); (7) Irrationality of the diploma in audio equipment repair requirement as applied to experienced radio presenters.

**\[PAST PAPER ISSUES — 2024 (Global Corporations Practices Act, Oz Warren/Grabbu/SPCC)\]:** Two decisions requiring separate analysis: (1) SPCC DECISION — amenability (nature test/Datafin — SPCC works closely with MTI, effectively the only way for Prekka companies to clinch business deals in Singapore; acquiescence of MTI); procedural impropriety: Panel included two of Grabbu's KEY COMPETITORS — apparent bias (Lawrence Khong; BOI v BOJ); Oz Warren's explanation was continuously interrupted by the Panel — excessive interference; Oz Warren grilled on political affiliations (Chiam See Tong — must be told of case clearly); factual/legal errors: Oz Warren was WRONGLY assumed to still be a Prekka legislator (he was a FORMER member); the offer of free Sailor Smith tickets was WRONGLY alleged to constitute 'Corruption' under s 3 (SPCC members are not 'Singaporean public officers' per the Act's definition — patent error of law). (2) MINISTER'S DECISION — jurisdictional error of precedent fact: power to suspend was conditional on a 'foreign public officer' being a shareholder — Oz Warren was no longer a current member so did not fall within that definition; Minister had power to SUSPEND for one year, not ORDER TERMINATION (excess of authority); Minister abrogated his discretion by deferring entirely and unquestioningly to the SPCC's report (cf Komoco); finality clause s 6 (Nagaenthran approach).

**\[PAST PAPER ISSUES — 2025 (Corporate Finances Control Act, Gamestonk)\]:** THREE decisions requiring separate analysis: (1) MINISTER'S DECLARATION of Gamestonk as restricted entity + fine: Was Minister's departure from MTI Circular a breach of legitimate expectation (Chiu Teng cf Tan Seng Kee — probably NOT, since promise was only by MTI, not Parliament, and life/liberty not at stake); improper purpose — declaring board game industry a 'restricted entity' to prevent 'political instability' (WDM — no proper purpose under the CFCA); Minister FINED Gamestonk S$50,000 — simply NO POWER to do this under the CFCA 2015 (patent error of law); Tan Seng Kee-type extraordinary circumstances for binding the Minister to the MTI Circular probably NOT met (promise only by MTI, not Parliament; life and liberty not at stake). (2) ACRA'S REJECTION of Gamestonk's appeal: amenability (probably a forfeiture case — Minister's declaration limits Gamestonk's legal rights to make payments); Bob Ong's 'persistent questioning' arguably amounts to excessive intervention (cf Chiam See Tong; BOI v BOJ); ACRA apparently biased — Bob Ong probably being the politician embarrassed by Gamestonk's North Coast Plan game (BOI v BOJ reasonable suspicion test; Lawrence Khong); principle of NECESSITY — could not apply because it was not necessary for Bob Ong to be on the panel (Sim Yong Teng). (3) MTI PERM SEC'S REFUSAL of two payments: First payment (S$150,000 to Singapore seller) — ONLY ONE CORRECT DECISION: authorise, since s 5 says Minister 'may only refuse' authorisation to counterparties outside Singapore and the seller was INSIDE Singapore (precedent fact review; Re Fong Thin Choo; mandatory order under CBB v Law Society); Second payment (S$99,000 to Ruritanian firm) — whether this had any legal effect at all, since it was BELOW S$100,000 (cf s 4 — only requires authorisation for payments EXCEEDING S$100,000); whether MTI Perm Sec had FETTERED DISCRETION in consulting Bob Ong — his decisions were 'based on the advice' of Ong (cf Komoco CA — must apply mind independently; abrogation of discretion); finality clause in s 7 (Nagaenthran approach).
